The Meanings and Linguistic Derivation of the Divine Name 'Rabb'
List of meanings of 'Rabb' and discussion of its derivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple meanings of the divine name 'Rabb' as summarized in the poem and the correct linguistic derivation according to the commentator.

Core Idea
The name 'Rabb' encompasses creation, ownership, and nurturing, and is properly an active participle, not a verbal noun.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the meaning of 'Rabb' as it appears in the text of Jawharat al-Tawhid. He first provides a poetic summary of its various meanings, then addresses a common linguistic misconception about its derivation, arguing for the view that it is an active participle rather than a verbal noun.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice that the passage includes a poem and a linguistic discussion.
2
Identify the poetic meanings of 'Rabb'
Find the poem by al-Sijai and list the meanings he attributes to 'Rabb', such as Creator, Owner, Manager, etc.
The poem is a mnemonic for the multiple meanings.
3
Understand the derivation issue
Consider why the commentator rejects the view that 'Rabb' is a verbal noun (masdar) and instead argues it is an active participle (ism fa'il).
The commentator finds the hyperbolic application ugly and prefers the linguistic form 'rabib'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (رَبِّهِ) أي : خالقه أو مالكه أو نحو ذلك من معاني الرب المنظومة في قول الشيخ السجاعي : [من الطويل] قَرِيبٌ مُحِيطٌ مَالِكٌ وَمُدَبِّرٌ مُرَبِّ كَثِيرُ الخَيْرِ وَالمُولِ لِلنَّعَمُ وَخَالِقُنَا المَعْبُودُ جَابِرُ كَسْرِنَا وَمُصْلِحُنَا وَالصَّاحِبُ الثَّابِتُ القِدَمُ وَجَامِعُنَا وَالسَّيِّدُ احْفَظْ فَهَذِهِ مَعَانٍ أَتَتْ لِلرَّبِّ فَادْعُ لِمَنْ نَظَمُ ووقع في عبارة كثير من العلماء أنه مصدر بمعنى التربية؛ وهي تبليغ الشيء شيئاً فشيئاً إلى الحد الذي أراده المربي، أطلق عليه تعالى مبالغة؛ أي: بدعوى أنه تعالى هو عين التربية، ولا يخفى ما فيه من البشاعة؛ فالأولى أنه اسم فاعل، فأصله (رابب)، ثم خُفِّفَ بحذف الألف وإدغام أحد المثلين في
His saying: (Rabbihi) i.e., His Creator, or His Owner, or similar from the meanings of Rabb that are versified in the saying of al-Sijai: [from al-Tawil] 'Near, Encompassing, Owner, and Manager, Nurturer, Abundant in goodness, the Giver of blessings, and our Creator, the Worshipped, the Mender of our brokenness, and our Reformer, and the Companion, the Eternal, and the Assembler of us, and the Master. Memorize, for these are the meanings that have come for Rabb, so pray for the one who composed them.' And it occurs in the statements of many scholars that it is a verbal noun (masdar) meaning tarbiya (nurturing), which is the bringing of a thing gradually to the limit intended by the nurturer. It is applied to the Exalted hyperbolically, i.e., claiming that He Himself is nurturing. The ugliness in that is not hidden. So it is more appropriate that it is an active participle (ism fa'il); its original is 'rabib', then it was lightened by dropping the alif and assimilating one of the two similar letters into
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a definition and linguistic analysis. The commentator first explains the meaning of 'Rabb' in the line 'Rabbihi' as Creator, Owner, etc., and presents a poetic summary by al-Sijai enumerating the meanings. Then he discusses the derivation: some scholars take it as a masdar (verbal noun) meaning 'tarbiya' (nurturing) applied to Allah hyperbolically. The commentator criticizes this as ugly and prefers that it is an ism fa'il (active participle) from the root r-b-b, originally 'rabib' lightened by dropping the alif and assimilating. The passage ends abruptly at 'fi' (into), likely continuing on the next page.

Key Terms
الرب
التربية
المصدر
اسم الفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'Rabb' is derived from tarbiya as a verbal noun and applied to Allah hyperbolically.
Correction: The commentator argues that this view is linguistically ugly and inappropriate; instead, 'Rabb' is an active participle from 'rabib', meaning 'One who nurtures'.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the preferred linguistic derivation of the word 'ربّ' (Rabb)?
A) It is a verbal noun (masdar) meaning tarbiya.
B) It is an active participle (ism fa'il) from 'rabib'.
C) It is a proper name without derivation.
D) It is a hyperbole (mubalagha).

Key Takeaway
The name 'Rabb' carries multiple meanings including creation, ownership, and nurturing, and is linguistically derived as an active participle, not a verbal noun.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call Allah 'Rabb', do you truly feel in your heart that He is your Creator, Owner, and Nurturer in every moment? Or does the word remain abstract? What would change in your daily actions if you lived with this full awareness?
?
The lecturer warns against attributing a process of nurturing to Allah. Have you ever thought of Allah's attributes as if they are in time? How does that diminish your reverence for Him? What step can you take today to purify your concept of His lordship?
?
The poem lists many attributes of Rabb: Near, Encompassing, Manager, Giver of blessings, Mender, Reformer, etc. Which of these do you most need to internalize right now? Commit to repeating one of these attributes in your supplication tonight.

Divine Transcendence

4 min
The First Two Beliefs: Tanzih and Rejection of Tashbih and Ta'til
States the first two beliefs: God's transcendence and essence not similar nor devoid of attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two foundational beliefs of Islamic theology regarding God's transcendence and His essence being neither similar to creation nor stripped of attributes.

Core Idea
God is unlike anything we imagine, and His essence has true attributes unlike creation's.

Sanusi begins the book by stating the first two beliefs that every Muslim must hold about Allah. The first establishes absolute transcendence: whatever the mind conceives, Allah is different. The second corrects two common errors: likening God to creation (tashbih) and denying His attributes (ta'til). These two beliefs form the bedrock of Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the straightforward meaning.
The passage is concise; every word carries weight.
2
Identify the two beliefs
Note that the passage explicitly presents two beliefs: the first about imagination and transcendence, the second about essence and attributes.
These are the first two items in a longer list of beliefs.
3
Understand the first belief (tanzih)
Reflect on the meaning: anything that can be imagined, God is contrary to it. This establishes God's absolute otherness.
This guards against anthropomorphism.
4
Understand the second belief (rejecting tashbih and ta'til)
The second belief affirms that God's essence is not like other essences (against tashbih) and that it is not devoid of attributes (against ta'til).
This is the balanced Sunni position.
5
Connect to the bigger picture
See how these two beliefs together protect both transcendence and the reality of God's attributes, forming the foundation of tawhid.
This is the starting point for all subsequent discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى: اعتقاد أن كل ما تصور في الأوهام فالله بخلافه . والثانية : اعتقاد أن ذاتَهُ تعالى ليست مشبهةً للذوات، ولا معطّلة عن الصفات).
The first: the belief that everything imagined in imaginations, God is contrary to it. And the second: the belief that His essence, exalted is He, is not similar to essences, nor devoid of attributes.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents two foundational beliefs of Islamic theology (tawhid). The first is absolute transcendence (tanzih): whatever the human mind can conceive, God is different from it. The second guards against two extremes: tashbih (comparing God to creation) and ta'til (stripping God of attributes). It affirms that God's essence is neither like created essences nor devoid of the attributes that belong to it properly. This is the balanced approach of Ahl al-Sunna.

Key Terms
اعتقاد
الأوهام
بخلافه
ذات
مشبهة
معطلة
الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tanzih means God is absolutely unknowable, with no attributes or names.
Correction: Tanzih means God is unlike creation, but He still has true and eternal attributes known through revelation.

Checkpoint
What two extremes are avoided by the second belief?
A) Tashbih and tanzih
B) Ta'til and tashbih
C) Tanzih and ta'til
D) Shirk and kufr

Key Takeaway
The first two beliefs establish that Allah is utterly transcendent while possessing real attributes—a middle path between tashbih and ta'til.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah—even to feel close to Him—do you unconsciously form a picture in your mind? How does the first belief, that 'everything imagined is contrary to Him', challenge you to purify your conception of your Lord right now? What specific mental image do you need to surrender today?
?
You affirm that Allah has attributes like mercy and power, but do you secretly slip into either making those attributes too human (so He becomes relatable) or stripping them of reality (so He becomes abstract)? Which tendency is more tempting for your soul, and what one change in your daily dhikr can help you stay on the middle path described in the second belief?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's essence is utterly unlike any essence you know, how would that change the way you speak to Him in du'a tonight? Would you be more reverent, more desperate, or more certain? Make a commitment to one specific word you will add to your sujood based on this lesson.

Why God Is Beyond Time
The commentator defines time and explains why God cannot be subject to time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time cannot be attributed to God and how the definition of time as contingent establishes God's transcendence beyond time.

Core Idea
God is beyond time; time is contingent and cannot contain the Eternal.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid clarifies a subtle point about God's attribute of remaining. The author distinguishes our time-bound remaining from God's eternal subsistence. By defining time as either celestial motion or a comparison of renewals, the commentator shows that time is created and contingent, making it impossible for God, the Necessary Being, to be subject to time. Let us unpack this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: الزمان (time), العدم (non-existence), الحادث (contingent).
The passage uses a dense style common in kalam texts.
2
Distinguish divine remaining from human remaining
Notice that the author starts by guarding against attributing our kind of remaining to God. Our remaining is mixed with non-existence because it spans time. God's remaining is eternal and not time-bound.
This distinction is crucial for understanding divine attributes.
3
Understand the two definitions of time
The passage gives two definitions: time as the motion of the celestial sphere, and time as a comparison of an imaginary renewal (like your expected coming) to a known renewal (like sunrise). Both are contingent.
The second definition is illustrated by the example 'I will come to you at sunrise.'
4
Apply the logical conclusion
Since time is contingent, only contingent things can be associated with it. God is not contingent, so He cannot be in time. The passage concludes that God exists before everything, after everything, and with everything.
This refutes any notion that God is limited by time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحترز المصنف بذلك من بقائنا؛ فإنه يُشابُ بالعدم ويخلط به؛ لأنه مقارنة استمرار الوجود زمانين فصاعداً، وهذا مستحيل في حقه تعالى؛ لأن الزمان حركة الفلك، أو مقارنة متجدّد (۳) موهوم لمتجدّد معلوم إزالة للإبهام) ، كما في قولك : (آتيك طلوع الشمس)، فالزمانُ هو مقارنة الإتيان المتجدّد الموهوم لطلوع الشمس المتجدّدِ المعلوم، وكلٌّ من حركة الفلك والمقارنة المذكورة حادث، ولا يقترنُ بالحادث إلا من كان مثله، ومحل كونه مستحيلاً إذا كان على وجه الحصر؛ بأن يقال: وجوده ليس إلا في زمان، وإلا فهو تعالى موجود قبل كل شيء وبعده ومعه.
And the author guarded by that against our remaining; for it is mixed with non-existence and blended with it, because it is the continuation of existence across two times or more, and this is impossible with respect to Him, the Exalted; because time is the motion of the sphere, or an imaginary renewal compared to a known renewal to remove vagueness, as in your saying: 'I will come to you at sunrise.' So time is the comparison of the imaginary renewed coming to the known renewed sunrise. And each of the motion of the sphere and the mentioned comparison is contingent, and nothing is associated with the contingent except what is like it. And the place of its being impossible is if it is restricted, such that it is said: His existence is only in time. Otherwise, He, the Exalted, exists before everything, after everything, and with everything.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of why the attribute of 'remaining' (al-baqā') when applied to God is understood differently from our remaining. The commentator begins by stating that the author (al-Laqqānī) guarded against attributing to God the kind of remaining that applies to us, because our remaining is mixed with non-existence (al-‘adam) and blended with it. The reason given is that our remaining is the continuation of existence across two or more times, which is impossible for God. Time itself is defined in two ways: as the motion of the celestial sphere, or as an imaginary renewal compared to a known renewal, like saying 'I will come to you at sunrise.' Both definitions indicate that time is contingent (ḥādith). Since time is contingent, only contingent things can be associated with it. Therefore, attributing a time-bound existence to God is impossible unless one restricts God's existence to being only in time. But God exists before everything, after everything, and with everything, meaning He is not bounded by time. The passage thus establishes God's transcendence beyond time as a necessary attribute.

Key Terms
الزمان
العدم
الحادث
القِدَم
مخالفة الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that God's eternity means He is subject to time or that time is a real entity independent of created things.
Correction: The passage clarifies that time is contingent (either motion or comparison), and God, being necessary, cannot be bounded by time. God exists before, after, and with all things, not in time.

Checkpoint
The passage defines time in two ways. Which of the following is NOT one of the definitions mentioned?
A) The motion of the sphere
B) A comparison of an imaginary renewal to a known renewal
C) The continuation of existence across two times
D) Both A and B are mentioned

Key Takeaway
Time is contingent, and God, being necessary and eternal, cannot be bounded by time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You measure your life by minutes and years, but God is beyond all that. When you panic over a deadline or an aging body, do you truly believe that He exists before, after, and with every moment? How would you live differently right now if you acted on that reality?
?
The passage says our remaining is mixed with non-existence. Every second you are alive, you are also closer to death. Does that thought drive you to despair, or does it push you to seek the One who is never touched by non-existence? What is one attachment to this world that you need to let go of today so that your hope is in the Eternal alone?
?
You say you trust God, but do you panic when He is silent for a day, a month, a year? Since He is not bound by time, His timing is not yours. Are you willing to surrender your timeline to His timeless wisdom, even if it means waiting until the moment you can no longer wait?

The Definition of Mukhalafah (God's Dissimilarity to Creation)
Defines mukhalafah as negation of four categories and their concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of the divine attribute of mukhalafah as the negation of corporeality, accidentality, wholeness, and partiality from God.

Core Idea
Mukhalafah negates every similarity between God and creation: no body, accident, whole, or part.

In this passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed, the commentator defines the negative attribute of mukhalafah. He explains that it means God is absolutely unlike created beings in every respect. The definition is given by listing four categories that must be negated: corporeality, accidentality, wholeness, and partiality, along with their necessary implications. This establishes a foundational principle of Islamic theology: God is not subject to any limitations or composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's precise wording establish the meaning.
The passage is a direct definition from the commentary tradition.
2
Identify the four negated categories
Find and list the four terms: jirmiyyah, aradiyyah, kulliyyah, juz'iyyah. Notice that each is a type of attribute that created beings have.
3
Understand the concomitants
For each category, the commentator gives a necessary concomitant (lazim). Understand why these follow logically from the category.
Corporeality → occupying space; accidentality → inherence in something; totality → bigness; partiality → smallness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَأَنَّهُ لِمَا يَنَالُ العَدَمُ، مُخَالفٌ ... إلخ)؛ أي: وواجب له أنه تعالى مخالف للحوادث التي يلحقها العدم، فهو بفتح الهمزة من (أنَّ) واسمها الضمير العائد عليه تعالي وخبرها (مخالف)، ويتعلق به الجار والمجرور قبله، وإنما قدمه لضرورة النظم، و(ما) واقعةً على الحوادث، وعائدها محذوف، و(أن) وما دخلت عليه في تأويل مصدر معطوف على الوجود، والتقديرُ (وواجب له تعالى مخالفته للحوادث التي يلحقها العدمُ)، وبذلك يندفع ما في حاشية الشيخ العدوي من أن في كلام المصنف تسمحاً ؛ لأن الصفة مخالفته، لا أنَّه ،مخالف، ووجه اندفاع ذلك أن القاعدة سبْكُ (أن) المفتوحة بمصدرٍ من خبرها، وهو شائع في العربية، فلا يُقال: فيه تسمح، وجَعْلُنا لذلك معطوفاً على الوجود أولى من جعله خبراً لمبتدأ محذوف، والتقدير (والصفة الثالثة من الصفات السلبية أنه ... إلى آخره وكلام الشيخ عبد السلام في هذا المقام حلُّ معنى لا حلُّ إعراب، وإن أوهمت عبارته خلاف ذلك . وإنما أسند المخالفة له تعالى لأنها تنزيه والموصوف به الله لا الحوادث، وكما أنه تعالى مخالف للحوادث مخالف للأعدام الأزليَّة كما عُلمَ من وصفه بالوجود ؛ إذ هي ليست موجودةً ؛ وقد ذكر الشيخ عبد السلام في هذا المقام أن الأعدام الأزلية من الحوادث، وهو سهر ؛ لأن الأعدام الأزلية واجبة كما تقدَّم ، وقد ذكرها والده مثالاً للعدم السابق، ولم يجعلها من الحوادث.
والمخالفة لما ذُكر: عبارةٌ عن سلبِ الجرمية والعرضية والكلية والجزئية ولوازمها عنه تعالى فلازم الجرمية التحيز ، ولازم العرضية القيام بالغير، ولازم الكلية الكبر، ولازم الجزئية الصغرُ إلى غير ذلك .
This is his saying: (And that He is unlike what attains annihilation) i.e., it is necessary for Him, exalted, that He is unlike the contingent things that are overtaken by annihilation. So (anna) is with fatha on the hamza, and its pronoun (hu) refers back to Him exalted, and its predicate is (mukhaalif). The genitive phrase before it is attached to it, and he only advanced it due to poetic necessity. (Maa) refers to contingent things, and its relative pronoun is omitted. (An) and what enters upon it are in the interpretation of a verbal noun coordinated with (al-wujud). The estimation is: (it is necessary for Him exalted that He be unlike the contingent things that are overtaken by annihilation). By this is repelled what is in the marginal gloss of al-Adawi that there is looseness in the author's expression, because the attribute is His dissimilarity (mukhaalafah), not that He is unlike (mukhaalif). The face of repelling that is the rule of casting (an) with fatha into a verbal noun from its predicate, which is common in Arabic, so it is not said there is looseness. Our making that coordinated with (al-wujud) is more appropriate than making it a predicate of an omitted subject, the estimation being (the third of the negative attributes is that He is... etc.). And the speech of Sheikh Abd al-Salam in this context is solving the meaning, not solving grammatical parsing, even if his expression suggests otherwise. He only ascribes the dissimilarity (mukhaalafah) to Him exalted because it is transcendence, and the one described by it is God, not the contingent things. And just as He exalted is unlike contingent things, He is unlike eternal non-existents as is known from His attribute of existence, since they are not existent. And Sheikh Abd al-Salam mentioned in this context that eternal non-existents are from contingent things, but that is erroneous, because eternal non-existents are necessary (for possibles) as has preceded, and his father mentioned them as an example of prior non-existence, and did not make them from contingent things. And the dissimilarity (mukhaalafah) with respect to what was mentioned is: an expression of negating corporeality, accidentality, totality, partiality, and their concomitants from Him, exalted. So the concomitant of corporeality is occupying space, the concomitant of accidentality is inherence in another, the concomitant of totality is bigness, the concomitant of partiality is smallness, etc.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the negative attribute of mukhalafah (God's dissimilarity to created things). The author begins by analyzing the matn line 'And that He is unlike what attains annihilation' from the Jawharah. He explains the grammatical construction, showing that the phrase means it is necessary for God to be unlike contingent beings that are subject to annihilation. He then addresses a criticism from al-Adawi, who claimed the wording was imprecise, defending the author by noting the Arabic rule of deriving a verbal noun from the predicate of 'anna'. He further clarifies that the attribution of dissimilarity to God is an act of transcendence (tanzih), not a description of contingent things. The author also clarifies that God is unlike both contingent things and eternal non-existents, though the latter are not considered 'contingent things' in the technical sense, correcting a misunderstanding of Sheikh Abd al-Salam. The passage then provides the technical definition of mukhalafah: it is the negation from God of corporeality (jirmiyyah), accidentality (aradiyyah), totality (kulliyyah), partiality (juz'iyyah), and their necessary concomitants. Each category is defined: corporeality entails spatial occupation, accidentality entails inherence in a substrate, totality entails bigness, partiality entails smallness, and so on. This comprehensive negation establishes that God is absolutely unlike any created or possible being.

Key Terms
مخالفة
حوادث
جرمية
عرضية
كلية
جزئية
قدم
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that mukhalafah allows God to share some attributes with creation, like existence or power, but this definition shows that even categories like corporeality and accidentality are negated, implying total dissimilarity.
Correction: Mukhalafah negates all creaturely attributes, including any form of composition, spatial location, or inherence. God is absolutely unique.

Checkpoint
What does the attribute of mukhalafah specifically negate from God?
A) Existence
B) Corporeality, accidentality, totality, partiality, and their concomitants
C) Power and will
D) Knowledge

Key Takeaway
Mukhalafah is the absolute negation of all creaturely attributes from God, establishing His total transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's greatness, do you ever picture something 'big'? The passage negates totality and bigness. How can you train your heart to worship a God who is beyond all quantity and comparison, and what specific step will you take today to purify your mental image of Him?
?
Is there any subtle belief in your heart that God resembles something you know—a person, an object, or an abstract quality? This lesson says mukhalafah negates every category of similarity. Confess to yourself: what creaturely attribute do you still unconsciously attach to God, and how will you actively reject it in your du'a today?
?
The definition of mukhalafah lists four negations and their necessary consequences. Which of these four (corporeality, accidentality, totality, partiality) do you find hardest to fully internalize in your worship? What will you change in your daily remembrance to make God's absolute uniqueness more real to you?

Divine Transcendence

Responding to the Objection on God's Reality
Objection about God's reality and scriptural response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to respond to the objection that negating all categories from God leaves His reality unknown, by relying on divine transcendence and the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
God's reality is known only to God, and He is nothing like His creation.

A common question arises: if God is not a body, not an accident, not a whole, not a part, then what is He? This lesson addresses that very objection, which the text attributes to Satanic whispers. The response is drawn directly from the Quran, teaching us to affirm what Allah affirms of Himself while acknowledging His absolute transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage and identify the doubt: if God is none of the known categories, what is His reality?
This is a natural question that can lead to confusion.
2
Learn the Quranic response
The answer is given by quoting the Quran: only God knows Himself, and nothing is like Him, yet He has attributes.
The verse balances negation and affirmation.
3
Reflect on the balance
Notice how the response does not attempt a philosophical definition but points to divine transcendence and revealed attributes.
This shows the proper method: accept what Allah says about Himself.

Commentary
This passage presents a hypothetical doubt (whispered by Satan) and its scriptural answer. The doubt: after negating all categories (corporeality, accidentality, whole, part) from God, it seems nothing positive remains to describe His reality. The answer is drawn from the Quran: only God fully knows His own reality ('God does not know except God' — a paraphrase of the verse meaning 'none knows God but God'), and the verse affirms both His incomparability ('There is nothing like Him') and His positive attributes ('He is the Hearing, the Seeing'). This shows that the negation of created categories does not lead to absolute negation; rather, we affirm what He affirms of Himself while maintaining His transcendence.

The Proof of God's Transcendence from Pre-Eternity
Commentator defines mukhalafa and presents a proof via qidam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute of God's transcendence (mukhalafa) is defined and proven through the argument from pre-eternity.

Core Idea
God's transcendence is proven by His pre-eternity: if He were like events, He would be originated.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid explains a central tenet of Islamic theology: God is absolutely unlike His creation. The commentator defines mukhalafa as the negation of all creaturely attributes, addresses a skeptical objection, and then presents a logical proof based on God's pre-eternity. By studying this passage, you will grasp both the meaning and the rational foundation of God's incomparability.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of mukhalafa
Identify what mukhalafa negates: corporeality, accidentality, universality, particularity, and their consequences. Understand these terms as categories of contingent beings.
Notice that the definition is negative—it strips away all attributes of created things.
2
Consider the objection about God's essence
A hypothetical objection asks: if God is none of these, what is He? The response quotes Quran 42:11, affirming that only Allah knows His essence.
This teaches that we must not speculate beyond what revelation says.
3
Follow the proof from pre-eternity
The commentator presents two formulations: (1) If not different from events, then similar; if similar, then originated; but God is pre-eternal, so not similar. (2) Pre-eternity itself implies impossibility of non-existence, unlike events, so God is different.
Both arguments use the established attribute of pre-eternity to prove transcendence.
4
Understand the nuance of two kinds of mukhalafa
The commentator clarifies that the mukhalafa proven here means God is not from the events, distinct from the negative attributes mentioned earlier. This ensures the argument is sound.
This distinction protects against a misinterpretation of the proof.

(The section on) Divine Matters. 15. From among events, and it is [؟] ; because eternal non-existences are necessary as previously mentioned (1), and his father mentioned them as an example of prior non-existence, and did not make them from events. And the difference from what is mentioned (2) is: the negation of corporeality, accidentality, universality, particularity and their concomitants from Him, Exalted. For the concomitant of corporeality is occupying space, the concomitant of accidentality is subsisting in another, the concomitant of universality is greatness, the concomitant of particularity is smallness, etc. • So if Satan casts into your mind: 'If the Lord is not a body, nor an accident, nor a universal, nor a particular, then what is His reality?' His saying: (The proof of this is pre-eternity) meaning: the evidence of what was mentioned, that He is different from events, is the evidence of pre-eternity, so the author's words are upon the assumption of an omitted genitive construction. And the exposition of the proof is that you say: If He were not different from events, He would be similar to them (3), and if He were similar to them, He would be originated, whereas His pre-eternity has been established by the previous evidence?! And it is valid to keep the author's words upon their apparent meaning so that pre-eternity itself is the evidence for the difference; because everyone for whom pre-eternity is necessary, non-existence is impossible for him, and none of the events have non-existence impossible for them, so none of them is pre-eternal, so the difference is established (4). (1) Meaning: necessary for the possible as said by al-'Allāma al-Amīr, and the eternal non-existences are from the category of the eternality of possibility, conceivable, not from the category of the possibility of eternality; for those are impossible, and see what preceded (p. 155). And they are (2) Meaning: for events that are reached by non-existence. (3) In their quiddity and similarity in the accidental is not required; for the consideration is by the essential which discloses the quiddity and indicates the sharing in genus, and from here you realize the error of the one who says: 'He is a body not like bodies', for example. (4) His saying: (So the difference is established) meaning that God is not from the events, so the difference resulting from this evidence is different from the difference counted among His attributes; because it is the negation of corporeality, accidentality, etc. as mentioned, and the difference resulting from this evidence is that He is not included in events nor counted among them, so this evidence is not apparent. End of al-Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a composite exposition: it defines the divine attribute of mukhalafa (difference from created things), addresses a skeptical objection, and proves the attribute through the argument from qidam (pre-eternity). The commentator begins by stating the subject: divine matters. Then he clarifies a point about eternal non-existences, noting they are necessary for possibles, which sets the stage. Next, he defines mukhalafa as the negation of corporeality (jirmiyya), accidentality ('aradiyya), universality (kulliyya), particularity (juz'iyya), and their concomitants. These are key: occupying space, subsisting in another, greatness, smallness, etc. This definition is negative – it strips away all attributes of contingent beings from God. After defining, he presents a hypothetical objection from Satan: if God is not a body, accident, universal, or particular, then what is He? The response is a direct appeal to divine inscrutability: 'None knows God but God,' citing Quran 42:11, 'There is nothing like Him.' The commentator then shifts to the proof of this attribute from the matn (core text): the proof is al-qidam (pre-eternity). He explains that the author's phrase 'burhānu hādhā al-qidam' (the proof of this is pre-eternity) should be understood as 'the evidence for [mukhalafa] is pre-eternity,' with an omitted genitive. He provides a full logical argument: if God were not different from events, He would be similar to them; if similar, then originated (since all events are originated); but God is pre-eternal (proved earlier), so similarity is absurd, hence mukhalafa is proven. Alternatively, the commentator suggests that pre-eternity itself is direct evidence: whatever is necessarily pre-eternal cannot undergo non-existence, but all events can undergo non-existence, so none are pre-eternal, establishing God's difference. The footnotes add nuance: the difference proven here is not the same as the negative attributes like 'not a body'; rather it means God is not included among events at all. The passage thus combines definition, objection, and proof into a coherent unit.

Misconception: If God is not a body, accident, universal, or particular, then He must be nothing or inconceivable.
Correction: The correct response is that God's essence is known only to Himself; we affirm what He affirms and negate what He negates. The Qur'anic verse 'There is nothing like Him' suffices.

Key Takeaway

The Two Meanings of Allah's Self-Subsistence and Their Proofs
Sharqawi defines the two aspects of qiyam bi al-nafs and presents their proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two components of qiyam bi al-nafs (independence from a locus and from a specifying agent) and the rational proofs that establish them.

Core Idea
Allah's self-subsistence means He needs neither a place to inhere in nor an external creator.

The concept of qiyam bi al-nafs is fundamental in Islamic theology, but it has two distinct aspects: freedom from needing a substratum and freedom from needing a maker. This lesson unpacks both meanings and shows how theologians prove them using rational arguments, ending with a framework that contrasts Allah, His attributes, and the attributes of contingents.

Guided Path
1
Read the two definitions
The passage first defines self-subsistence as lack of need for a locus (mahall) and lack of need for a specifying agent (mukhassis). Understand each term carefully.
Note that 'locus' does not mean physical place; it means a substratum in which something inheres.
2
Examine the proof for independence from a locus
Follow the logical argument: if Allah needed a locus, He would be an attribute; but attributes cannot be qualified with attributes of meanings, which are necessary for Him. This leads to a contradiction, so the assumption is false.
The proof uses reductio ad absurdum (khalaf).
3
Examine the proof for independence from a specifying agent
If Allah needed a specifying agent, He would be contingent; but His existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness have already been established, so He cannot be contingent.
This proof relies on previously established attributes of necessity.
4
Study the caution and fourfold categorization
The passage concludes with a tanbih that distinguishes four cases: Allah independent of both, His attributes independent of specifying agent but subsisting in His essence, essences of contingents needing a specifying agent but not a locus, and attributes of contingents needing both.
Reflect on why 'need' is not used for Allah's attributes to avoid misunderstanding.

(٢) تقدم ( ص ١٥٤).
(۳) ولم يذكر السلبية ؛ لأن اتصاف المعنى بالصفات السلبية صحيح؛ فيقال : البياض ليس بسواد.

And the meaning of His self-subsistence is His lack of need, exalted, for a locus (al-mahall); i.e., the essence in which He subsists, not meaning place, because that is known from His dissimilarity to contingents. And al-Ghanimi said: (There is no impediment to carrying 'al-mahall' over its two meanings here) (1). And His lack of need, exalted, for a specifying agent (al-mukhassis); i.e., the bringer into existence. This second is, although it is dispensed with by pre-eternity, it was previously stated that the scholars are not satisfied in this discipline with the indication of implicative implication (dalālat al-iltizām) due to the severe danger of ignorance of the creeds (2). So the meaning of self-subsistence is two things: His lack of need for a locus, and His lack of need for a specifying agent.

The proof for His lack of need for a locus: if He were in need of a locus, He would be an attribute; and if He were an attribute, He would not be qualified with attributes of meanings and moral attributes (3), and they are necessarily subsistent by Him, exalted, according to evidence indicating that. This is a contradiction (khalaf) with a fatha on the kha, i.e., worthy of being thrown behind the back, or with a damma, i.e., false and void. And if that is invalidated, what it entailed is invalidated, namely His being an attribute; then what it entailed also is invalidated, namely His need for a locus. And if His need for a locus is invalidated, His lack of need for a locus is established, and that is what is desired.

The proof for His lack of need for a specifying agent: if He were in need of a specifying agent, He would be contingent (ḥādith); how, when already the necessity of His existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness as essence and attributes have preceded?!

Caution (tanbīh): It is known from this that He, exalted, is independent of both locus and specifying agent together. As for His attributes, they are independent of the specifying agent and subsist by His essence, exalted; one does not express in them 'need' for the essence because of the ambiguity it contains. And al-Fakhr was disrespectful when he used the words 'need' and 'requirement' for them. The essences of contingents are in need of a specifying agent and independent of the essence in which they subsist; and the attributes of contingents are in need of both together. So the divisions are four: ponder.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage explains the two meanings of qiyām bi al-nafs and provides proofs for each. It begins by stating that the meaning of self-subsistence is two things: (1) lack of need for a locus (mahall), meaning a substratum in which to subsist, not a physical place, since Allah's dissimilarity to contingents already indicates He is not spatial; (2) lack of need for a specifying agent (mukhassis), which is the agent that brings something into existence. Although the second could be inferred from pre-eternity, the author notes that scholars do not rely on implicative implications due to the gravity of ignorance in matters of creed.

Then it presents the proof for the first aspect: if Allah needed a locus, He would be an attribute (since attributes inhere in substances). But if He were an attribute, He could not be qualified with attributes of meanings and moral attributes, which must subsist in Him based on evidence. This leads to a contradiction (khalaf). Therefore, the assumption of need for a locus is false, so its opposite is established: He does not need a locus.

The proof for the second aspect: if Allah needed a specifying agent, He would be a contingent being (ḥādith). But His existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness have already been proven for His essence and attributes. Thus He cannot be contingent, so He does not need a specifying agent.

A caution (tanbīh) follows summarizing the logical categories: Allah is independent of both locus and specifying agent; His attributes are independent of a specifying agent but subsist in His essence (though 'need' is not used for them due to potential misunderstanding); the essences of contingents need a specifying agent but are independent of a locus; the attributes of contingents need both. Thus four divisions. The passage also criticizes Fakhr al-Din al-Razi for improperly using the language of need for Allah's attributes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That qiyam bi al-nafs only means independence from a locus, ignoring independence from a specifying agent.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that self-subsistence has two meanings: lack of need for a locus and lack of need for a specifying agent. Both are necessary for a complete understanding.

A) His independence from a specifying agent
B) His independence from a locus
C) His independence from both
D) His subsistence in Himself

Key Takeaway
Allah's self-subsistence means He is independent from both needing a locus and needing a specifying agent, and this is proven through rational arguments that avoid contradiction.

Allah is Free from Opposites, Likenesses, Partners, Children, and Companions
The poem lists the five categories negated from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic doctrine of tanzih by learning the five categories that are negated from Allah.

Core Idea
Allah is absolutely unique, utterly unlike any created thing.

This lesson introduces the fundamental belief of tawhid as expressed in tanzih. The poet enumerates the five things that cannot be attributed to Allah: opposites, likenesses, partners, children, and companions. Understanding this list is essential to grasping Islamic monotheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem line and translation
Read the Arabic line of poetry once, then its literal translation. Notice the five terms being negated.
The line is from the Jawharat al-Tawhid.
2
Understand each term
Identify each of the five terms: didd (opposite), shibh (likeness), sharik (partner), walad (child), sadiq (companion). Reflect on what each means in context.
Didd is a logical opposite, not just an enemy.
3
Reflect on why each negation is necessary
Consider why attributing any of these to Allah would compromise tawhid. The next lesson will explain the proof for didd.

تنزيه الله تعالى عن الضد والشبيه والشريك والولد والصديق] ـ ـ ٢٦ - عَنْ ضِدُّ أَوْ شِبْهِ شَرِيكَ مُطْلَفَا وَوَالِدٍ كَذَا الوَلَدُ وَالأَصْدِقَا قوله : (عَنْ ضِدَّ) أي: مضادّ له تعالى، والجار والمجرور متعلق بقوله (منزهاً)، والضدانِ هما الأمران الوجوديان اللذان بينهما غاية الخلاف لا يجتمعان، فلو فُرضَ أن الله ضدا في ذاته أو صفاته، لوجب ارتفاع ذاته أو صفاته ارتفاعاً مطلقاً إن ثبت الضد دائماً، أو ارتفاعاً مقيّداً بحالة وجود الضد إن لم يثبت دائماً ؛ لأنه متى ثبت أحد الضدين ارتفع الآخر، والفرضُ أنه واجب الوجود قديمٌ، وكذا صفاته، هذا خَلفٌ (۱)
Allah is Exalted from Didḍ (opposite), Shabīh (likeness), Sharīk (partner), Walad (child), and Ṣadīq (companion). [Poem line:] 26 - From didḍ or shabīh, partner is cut off? and parent and similarly child and close friends. His statement: 'ʿan ḍidḍ' i.e., opposite to Him, exalted. The prepositional phrase is connected to His statement 'munazzahan'. And the two opposites (ḍiddān) are two existential things between which there is ultimate opposition, they do not concur. So if it were assumed that Allah has a didḍ in His essence or attributes, then His essence or attributes would necessarily cease to exist, absolutely if the opposite is eternal, or conditionally at the time of the opposite's existence if not eternal, because whenever one opposite is established, the other ceases. And the assumption is that He is the Necessary Existent, eternal, and likewise His attributes. This is absurd (khalf). (1)
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage covers the doctrine of tanzīh (transcendence) by negating various entities from Allah: didḍ (opposite), shabīh (likeness), sharīk (partner), walad (child), and ṣadīq (close companion). The author then explains the meaning of 'didḍ' as two existential things with ultimate mutual opposition that cannot coexist. The argument shows that if Allah had a didḍ, His existence would be contingent on the absence of the opposite, which is impossible for the Necessary Existent. Similarly, a shabīh (likeness) is negated because it would imply multiplicity and limitation. The proof is a classic kalam demonstration from the impossibility of two necessary existents.

Allah is above having any opposite, likeness, partner, child, or companion.

?

?
Have you ever felt that Allah is distant or in need of intermediaries to be approached? How does negating sadiq (companion) affirm that Allah is directly accessible and without need of companionship? What change does this demand in your relationship with Him?

The Proof that Allah Cannot Have an Opposite
The author defines 'didd' and gives a proof against Allah having one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'didd' and the rational proof that Allah cannot have an opposite.

Core Idea
If Allah had an opposite, His existence would depend on the absence of that opposite, which is impossible for the Necessary Existent.

After negating opposites from Allah in the previous lesson, the author now defines what an opposite is and provides a logical proof. This proof demonstrates that a necessary existent cannot coexist with an opposite, as it would entail contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of didd
The passage defines 'didd' as two existential things that are maximally opposed and cannot coexist. Focus on the phrase 'ultimate opposition' and 'do not concur'.
Didd is a technical term in kalam.
2
Understand the argument
Work through the conditional: if Allah had a didd, then His essence or attributes would cease to exist. Consider the two cases: the didd is eternal or not.
The proof uses the principle that opposites cannot coexist.
3
See the conclusion
The assumption leads to an absurdity (khalf) because Allah is the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) and eternal. Therefore, He cannot have a didd.
This is a classic kalam proof for tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The prepositional phrase is connected to His statement 'munazzahan'. And the two opposites (ḍiddān) are two existential things between which there is ultimate opposition, they do not concur. ?

?

Divine Transcendence

Negation of a divine opposite (ḍidd)
Definition of 'ḍidd' and proof of impossibility of divine opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of an opposite (ḍidd) in Islamic theology and why it is impossible for God to have an opposite.

Core Idea
God cannot have an opposite because opposites cancel each other, but God is eternal and necessary.

In this lesson, the commentator explains what an 'opposite' (ḍidd) means in a theological sense: two existent things that cannot coexist. He then proves that applying this concept to God would lead to a contradiction, because God's essence and attributes are eternal and necessary. Understanding this proof strengthens our conviction in God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First read the Arabic and translation. See how the author defines 'ḍidd' and then presents the proof.
Pay attention to the logical structure.
2
Understand the definition of ḍidd
Note that the two opposites are existential matters with extreme opposition, and they cannot coexist. This is the key premise.
The definition is technical and specific.
3
Follow the proof step by step
The proof is conditional: if God had an opposite, then His essence or attributes would be removed either absolutely or conditionally. But God is necessary and eternal, so this is impossible.
The proof hinges on the rule that opposites cannot coexist.
4
Reflect on the contradiction
Realise that the assumption leads to a 'khalaf' (contradiction), so the assumption must be false. This is a standard logical method in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (عَنْ ضِدَّ) أي: مضادّ له تعالى، والجار والمجرور متعلق بقوله (منزهاً)، والضدانِ هما الأمران الوجوديان اللذان بينهما غاية الخلاف لا يجتمعان، فلو فُرضَ أن الله ضدا في ذاته أو صفاته، لوجب ارتفاع ذاته أو صفاته ارتفاعاً مطلقاً إن ثبت الضد دائماً، أو ارتفاعاً مقيّداً بحالة وجود الضد إن لم يثبت دائماً ؛ لأنه متى ثبت أحد الضدين ارتفع الآخر، والفرضُ أنه واجب الوجود قديمٌ، وكذا صفاته، هذا خَلفٌ
His statement: 'an ḍidd' meaning: contrary to Him, may He be exalted. The prepositional phrase is attached to His statement 'munazzahan'. And the two opposites are two existential matters between which there is extreme opposition and they cannot coexist. So if it were supposed that God has an opposite in His essence or attributes, then it would necessitate the removal of His essence or attributes absolutely if the opposite is established perpetually, or a restricted removal in the state of the existence of the opposite if it is not established perpetually; because whenever one of the two opposites is established, the other is removed. And the assumption is that He is necessarily existent and eternal, and likewise His attributes. This leads to a contradiction.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage defines 'ḍidd' (opposite) as two existent things that are maximally opposed and cannot coexist. The proof: If God had an opposite, then when that opposite exists, God's essence or attributes would have to cease to exist, because opposites cannot coexist. But God is necessarily existent (wājib al-wujūd) and eternal (qadīm), so His essence and attributes cannot cease. Therefore, assuming an opposite leads to a contradiction, proving that God has no opposite.

Key Terms
ضد
مضاد
الضدان
غاية الخلاف
ارتفاع
واجب الوجود
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'opposite' in this context means a rival being or a competitor in power, like another god.
Correction: The term 'ḍidd' has a specific technical definition: two existent things that are maximally opposed and cannot coexist. It is not simply a rival or enemy. The proof uses this logical definition to show impossibility.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for God to have an opposite (ḍidd)?
A) Because God is one
B) Because opposites cannot coexist and God is eternal
C) Because the Quran denies it
D) Because God is all-powerful

Key Takeaway
God cannot have an opposite because if He did, His existence would be contingent on the absence of that opposite, contradicting His necessary and eternal nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials, do you ever feel as though something in creation could overpower God's will? How does this proof—that God cannot have an opposite—challenge that feeling and what would it mean for you to truly internalize it?
?
Is there anything in your life that you treat as a 'rival' to God's authority over your heart—a fear, a desire, a person? If God has no opposite, then nothing can stand against His claim on you. What would it take for you to stop feeding that rivalry and submit fully?
?
The proof shows that God's essence cannot be removed even conditionally. Yet you sometimes live as though your sins or failures could undo His mercy or presence. Are you willing to let go of that false belief and trust that nothing—not even your own shortcomings—can diminish His reality?

Understanding the Negation of Likeness (Shibh)
The commentator defines 'shibh' and related terms, affirming God's uniqueness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific meaning of 'shibh' in this theological context and why Allah is absolutely free from any likeness.

Core Idea
Allah is unlike anything: no similarity, no equal, no match.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the term 'shibh' (likeness) and related words to clarify that Allah is absolutely unique. He distinguishes between different degrees of similarity and asserts that Allah has no similar whatsoever in His essence, attributes, or actions. This is a foundational point of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text once without analyzing; then read the literal translation. Let the commentator's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'aw' instead of 'waw' and the poetic necessity.
2
Distinguish the three terms
Identify the three terms: shibh/shabīh, naẓīr, and mathīl. Note how each is defined as a different degree of equality.
The passage says shibh/shabīh is equal in most aspects, naẓīr in some, mathīl in all.
3
Understand the proof and conclusion
Read the proof: Allah must be different from all contingent beings. Therefore, He has no similar. Reflect on why this is necessary for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أَوْ شِبْهِ) معطوف على (ضد)، و(أو) بمعنى الواو، وإنما عبر الناظم بـ (أو) لضرورة النظم والشبه والشبيه بمعنى ؛ كالحب والحبيب، وذلك المعنى هو المساوي في أغلب الوجوه والنظير : هو المساوي ولو في بعض الوجوه، والمثيل: هو المساوي في جميع الوجوه، لكن المراد بالشبه هنا : مطلق المشابه، فيشمل كلا منهما، فليس له تعالى مشابه في ذاته ولا في صفاته ولا في أفعاله؛ لوجوب مخالفته تعالى للممكنات ذاتاً وصفات وأفعالاً.
His statement: 'aw shibhi' is coordinated with 'ḍidd', and 'aw' is in the meaning of 'wāw'. The poet only used 'aw' due to poetic necessity. 'Shabh' and 'shabīh' have the same meaning, like 'ḥubb' and 'ḥabīb'. And that meaning is 'the equal in most aspects'. 'Naẓīr' is the equal in some aspects. 'Mathīl' is the equal in all aspects. But what is meant by 'shibh' here is absolute similarity, so it includes both. So He, exalted, has no similar in His essence, nor in His attributes, nor in His actions, due to the necessity of His being different from contingent beings in essence, attributes, and actions.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage explains the term 'shibh' (likeness). The commentator notes that 'aw' is used instead of 'wāw' for poetic necessity. He then distinguishes between shibh/shabīh (equal in most aspects), naẓīr (equal in some aspects), and mathīl (equal in all aspects). In this context, 'shibh' means any form of similarity. The proof that God has no likeness is that He must necessarily be different (mukhālif) from all contingent beings (mumkināt) in essence, attributes, and actions.

Key Terms
شبه
شبيه
النظير
المثيل
المشابه
مخالفته للممكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'shibh' only means exact likeness or equal in all aspects, so they might think God could have some minor similarity.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that 'shibh' here means absolute similarity, including any degree. God has no similarity at all.

Checkpoint
What does 'shibh' mean in this context?
A) Equal in all respects
B) Equal in most respects
C) Any form of similarity
D) Equal in some respects

Key Takeaway
Allah is absolutely unlike any creation in every way; no similarity exists in essence, attributes, or actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that Allah is unlike anything, but when you imagine His mercy or power, do you subconsciously picture a 'larger' version of a human attribute? What step will you take today to train your mind to affirm complete dissimilarity in attributes?
?
The passage states that Allah has no similar in His actions. Think of an event in your life where you attributed a 'kindness' to Allah that felt like a human gesture. How can you rephrase that experience in a way that respects His absolute transcendence?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is absolutely unlike any thought you can form of Him, what would change about how you speak to Him in dua? Write down one phrase you will consciously replace to avoid shibh in your worship.

Negation of Parent and Child and the Reality of Likeness
Sanusi negates parent and child from God, then defines types of similarity and refutes a misunderstanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be negated from having a parent or child, and how the concept of likeness (shabīh, naẓīr, mithl) is understood in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
God has no parent, no child, and nothing is like Him in any true sense.

Sanusi's text affirms God's transcendence by negating any notion of parent or child from Him. But what does it mean for something to be 'like' God? The lesson unpacks the definitions of similarity and resolves a debate among theologians about the nature of likeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a direct negation and then moves to a scholarly debate.
2
Understand the negation of parent and child
Focus on the first part: why is God negated from having a parent or child? The commentator explains that 'parent' applies to both father and mother, and the result would imply separation from other beings.
The negation is absolute – it covers any form of generation or parentage.
3
Examine the definitions of shabīh, naẓīr, mithl
Find the three terms in the footnote: shabīh (similar in some aspects), naẓīr (counterpart in most), mithl (like in all). Notice the gradation.
These definitions set up the following argument about what true likeness means.
4
Analyze the Ash'ari dispute and its resolution
Read the refutation of the Ash'ari claim that likeness requires equality in all respects. The passage uses a hadith and linguistic usage to show that likeness can be restricted to one aspect.
The resolution clarifies that God's uniqueness is not threatened by limited similarity among creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَوَالِدٍ) أي: ومنزّهاً عن والد أباً كان أو أمّا ؛ لصدق الوالد بهما، فليس منفصلاً عن غيره. وقوله : (كَذَا الوَلَدُ) خبرٌ مقدَّم ومبتدأ مؤخر ؛ أي: الولد كالوالد في وجوب تنزيه الله عنه. وعند العلامة الأمير في حاشيته: الشبيه ولو في بعض الوجوه، والنظير في أغلبها والمثل في جميعها. ثم قال: وفي شرح السعد عند قول النسفي: ولا يشبهه شيء ما نصه: قال الشيخ أبو المعين في «التبصرة»: إنا نجد أهل اللغة لا يمتنعون عن القول بأن زيداً مثل لعمرو الفقه إذا كان يُساويه فيه، ويسد مسده في ذلك الباب وإن كان بينهما مخالفة بوجوه، وما يقوله الأشعرية من أنه لا مماثلة إلا بالمساواة من جميع الوجوه، فاسد ؛ لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «الحنطة بالحنطة مثلاً بمثل»، وأراد الاستواء بالكيل لا غير، وإن تفاوت الوزن وعدد الحبات والصلابة والرخاوة، والظاهر أنه لا مخالفة ؛ لأن مراد الأشعري المساواة من جميع الوجوه فيما به المماثلة؛ كالكيل مثلاً، وإلا فاشتراك الشيئين في جميع الأوصاف ومساواتهما في جميع الوجوه فيرفع التعدد، فكيف يتصور التماثل؟!
His statement: 'wa wālidin' meaning: and exalted from a parent, whether father or mother, because 'parent' applies to both; so He is not separate from other than Him. And his statement: 'kadhā al-waladu' is a predicate fronted and subject delayed; i.e., the child is like the parent in the necessity of exalting God from it. And according to al-Amir in his gloss: al-shabīh (the similar) is even in some aspects, al-naẓīr (the counterpart) is in most, and al-mithl (the like) is in all. Then he said: In the commentary of al-Sa'd on al-Nasafī's statement 'and nothing resembles Him': Abu al-Mu'īn in al-Tabṣira said: we find that linguists do not refrain from saying Zayd is the like of Amr in jurisprudence if he equals him in it and takes his place in that field even if there are differences in other aspects. And what the Ash'aris say that there is no likeness except equality in all respects is invalid, because the Prophet (saw) said: 'Wheat for wheat, like for like,' and he intended equality in measure only, even if weight, number, hardness, and softness differ. And it appears that there is no conflict, because al-Ash'arī meant equality in all respects in that aspect of likeness, like measure, otherwise sharing in all attributes and equality in all respects would remove multiplicity, so how could likeness be conceived?
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage explains the negation of parent and child from God. The commentator interprets 'wālid' as any parent, and 'walad' as child. The proof is implicit: having a child would imply multiplicity and similarity, which are impossible for God. The included footnote from al-Amir discusses the definitions of shabīh, naẓīr, and mithl, and argues against the Ash'ari position that absolute equality in all respects is required for likeness, citing linguistic usage and a hadith about wheat. The conclusion is that if two things shared all attributes, they would be identical and multiplicity would be impossible, so likeness is not absolute identity.

Key Terms
والد
ولد
الشبيه
النظير
المثل
التماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Likeness (mumāthala) requires equality in all respects.
Correction: Likeness can be limited to a specific aspect, as shown by linguistic usage and the Prophet's hadith about wheat exchanged like for like in measure only.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does al-Amir say about 'al-shabīh'?
A) It is equality in all respects.
B) It is similarity in some aspects.
C) It is equality in measure only.
D) It means 'like' in all respects.

Key Takeaway
God is free from parent, child, and any being truly like Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him in terms of your own qualities or human categories? How can you train your mind to negate such likeness?
?
The hadith about wheat 'like for like' shows that equality in one aspect doesn't mean overall similarity. Are there areas in your life where you equate partial resemblance with complete identity, leading to errors in judgment about people or about God?
?
The passage refutes the idea that 'nothing can be said to be like God in any way'. How does this understanding guard against two extremes: excessive negation that strips God of all attributes, and excessive anthropomorphism that limits Him to human qualities? Which extreme are you more prone to, and what step will you take today to correct it?

God's Transcendence Above Human Relations
Commentator denies Jesus' sonship and states God is exalted above friends.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God is completely free from human relations like fatherhood, sonship, and friendship in their literal, physical senses.

Core Idea
God transcends all human social bonds – He is beyond being a friend or a father in the conventional sense.

This lesson begins with the commentator reaffirming that Jesus is not a literal son of God, and that God is exalted above the entire category of friends. The passage sets the foundation for understanding why certain terms cannot be applied to God in their conventional meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening denial of sonship
Read the first sentence which states that Jesus is not a son and that his creation without a father is analogous to Adam's, but Adam is more extraordinary.
2
Note the comparison with Adam
Observe how the commentator uses Adam's creation to emphasize God's power and transcendence: Adam had no parents at all.
3
Understand the phrase 'exalted above friends'
The commentator explains that 'friends' here refers to the genus of friendship, not necessarily multiple individuals. God is above any form of friendship in the human sense.
This sets the stage for the detailed definition of a true friend in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنزه الله عنه، فليس عيسى ولد، بل خلقه الله تعالى بلا أب كما خلق آدم بلا أب بل آدم أغرب؛ حيث خلقه من تراب بلا أب ولا أم، فليس غيره تعالى منفصلاً عنه. قوله : (وَالأَصْدِقَا) أي : ومنزّهاً عن الأصدقاء، وليس الجمع مراداً، بل المراد الجنس المتحقق ولو في واحدٍ، ولذا قال المصنف في «كبيره»: (ويجب التنزه جنس الأصدقاء) انتهى. والصَّدِيقُ : هو الصادق فى ودهِ؛ بحيث يكون معك في الحقِّ، ويضر نفسه لينفعَكَ، وإذا حصل لك مشقةٌ من كدرات الزمان شتّت أمره ليجمع أمرك، كما قال بعضُهم : [من الرجز] إِن صَدِيقَ الحَقِّ مَنْ كَانَ مَعَكُ وَمَنْ يَضُرُّ نَفْسَهُ لِيَنْفَعَكْ وَمَنْ إِذَا رَيْبُ الزَّمَانِ صَدَّعَكْ شَتَّتَ فِيكَ شَمْلَهُ لِيَجْمَعَكْ وهو نادر جدا في هذا الزمان. والمحال أن يكون الله صديق على الوجه المعتاد من أن كلا يعاون صاحبه وينفعه، فلا ينافي أن يكون الله صديق بمعنى المخلص في عبادته تعالى، لكن لا يجوز أن يُطلق : صديق الله ؛ لأنه لم يرد، مع أنه يوهم المعنى المحال. وكما أنه يستحيلُ على الله الأصدقاء، يستحيل عليه الأعداء على الوجه المعتاد ؛ من أن كلا يؤذي الآخر ويضره، فلا ينافي أن يكون الله عدو بمعنى المخالف لأمره، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَوْمَ يُحْشَرُ أَعْدَاءُ الله إِلَى النَّارِ) [فصلت: ۱۹]. ومعنى قوله تعالى في حق سيدنا عيسى عليه السلام: ﴿وَرُوحُ مِنْهُ﴾ [النِّسَاء: ١٧١] ناشئ عنه خلقاً ؛ نظير قوله تعالى : ﴿وَسَخَّرَ لَكُم مَّا فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَمَا فِي الْأَرْضِ جَمِيعًا مِنْهُ﴾ [الجَاثِيَة : ١٣]. وإطلاق لفظ البنوة بالمعنى اللغوي المجازي صحيح فيها، ولكن لم يرد شرعاً، ولكن ورد إشارة إليه في قوله سبحانه وتعالى : ونَحْنُ أَبْنَوا الله وَاحِبو [المائدة: ۱۸] والمعنى : كأبنائه في القرب والمنزلة ، وهو كأبينا في الرحمة والشفقة كما قاله الجلال السيوطي في تفسيره»، وعليه لا يكفر إلا معتقد الجزئية عند هذا الاستعمال
He is exalted above that, so Jesus is not a son, rather Allah created him without a father just as He created Adam without a father—rather Adam is more extraordinary, as He created him from dust without father or mother. So there is none other than Him separate from Him. His statement: (and the friends) meaning: and He is exalted above friends, and the plural is not intended, rather the intended meaning is the genus realized even in one. Hence the author said in his 'Kabir': 'It is necessary to be exalted above the genus of friends.' End. And al-ṣadīq is the one who is truthful in his love, such that he is with you in truth, and harms himself to benefit you, and if you encounter hardship from the vicissitudes of time, he scatters his own affairs to gather your affairs, as some said: [ar-rajaz] 'Indeed the true friend is the one who is with you, and who harms himself to benefit you, and when the uncertainty of time afflicts you, he scattered his totality to gather you.' And it is very rare in this time. And it is impossible for Allah to be a friend in the customary manner that each assists his companion and benefits him. So it does not contradict that Allah is a friend in the sense of being sincere in His worship, but it is not permissible to say 'friend of Allah' because it has not been revealed, and it suggests the impossible meaning. And just as friends are impossible for Allah, enemies are also impossible for Him in the customary manner, that each harms and hurts the other. So it does not contradict that Allah is an enemy in the sense of opposing His command, as in His saying: 'And the day when the enemies of Allah are gathered to the Fire' [Fussilat:19]. And the meaning of His statement concerning our master Jesus: 'and a spirit from Him' [an-Nisa:171] is that He originated him as a creation, similar to His statement: 'And He subjected to you whatever is in the heavens and whatever is in the earth, all from Him' [al-Jathiyah:13]. And the use of the word 'sonship' in a metaphorical linguistic sense is correct in it, but it has not come in revelation, but there is an indication to it in His saying: 'And we are the sons of Allah and His beloved ones' [al-Ma'idah:18] and the meaning: like His sons in nearness and status, and He is like our father in mercy and compassion, as al-Jalal al-Suyuti said in his Tafsir, and accordingly only the one who believes in sonship in the literal sense disbelieves in this usage.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a theological explanation of God's transcendence beyond human social relations, specifically friends and enemies. The commentator begins by continuing a discussion about Jesus, affirming that he is not a literal son of God, and that his creation without a father is analogous to Adam's creation, but Adam is more extraordinary as he had no parents. Then the commentator addresses the term 'al-aṣdiqā' (friends) from the text. He clarifies that the intended meaning is the genus of friendship, not necessarily plurality. He defines a 'ṣadīq' (true friend) as one who is truthful in love, who joins you in truth, harms himself to benefit you, and when you face hardship, he sacrifices his own interests for yours, illustrating with a poem. Such true friendship is very rare. He then states that it is impossible (muḥāl) for God to be a friend in the customary sense of mutual assistance and benefit. This does not preclude God being called 'friend' metaphorically as the one sincere in worship, but the term 'friend of Allah' is not permissible to use because it is not revealed and implies the impossible meaning. Similarly, enemies are impossible for God in the customary sense of mutual harm, but God can be called 'enemy' in the sense of opposing His command, as the Quran says. The commentator then addresses the verse about Jesus being a 'spirit from Him', explaining it refers to creation, not emanation. Finally, he discusses the metaphorical use of 'sonship' in the Bible saying 'we are sons of God', interpreting it as closeness and station, and notes that only literal belief in sonship entails disbelief.

Key Terms
الأصدقاء (al-aṣdiqā')
الولد (al-walad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the plural 'al-aṣdiqā' (friends) indicate?
A) That God has many friends
B) That there are multiple types of friends
C) That it refers to the genus of friendship
D) That it is a grammatical error

Key Takeaway
God is beyond human social categories; even the concept of 'friend' in its conventional sense cannot be applied to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what ways have you, by habit or assumption, pictured God as a 'friend' or 'companion' in a human sense? How does this lesson demand you strip your understanding of such imagery, and what specific mental discipline will you adopt to maintain tawhid?
?
When you hear of prophets being called 'friends of God,' do you feel the tension between that honorific and the absolute negation of friendship in this lesson? How will you reconcile this without compromising God's transcendence?
?
This lesson denies that God is a father or a friend—two relationships that humans deeply crave. Which of these do you find harder to let go of emotionally, and why? Describe one concrete step you will take today to let this lesson reshape how you speak and think about your connection to Allah.

Divine Transcendence

The True Friend and Why God Cannot Be One
Commentator defines true friendship and asserts its impossibility for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the qualities of a true friend in Islamic thought and why such a relationship is impossible for God.

Core Idea
True friendship involves mutual sacrifice and benefit, which is incompatible with God's perfection and self-sufficiency.

After establishing that God is exalted above the genus of friends, the commentator now defines what a true friend (ṣadīq) is. This definition reveals that the customary sense of friendship entails mutual support, which cannot apply to God. The passage then explains why it is impermissible to call God a 'friend' in that sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-ṣadīq
The commentator gives a detailed description: a true friend is truthful in love, stands with you in truth, harms himself to benefit you, and sacrifices his own affairs to help you in hardship.
2
Reflect on the poem
The poem illustrates the ideal friend: one who is with you, harms himself for you, and scatters his own unity to gather yours.
Notice the rarity mentioned: such friendship is very rare in this time.
3
Understand the impossibility for God
Mutual assistance is impossible for God because He is self-sufficient and perfect. Hence, God cannot be a 'friend' in the customary sense.
4
Note the exception and the prohibition
God can be called 'friend' metaphorically as the one sincere in worship, but the phrase 'friend of Allah' is not permissible because it is not revealed and suggests the impossible meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary

Key Terms
الصديق (al-ṣadīq)
المحال (al-muḥāl)
الوجه المعتاد (al-wajh al-mu‘tād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is permissible to call God a 'friend' because the term is positive.
Correction: The commentator explains it is impermissible because it has not been revealed and implies mutual assistance, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for God to be a friend in the customary sense?
A) Because God is too great to have friends
B) Because the concept of mutual assistance is incompatible with God's perfection
C) Because the term 'friend' is never used in the Quran
D) Because friends are always multiple, and God is one

Key Takeaway
True friendship involves mutual sacrifice, which cannot apply to God; hence the term 'friend of God' is not used in revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time you called God your 'Friend' or heard someone else do so. How does this lesson challenge that habit? What must you change in how you speak about your relationship with Allah?
?
The commentator says a true friend 'harms himself to benefit you.' If you fully internalized that this cannot apply to God, does it change how you understand His mercy? Are you tempted to think God 'needs' your worship? What does this show about your heart?
?
You say you love Allah, but do you ever treat Him like a human friend — expecting mutual give-and-take? After today, will you watch your tongue and heart when you speak of your bond with Him? Write down one phrase you will avoid and one you will use instead.

Enemies, Metaphors, and Scriptural Language
Commentator extends argument to enemies and interprets metaphorical scriptural language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God cannot have enemies in a human sense, but the term is used metaphorically; and how metaphorical language is used for 'spirit from Him' and 'sons of God'.

Core Idea
God has no enemies in the human sense; Quranic terms like 'enemies of Allah' are metaphorical, and so are references to sonship.

The commentator now applies the same reasoning to enemies—ruling out customary mutual harm. He then addresses key scriptural phrases: 'spirit from Him' about Jesus and 'sons of God' in the Bible, showing they must be understood metaphorically to maintain God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the section on enemies
Just as friends are impossible, enemies are impossible in the customary sense of mutual harm. But 'enemies of Allah' in the Quran means those who oppose His command.
2
See the Quranic verse
The commentator quotes 'And the day when the enemies of Allah are gathered to the Fire' (Fussilat:19) as an example of metaphorical usage.
3
Understand 'spirit from Him'
The verse about Jesus being a 'spirit from Him' means he was created by God, similar to the subjection of all things 'from Him'.
4
Grasp the metaphor of 'sons of God'
The phrase means closeness and status, not literal sonship. Only those who believe in literal sonship disbelieve.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
