So it does not contradict that Allah is a friend in the sense of being sincere in His worship, but it is not permissible to say 'friend of Allah' because it has not been revealed, and it suggests the impossible meaning. And just as friends are impossible for Allah, enemies are also impossible for Him in the customary manner, that each harms and hurts the other. So it does not contradict that Allah is an enemy in the sense of opposing His command, as in His saying: 'And the day when the enemies of Allah are gathered to the Fire' [Fussilat:19]. And the meaning of His statement concerning our master Jesus: 'and a spirit from Him' [an-Nisa:171] is that He originated him as a creation, similar to His statement: 'And He subjected to you whatever is in the heavens and whatever is in the earth, all from Him' [al-Jathiyah:13]. And the use of the word 'sonship' in a metaphorical linguistic sense is correct in it, but it has not come in revelation, but there is an indication to it in His saying: 'And we are the sons of Allah and His beloved ones' [al-Ma'idah:18] and the meaning: like His sons in nearness and status, and He is like our father in mercy and compassion, as al-Jalal al-Suyuti said in his Tafsir, and accordingly only the one who believes in sonship in the literal sense disbelieves in this usage.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a theological explanation of God's transcendence beyond human social relations, specifically friends and enemies. The commentator begins by continuing a discussion about Jesus, affirming that he is not a literal son of God, and that his creation without a father is analogous to Adam's creation, but Adam is more extraordinary as he had no parents. Then the commentator addresses the term 'al-aṣdiqā' (friends) from the text. He clarifies that the intended meaning is the genus of friendship, not necessarily plurality. He defines a 'ṣadīq' (true friend) as one who is truthful in love, who joins you in truth, harms himself to benefit you, and when you face hardship, he sacrifices his own interests for yours, illustrating with a poem. Such true friendship is very rare. He then states that it is impossible (muḥāl) for God to be a friend in the customary sense of mutual assistance and benefit. This does not preclude God being called 'friend' metaphorically as the one sincere in worship, but the term 'friend of Allah' is not permissible to use because it is not revealed and implies the impossible meaning. Similarly, enemies are impossible for God in the customary sense of mutual harm, but God can be called 'enemy' in the sense of opposing His command, as the Quran says. The commentator then addresses the verse about Jesus being a 'spirit from Him', explaining it refers to creation, not emanation. Finally, he discusses the metaphorical use of 'sonship' in the Bible saying 'we are sons of God', interpreting it as closeness and station, and notes that only literal belief in sonship entails disbelief.

Key Terms
الأعداء (al-a‘dā')
المجاز (al-majāz)
روح منه (rūḥ minhu)
البنوة (al-bunuwwa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'enemies of Allah' indicates God has personal enmity with individuals.
Correction: It means those who oppose His commands; God does not hate in a human sense.

Checkpoint

A) That Jesus is a part of God
B) That Jesus was created by God
C) That Jesus is a divine spirit
D) That the spirit is from God's essence

Key Takeaway
When the Quran speaks of God's enemies or sonship, it uses metaphorical language that must be understood in a way consistent with God's transcendence.

You may have casually used phrases like 'son of God' for Jesus in conversation. After this lesson, will you commit to clarifying the metaphorical meaning to protect God's transcendence? What specific step will you take the next time this topic arises?

Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ Negates Eight Types of Disbelief
Explains how Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ negates eight types of kufr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ refutes eight specific categories of disbelief and why it is a decisive scriptural foundation for divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ is a decisive negation of all false conceptions of God.

This lesson demonstrates how a short sūrah serves as a foundational proof for God's transcendence. The author explains the occasion of revelation, where polytheists asked about God in material terms, and shows how each verse of Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ negates a distinct form of disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author presents Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ as the decisive foundation confirming rational proof.
The sūrah is introduced alongside the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' from Sūrat al-Shūrā.
2
Identify the eight types of disbelief
The passage says the sūrah negates 'eight types of disbelief.' As you read, list the specific negations mentioned: multiplicity/number, paucity/deficiency, causality/effectuality, similarity/likeness.
Though eight are claimed, only four are explicitly named; each verse may cover two aspects.
3
Match each verse to its negation
Go through the four verses one by one and connect them to the negation they entail. For example, verse 1 negates multiplicity, verse 2 negates deficiency, etc.
This is the core of the passage's argument.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these negations are important. They refute polytheistic and anthropomorphic concepts, establishing God's absolute uniqueness and self-sufficiency.
This connects the passage to broader tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The decisive foundation in that, confirming the intellectual proof, is His saying the Most High: 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' [Shūrā: 11], and His saying the Most High: 'Say: He is Allah, the One' [Ikhlāṣ: 1] to the end of the sūrah which is called Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ. The reason for its revelation is that the polytheists asked the Prophet about his Lord and said: Describe your Lord to us; is it of gold or of silver? And this sūrah negated the eight types of disbelief; because His saying: 'Say: He is Allah, the One' negated multiplicity and number, and His saying: 'Allah, the Self-Sufficient' [Ikhlāṣ: 2] – He is the one sought in needs – negated paucity and deficiency, and His saying: 'He begets not, nor is He begotten' [Ikhlāṣ: 3] negated causality and effectuality; i.e., that He is a cause for other than Him, or that He is an effect of other than Him; and His saying: 'And there is none equal to Him' [Ikhlāṣ: 4] negated similarity and likeness.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage identifies Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ as the decisive scriptural foundation (al-aṣl al-qāṭiʿ) that confirms the rational proof for God's transcendence. It begins by citing two verses: 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Shūrā 42:11) and the entire sūrah itself. The author then explains the occasion of revelation: the polytheists asked the Prophet to describe his Lord in material terms (gold or silver). The sūrah negates eight types of disbelief (kufr), each verse addressing a specific form. Verse 1 ('Say: He is Allah, the One') negates multiplicity and number. Verse 2 ('Allah, the Self-Sufficient') negates paucity and deficiency. Verse 3 ('He begets not, nor is He begotten') negates causality and effectuality – meaning that God is neither a cause of another nor an effect of another. Verse 4 ('And there is none equal to Him') negates similarity and likeness. This shows how the sūrah comprehensively refutes false conceptions about God.

Correction: The passage shows that the sūrah explicitly negates eight types of disbelief, including polytheism, anthropomorphism, and any suggestion of deficiency or causality in God.

Resolving the Apparent Problem in 'Laysa Kamithlihi Shay'un'
Explains the problem in the verse and gives three answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical theological answers to the problem that arises from the literal reading of the verse 'There is nothing like His like'.

Core Idea
The verse emphatically negates any likeness to Allah through rhetorical devices.

The Qur'anic verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'un' (42:11) is a cornerstone of Islamic divine transcendence. However, a careful reader might notice a seeming paradox: if 'kāf' means 'like', the verse literally says 'nothing is like His like', which could imply a 'like' exists. This lesson explores three classical answers from the tradition that resolve this problem and reinforce the absolute uniqueness of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the problem
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the issue: the verse appears to negate only the 'like of a like', potentially implying a like exists.
The problem is famous among scholars of tafsir and kalam.
2
Study the first answer: redundant kāf
The first answer treats the 'kāf' as a redundant particle for emphasis, making the negation absolute. Understand how this resolves the issue.
3
Study the second answer: mathal as attribute
The second answer reinterprets 'mathal' to mean 'attribute', so the verse negates any similarity in attributes, not a separate entity.
This shifts the focus from essence to attributes.
4
Study the third answer: metonymy (kināya)
The third answer explains the verse as a metaphor: negating the like of the like implies negating the like itself, and this indirect expression is more emphatic.
The logical proof involves a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Review and test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can distinguish the three answers and their rationales.
Reflect on why metonymy is considered more emphatic than explicit statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الآية السابقة إشكال مشهور ; وهو أن الكاف بمعنى مثل، فيصير المعنى: ليس مثل مثلِهِ شيءٌ ، فالمنفي مثل المثل، فتوهمُ الآية حينئذ وجودَ المثل ! (۲) وأجيب عن ذلك بأجوبة : منها : أن الكاف صلةٌ ; أي زائدةٌ لتأكيد نفي المثل ; فالمعنى: انتفى المثل انتفاء مؤكداً . (۳) ومنها : أن المثل بمعنى الصفة فالمعنى : ليس كصفةِ اللهِ شيءٌ . ومنها : أن الآية من باب الكناية; على حدّ مثلُكَ لا يبخل (٤) تريدُ : أنت لا تبخل ووجه كونها من باب الكناية : أنه يلزم من نفي مثل المثل نفي المثل; لأنه لو فُرضَ وجود المثل، لكان الله مثلاً لذلك المثل (۱) ، وهو لا يصح نفيه لوجوبِ وجوده، وقد دلَّتِ الآية على نفي مثل المثل، فلزم من ذلك نفي المثل، وهذا هو المراد، فالقصد نفي مثلِهِ تعالى بأبلغ وجه; إذ الكناية أبلغ من التصريح; لتضمنها إثبات الشيء بدليل
And in the previous verse there is a famous problem; which is that the 'kāf' means 'like', so the meaning becomes: 'There is nothing like His like', so what is negated is the like of the like, so then the verse implies the existence of the like! And it was answered with several answers: among them: that the 'kāf' is a connective (ṣila); i.e., redundant to emphasize the negation of the like; so the meaning: the like is negated with emphatic negation. And among them: that 'mathal' means attribute (ṣifa), so the meaning: 'There is nothing like God's attribute.' And among them: that the verse is of the category of metonymy (kināya), on the pattern of 'your like does not withhold' meaning 'you do not withhold', and the reason for its being of the category of metonymy is that from the negation of the like of the like follows the negation of the like; because if the existence of the like were assumed, then God would be a like to that like, and its negation is not permissible due to the necessity of His existence, and the verse has indicated the negation of the like of the like, so from that follows the negation of the like, and this is what is intended; so the purpose is the negation of His like, the Most High, in the most emphatic manner, for metonymy is more emphatic than explicit statement, because it implies establishing the thing by evidence.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage addresses a famous problem (ishkāl mashhūr) in the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Shūrā 42:11). The problem is that the word 'kāf' (ك) normally means 'like', so the literal reading would be 'There is nothing like His like', which seems to negate only the like of a like, thereby implying that a like exists. The author presents three answers to resolve this. First, the 'kāf' is a redundant particle (ṣila / zā'ida) used for emphasis, so it simply reinforces the negation of any likeness; the meaning is that any like is emphatically negated. Second, 'mathal' here means 'attribute' (ṣifa), so the verse means 'Nothing is like God's attribute', avoiding the implication. Third, the verse is a case of metonymy (kināya), similar to the Arabic expression 'your like does not withhold' meaning 'you do not withhold'. The logic: if the like of the like were negated, then the like itself must be negated; because if a like existed, God would be a like to that like, which is impossible given the necessity of His existence. The verse intends the negation of any like, and metonymy achieves this more emphatically than explicit statement because it implies proof.

Key Terms
الكاف زائدة
المثل بمعنى الصفة
الكناية
نفي المثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the verse literally says 'like of a like', implying that Allah has a like.
Correction: The three answers show that the literal meaning is not intended; instead, the verse uses rhetorical devices to emphatically negate any likeness.

Checkpoint
According to the second answer, what does 'mathal' mean in the verse?
A) Like
B) Attribute
C) Metonymy
D) Redundant

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'un' emphatically negates any likeness to Allah, using redundant particles, reinterpretation of terms, or metonymy to avoid any implication of a like.

When you read 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'un', do you feel awe or familiarity? How will you train your mind to think of Allah as utterly unique, even when the world constantly compares things?
?
The verse uses indirect speech for greater emphasis—how often do you approach Allah's words with the assumption that His rhetoric is always deeper than your first reading? Will you commit to studying Quranic Arabic to catch these subtleties and grow closer to Him?

The Eloquence of Kinaayah in Denying Likeness to God
The commentator explains the logical reasoning behind the verse's use of kinaayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse uses indirect expression (kinaayah) to deny any likeness to Allah and how this makes the denial more emphatic.

Core Idea
Denying indirectly is stronger than denying directly.

The commentator unpacks the logical reasoning behind the verse's use of kinaayah (indirect expression). He shows that by denying the 'like of the like,' the denial of any likeness itself necessarily follows, and this method is more eloquent than explicit statement. Let's explore this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the logical flow of the argument.
Notice the term 'mithl al-mithl' (like of the like).
2
Understand the logical implication
The commentator argues that denying the 'like of the like' entails denying the 'like' itself. Follow the reasoning: if a like existed, God would be like to that like, but God cannot be denied, so the like must be denied.
This is a deductive argument.
3
Compare kinaayah vs. explicit statement
Reflect on why kinaayah is considered more eloquent. The passage states it includes proving the thing by evidence, making the denial stronger.
Think about how indirect speech can be more powerful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تبخل ووجه كونها من باب الكناية : أنه يلزم من نفي مثل المثل نفي المثل؛ لأنه لو فُرضَ وجود المثل، لكان الله مثلاً لذلك المثل (۱) ، وهو لا يصح نفيه لوجوبِ وجوده، وقد دلَّتِ الآية على نفي مثل المثل، فلزم من ذلك نفي المثل، وهذا هو المراد، فالقصد نفي مثلِهِ تعالى بأبلغ وجه؛ إذ الكناية أبلغ من التصريح؛ لتضمنها إثبات الشيء بدليل (۲)
Do not be stingy? And the aspect of it being of the category of kinaayah is that denying the like of the like entails denying the like; because if the existence of a like were supposed, then God would be a like to that like (1), and it is not valid to deny Him due to the necessity of His existence, and the verse has indicated the denial of the like of the like, so from that the denial of the like follows, and this is the intended meaning. So the purpose is to deny His likeness—exalted is He—in the most eloquent manner, for kinaayah is more eloquent than explicit statement because it includes proving the thing by evidence (2).
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument explaining why the verse uses kinaayah (indirect expression) to deny any likeness to God. The commentator begins by stating that the aspect of verse being from the category of kinaayah is that denying the 'like of the like' (mithl al-mithl) necessitates denying the 'like' (al-mithl) itself. The reasoning is as follows: if we were to suppose the existence of a 'like' of God, then God would be a like to that like (since both are similar). But we cannot deny God's existence because it is necessary; however, the verse denies the 'like of the like' (i.e., something that is a like to God's like). From this, it logically follows that the 'like' itself is denied. The intended meaning is to deny any likeness to God in the most emphatic way, for kinaayah is more eloquent than explicit statement because it implies the proof of the thing as well.

Key Terms
كناية
مثل
نفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse explicitly denies any likeness to God, but the commentator shows it is a more indirect and stronger denial.
Correction: The verse uses kinaayah, which is an indirect expression that implies the proof, making the denial more emphatic than a direct statement.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the verse use kinaayah rather than explicit statement?
A) Because explicit denial is forbidden.
B) Because kinaayah is more eloquent and implies the proof.
C) Because the verse is ambiguous.
D) Because the like does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Kinaayah is a powerful rhetorical tool; in this verse, it strengthens the denial of any likeness to Allah by making the proof implicit.

The Four Divisions of God's Will and Command
Enumerates four cases of divine will and command, refuting Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical divisions of God's will and command, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God's will and command are identical.

Core Idea
God's will and command are not always the same; they can be separate for a divine wisdom.

This passage clarifies a subtle but crucial point in Islamic theology: the relationship between God's will (irada) and His command (amr). The author, Imam al-Sanusi, enumerates four logical possibilities, demonstrating that God may command something without willing it, and vice versa. This refutes the Mu'tazilite position that God's will for a person's act is identical to His command.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the use of examples.
2
Identify the four divisions
The passage presents four logical possibilities: (1) neither will nor command, (2) will but not command, (3) command but not will, (4) both will and command (implied). Find each in the text and note the examples given.
The fourth division is not explicitly listed but understood from the context.
3
Understand the examples
The author gives specific examples: disbelief from certain disbelievers (case 1), disbelief and sins from those God knows will not believe (case 2), and faith from those disbelievers (case 3). Reflect on why these examples are chosen.
The example in case 2 shows that God wills disbelief for some but does not command it.
4
Connect to the Mu'tazila refutation
The Mu'tazila argued that God's will and command are identical, meaning if God commands something He must will it. The four divisions show this is false: God can command faith without willing it from certain people.
The verse 'He is not questioned about what He does' justifies God's wisdom.
5
Reflect on divine wisdom
Consider why God would command something He does not will. The passage says it is for a wisdom known only to Him. Reflect on the principle that God's actions are not subject to human questioning.
This emphasizes divine transcendence and sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منهم وأمرهم به، وقد لا يريد ولا يأمر كالكفر من هؤلاء، فإنه تعالى لم يرده منهم ولم يأمرهم به، وقد يريد ولا يأمر كالكفر الواقع ممن علم الله عدم إيمانهم، وكالمعاصي فإنه أراد ذلك ولم يأمر به، وقد يأمر ولا يريد كإيمان هؤلاء ؛ فإنه أمرهم به ولم يرده منهم، وإنما أمرهم به مع كونه لم يرده منهم لحكمة يعلمها سبحانه وتعالى: وَلَا يُسْأَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ [الأنبياء: ٢٣]، فالأقسام أربعة.
From them and His command to them, and He may not will nor command, like disbelief from these, for He did not will it from them nor command them to it. And He may will but not command, like disbelief occurring from those whom God knew would not believe, and like sins, for He willed that but did not command it. And He may command but not will, like faith of these, for He commanded them to it but did not will it from them. He only commanded them to it despite His not willing it from them for a wisdom He knows, Exalted is He: 'He is not questioned about what He does' [Quran 21:23]. So the divisions are four.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a classification of four logical possibilities regarding God's will (irada) and command (amr). The author enumerates: (1) God neither wills nor commands something, as in disbelief from certain disbelievers; (2) He wills but does not command, as in disbelief from those He knows will not believe, and sins in general; (3) He commands but does not will, as in faith from those disbelievers; (4) He both wills and commands (implied but not stated here). The purpose is to refute the Mu'tazilite claim that God's will for another's act is identical to His command. The verse 'He is not questioned about what He does' justifies that God's command without will is for a hidden wisdom.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
أمر (amr)
كفر (kufr)
معصية (ma'siya)
حكمة (hikma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's command for something implies He always wills that thing to happen from the commanded person.
Correction: The passage shows God can command faith without willing it from certain individuals, and can will disbelief without commanding it. Command and will are not identical.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of God commanding but not willing?
A) Disbelief from certain disbelievers
B) Disbelief from those God knows will not believe
C) Faith from those disbelievers
D) Sins in general

Key Takeaway
God's will and command are distinct; He may command what He does not will and will what He does not command, all for a wisdom known only to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself disobeying God, do you try to lessen your guilt by thinking, 'God willed it anyway'? How does realizing that God wills but does not command sins change your accountability before Him? Will you repent today?
?
God commands you to believe in Him, but He may not have willed that from you. Does that thought make you tremble at His sovereignty? How will you strive to ensure your faith is genuine, knowing that only those whom He wills to believe truly believe? Will you ask Him today for steadfastness?
?
There are people whom God commands to believe but does not will belief from them. If you were among them, you would be left in disbelief. When you look at the state of your own heart, does it humble you that your faith is only by His will? How does that move you to thank Him and rely on His mercy today?

Divine Transcendence
beginner

Dealing with Anthropomorphic Texts: Ta'wil and Tafwid
Commentator explains the meaning of 'text' and 'gives the impression of anthropomorphism' in the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two permissible methods (ta'wil and tafwid) for dealing with scriptural texts that seem to imply anthropomorphism, and the importance of affirming God's transcendence.

Core Idea
When scripture seems to say God is like us, we either interpret it or entrust its meaning to God.

One of the most delicate tasks in Islamic theology is dealing with Quranic and hadith texts that, taken literally, seem to attribute human-like qualities to Allah. The great scholar al-Laqqani summarizes the correct approach in a single verse: such texts must be either interpreted figuratively (ta'wil) or their true meaning delegated to Allah (tafwid), while always affirming His absolute transcendence (tanzih). In this lesson, we will unpack this verse and its commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the source verse and translation
Read the Arabic verse 'وكل نص أوهم التشبيها أوله أو فوض ورم تنزيهها' and its translation. Notice the two commands: 'interpret it' or 'delegate' and the goal 'seek to declare it transcendent'.
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on the terms 'nass', 'tashbih', ta'wil, tafwid, and tanzih. The commentator explains what 'nass' means here: it is a text from Quran or Sunnah that is not already settled by consensus or analogy, and it does not mean an unambiguous text.
An unambiguous text cannot be interpreted away.
3
Explore the concept of 'giving the impression'
The phrase 'awhama al-tashbiha' means the text creates an impression (wahm) of anthropomorphism in the mind, not that it necessarily states it. The commentator clarifies that 'tashbih' here means 'likeness' (mushabaha) as a concept.
4
Identify the two methods
The verse gives two options: ta'wil (figurative interpretation) or tafwid (delegation of the true meaning to Allah). Both are valid, but the goal is always to affirm tanzih (transcendence).
Scholars differ on which is preferred, but both are acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُلُّ نَصٍّ أَوْهَمَ التَّشْبِيهَا أَوْلَهُ أَوْ فَوِّضُ وَرُمْ تَنْزِيهَا قوله : (وَكُلُّ نَصٍّ ... إلخ) يصح قراءة (كل) بالرفع مبتدأ، وجملة (أوله) خبر، وبالنصب مفعول لفعل محذوف من باب الاشتغال والمراد بالنص هنا : ما قابل القياس والاستنباط والإجماع، وهو الدليل من الكتاب أو السنة، وليس المراد به ما قابل الظاهر، وهو ما أفاد معنى لا يحتمل غيره، إذ لو كان هذا هو المراد لم يمكن تأويله. وقوله: (أَوْهَمَ التَّشْبِيهَا)؛ أي: أوقع في الوهم صحة القول به بحسب ظاهره والمراد من التشبيه المشابهة لا فعل الفاعل.
And every text that gives the impression of anthropomorphism — either interpret it (ta'wīl) or delegate (tafwīḍ) and seek to declare it transcendent. His statement: (and every text... etc.) — it is valid to read 'kullu' in the nominative as a subject, and the clause 'interpret it' is the predicate, and in the accusative as an object of a deleted verb from the category of 'al-ishtighāl'. What is meant by 'text' here is what is opposed to analogy (qiyās), deduction (istinbāṭ), and consensus (ijmāʿ), and it is the evidence from the Book or the Sunnah. It does not mean what is opposed to the apparent meaning (al-ẓāhir), i.e., what conveys a meaning that cannot be other than it, because if that were intended, it could not be interpreted. And his statement: (gives the impression of anthropomorphism) means: it creates in the mind the validity of holding it according to its apparent meaning. What is meant by 'anthropomorphism' (al-tashbīh) is likeness (al-mushābaha), not the act of making similar.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a poetic verse (line 40) from the Jawharat al-Tawḥīd. The verse commands that any scriptural text (nass) that gives the impression of anthropomorphism (tashbīh) must be dealt with in one of two ways: either ta'wīl (figurative interpretation) or tafwīḍ (delegation of the true meaning to God), while always affirming God's transcendence (tanzīh). The commentator clarifies the scope of 'text' here: it refers to evidence from the Quran or Sunnah that is suitable for interpretation, as opposed to matters settled by consensus or deduction. He emphasizes that 'text' here does not mean a clear, unambiguous statement (nass as opposed to ẓāhir), because an unambiguous statement cannot be interpreted away. The phrase 'gives the impression of anthropomorphism' means that the apparent wording suggests a likeness between God and creation, but that impression is only a wahm (illusion). The commentator clarifies that 'tashbīh' here means 'likeness' (mushābaha) as a concept, not the verbal noun of making something similar. The underlying principle is that God is absolutely transcendent, so any text that seems to imply similarity must be reinterpreted or its true meaning entrusted to God.

Key Terms
نص
تشبيه
تأويل
تفويض
تنزيه
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that only explicit comparative texts need interpretation, but even texts that merely create an impression require ta'wil or tafwid.
Correction: The passage states that any text that 'gives the impression' (awhama) of anthropomorphism must be addressed, not only those explicitly stating likeness.

Checkpoint
What are the two permissible methods for dealing with a scriptural text that gives the impression of anthropomorphism?
A) Rejection and negation.
B) Ta'wīl (interpretation) and tafwīḍ (delegation).
C) Literal acceptance and comparison.
D) Consensus and analogy.

Key Takeaway
When a scriptural text seems to imply God resembles creation, a believer must either interpret it figuratively or entrust its true meaning to Allah, while always affirming His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses about Allah's attributes that seem anthropomorphic (like His hand or His coming), do you feel a tension in your heart? How do you typically resolve that tension — by interpreting it, by trusting Allah's wisdom, or by avoiding the thought altogether? This lesson calls you to either interpret or delegate; which path have you been taking, and what does that say about your relationship with Allah's revelation?
?
Are there any verses or hadiths about Allah that you have deliberately set aside because they make you uncomfortable? This lesson demands that you do not ignore them but either understand them through sound interpretation or submit to their true meaning being known only to Allah. Which will you choose, and what might that require of you in terms of humility or effort?
?
The commentator emphasizes that the term 'text' here refers to evidence that is open to interpretation, not clear and unambiguous rulings. Have you ever mistakenly thought that any difficulty in understanding a divine attribute can be resolved by interpretation? This lesson challenges you to discern between texts that can be interpreted and those that must be entrusted. How will you prepare yourself to make that distinction, and are you willing to accept that some meanings are beyond human comprehension?

The Consensus on Ta'wil and the Meaning of 'Above'
States the agreement on interpreting anthropomorphic verses and gives the example of 'fawqiyya'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general consensus among Ahl al-Haqq on interpreting anthropomorphic verses and how the Khalaf interpret 'fawqiyya' as transcendence in majesty.

Core Idea
The Salaf consign the meaning to Allah, the Khalaf specify figurative meanings.

This passage establishes the foundational principle that any Qur'anic or hadith text that seems to attribute a direction or body to Allah must be interpreted figuratively, because Allah is absolutely transcendent. The commentator provides two examples; we will focus on the first: the verse about the angels fearing their Lord 'from above them.'

Guided Path
1
Read the principle statement
Read the opening sentence where the commentator states the consensus on ta'wil for verses that suggest direction, body, form, or limbs.
Note the exception: Mujassimah and Mushabbihah disagree.
2
Learn the first example
Examine the verse from Surah An-Nahl 50 and the two interpretations: Salaf say 'fawqiyya we do not know', Khalaf say 'transcendence in majesty'.
3
Understand the rationale
Reflect on why tanzih necessitates ta'wil: because Allah cannot be contained by direction.
This connects to the core idea of transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أنَّه إذا ورد في القرآن والسنة ما يُشعر بإثبات الجهة أو الجسميَّة أو الصورة أو الجوارح، اتفق أهل الحقِّ وغيرهم ما عدا المجسمة والمشبهة على تأويل ذلك؛ لوجوب تنزيهِهِ تعالى عما دلَّ عليه ما ذكر بحسب ظاهره:
فممَّا يوهم الجهة قوله تعالى: ﴿ يَخَافُونَ رَبَّهُم مِّن فَوْقِهِمْ ﴾ [النحل: ٥٠]، فالسلف يقولون فوقيَّة لا نعلمها (۱) ، والخلف يقولون : المراد بالفوقية التعالي في العظمة؛ فالمعنى يخافون؛ أي: الملائكة ربَّهم من أجل تعاليه في العظمة؛ أي ارتفاعه فيها.
ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى [طه، فالسلف يقولون: استواء لا نعلمه والخلف يقولون : المراد به الاستيلاء والمُلك كما قال الشاعر: [من الرجز]
قَدِ اسْتَوَى بِشَرٌ عَلَى العِرَاقِ مِنْ غَيْرِ سَيْفِ وَدَمِ مُهْرَاقِ
وسأل رجل الإمام مالكاً عن هذه الآية فأطرق رأسه مليا، ثم قال: الاستواء غير مجهول، والكيف غير معقول والإيمان به واجب، والسؤال عنه بدعة، وما أظنُّك إلا ضالا ، فأمر به فأخرج، وسأل الزمخشري الغزالي عن هذه الآية فأجابه بقوله : إذا استحال أن تعرف نفسك بكيفية أو أبنيَّة، فكيف يليقُ بعبوديتك أن تصفه تعالى به (أين) أو كيف)، وهو مقدَّس عن ذلك؟ ثم جعل يقول : [من الرمل]
قُلْ لِمَنْ يَفْهَمُ عَنِّي مَا أَقُولُ: قَصِّرِ القولَ فَذَا شَرْحُ يَطُول
ثَمَّ سِرُّ غَامِضٌ مِنْ دُونِهِ قَصُرَتْ واللهِ أَعْناقُ الفُحُولُ
The summary is that if there occurs in the Qur'an and Sunnah what suggests the affirmation of direction (jiha) or corporeality (jismiyya) or form (sura) or limbs (jawarih), then Ahl al-Haqq and others – except the Mujassimah (anthropomorphists) and Mushabbihah (assimilators) – agree on interpreting (ta'wil) that, because of the obligation to transcend (tanzih) Him, Most High, from what is indicated by what was mentioned according to its apparent meaning. Among what suggests direction is His saying, Most High: {They fear their Lord from above them} (An-Nahl 50). The Salaf say: a 'fawqiyya' (being above) that we do not know. And the Khalaf say: what is meant by 'fawqiyya' is transcendence in majesty; so the meaning is: they fear; i.e., the angels fear their Lord because of His transcendence in majesty; i.e., His elevation therein. And similar is His saying, Most High: {The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne} (Taha 5). The Salaf say: an 'istawa'' that we do not know. And the Khalaf say: what is meant by it is conquest and dominion, as the poet said (rajaz meter): 'Bishr has established himself over Iraq without sword and spilled blood.' And a man asked Imam Malik about this verse, so he bowed his head for a long time, then said: 'The istiwa' is not unknown, the modality is not conceivable, believing in it is obligatory, asking about it is an innovation, and I do not think you are anything but a misguided person.' So he ordered him to be taken out. And al-Zamakhshari asked al-Ghazali about this verse, and he answered him by saying: 'If it is impossible for you to know yourself by a modality or structure, then how befitting of your servitude is it that you describe Him, Most High, with 'where' or 'how'? And He is sanctified from that.' Then he began reciting (ramal meter): 'Say to one who understands from me what I say: cut short the speech, for this is a long explanation. Then there is a subtle mystery beyond which the necks of the mighty have fallen short, by Allah.'
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage summarizes the consensus among Ahl al-Haqq (with the exception of the Mujassimah and Mushabbihah) that any Quranic or hadith text which appears to affirm direction, body, form, or limbs must be interpreted figuratively (ta'wil) due to the necessity of tanzih (transcendence). Two key examples are provided. First, the verse 'They fear their Lord from above them' (Quran 16:50). The Salaf approach is to say 'fawqiyya (above-ness) that we do not know,' while the Khalaf interpret 'above' as transcendence in majesty. Second, the verse 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' (Quran 20:5). The Salaf say 'istawa' (establishing) that we do not know, while the Khalaf interpret it as 'istila'' (conquest) and mulk (dominion), citing the famous poetic line of Bishr. The passage then recounts two famous anecdotes. Imam Malik's response to a questioner: he affirmed that istiwa' is known (in meaning), the modality (kayf) is unknown, belief is obligatory, and asking about it is an innovation – and he had the questioner expelled. The second anecdote: al-Zamakhshari asked al-Ghazali about the same verse; al-Ghazali replied that it is impossible to know yourself by modality or structure, so how can you attribute 'where' or 'how' to Allah? He then recited two lines of poetry emphasizing the depth of the mystery. These examples illustrate both the Salaf's tafwid and the Khalaf's ta'wil while emphasizing tanzih.

Key Terms
الجهة
الجسمية
الصورة
الجوارح
التنزيه
التأويل
الفوقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Salaf reject all interpretation and read the verses literally.
Correction: The Salaf affirm the attribute but consign the modality to Allah; they do not take a literal spatial meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which two groups do NOT agree with Ahl al-Haqq on ta'wil?
A) The Salaf and the Khalaf
B) The Mujassimah and the Mushabbihah
C) The Ash'aris and the Maturidis
D) The Sufis and the philosophers

Key Takeaway
The Salaf and Khalaf agree that apparent direction must be interpreted to preserve tanzih, differing only in specifying the figurative meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'They fear their Lord from above them,' do you feel a subtle inclination to imagine a spatial direction for Allah? What does this lesson demand you to do with that inclination?
?
The Salaf and Khalaf both affirm tanzih, but differ in explicitness. Which approach resonates more with your current state of faith? Are you willing to submit to the way that guards Allah's transcendence most rigorously, even if it feels abstract?
?
The Mujassimah are excluded from Ahl al-Haqq for taking literal meanings. Have you ever heard a Muslim speak of Allah in a way that implies a body or location? What will you do when you encounter such speech? Will you correct with knowledge and kindness, or remain silent?

The Interpretation of 'Istiwa' and the Position of the Salaf
Gives the example of 'istiwa' and relates responses of Malik and Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the interpretation of the verse about Allah's istiwa' over the Throne and the famous responses of Imam Malik and al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Istiwa' is known, its modality is unknown, and asking about it is innovation.

This passage continues with the second example: the verse 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne.' It contrasts the Salaf's approach of tafwid (consigning the meaning to Allah) with the Khalaf's interpretation as 'istila' (conquest). The commentator then relates two powerful anecdotes that illustrate the proper attitude: Imam Malik's firm response and al-Ghazali's poetic reflection.

Guided Path
1
Examine the second example
Read the verse from Surah Taha 20:5 and the two interpretations by Salaf and Khalaf.
The Khalaf use a line of poetry to support the meaning of 'istila'.
2
Study Imam Malik's response
Read the story of the man who asked about istiwa', and note Malik's four-part answer.
He said: 'Istiwa' is not unknown, the modality is not conceivable, belief is obligatory, asking about it is innovation.'
3
Study al-Ghazali's response
Read al-Ghazali's analogy: if you cannot know yourself by modality or structure, how can you describe Allah with 'where' or 'how'?
He ends with poetry emphasizing the mystery.
4
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on the common thread: both Salaf and Khalaf uphold tanzih, but the Salaf stop at tafwid while the Khalaf offer interpretations.
Both approaches are valid within Sunni orthodoxy.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Salaf did not interpret the verse at all and just said 'we don't know the meaning.'
Correction: The Salaf affirmed the meaning of istiwa' in the Arabic language but consigned the modality (how) to Allah.

Checkpoint
What did Imam Malik say about asking about the kayf (modality) of istiwa'?
A) It is obligatory
B) It is recommended
C) It is an innovation
D) It is permissible

Key Takeaway
The proper stance regarding Allah's attributes is to affirm the meaning without inquiring into the modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the verse 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne', does your mind automatically form a mental image? What does this lesson teach you about that instinct, and will you now discipline yourself to say 'I believe in it without knowing how'?
?
Imam Malik called the questioner misguided and ordered him out. Have you ever asked a question about Allah that, looking back, was inappropriate? How can you cultivate the adab (etiquette) of not asking about matters beyond your comprehension?
?
Al-Ghazali points out that you cannot even know your own soul's 'how'. Think of the deepest mystery about your own existence that you cannot explain. How does that humble you? Now apply that to Allah's attributes—does your heart finally submit to the mystery? What will you do when you are tempted to ask 'how' again?

The General Rule for Interpreting Ambiguous Verses on Divine Attributes
States the rule and two examples with Salaf and Khalaf views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni orthodox rule for interpreting Qur'anic verses that seem to attribute direction or body to Allah, and the difference between the Salaf and Khalaf approaches.

Core Idea
All Muslims agree on divine transcendence; they only differ on whether to interpret ambiguous verses verbally.

When we read verses like 'the Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne', we must uphold Allah's transcendence. The scholars have two main approaches: tafwid (consigning the meaning to Allah) and ta'wil (interpretation). This passage from Sanusi lays out the rule and gives examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the rule
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the opening statement that sets the rule for any verse or hadith that suggests direction, body, etc.
The rule is that ta'wil is necessary to preserve transcendence.
2
Examine the first example: 'above them'
The verse 'They fear their Lord above them' is given. Understand how the Salaf and Khalaf each explain 'above-ness'.
Salaf: 'above-ness we do not know'; Khalaf: 'exaltedness in majesty'.
3
Examine the second example: 'istawa'
The verse 'al-Rahman 'ala al-'arsh istawa' is given. Compare the Salaf saying 'istawa we do not know' with the Khalaf interpreting it as 'domination', supported by poetry.
Note that both groups affirm transcendence.
4
Reflect on the unity of purpose
Ask yourself: do the Salaf and Khalaf disagree on the fundamental truth? The passage shows they agree on the necessity of transcendence, differing only in method.
This prevents misunderstanding that they hold opposing beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أنَّه إذا ورد في القرآن والسنة ما يُشعر بإثبات الجهة أو الجسميَّة أو الصورة أو الجوارح، اتفق أهل الحقِّ وغيرهم ما عدا المجسمة والمشبهة على تأويل ذلك؛ لوجوب تنزيهِهِ تعالى عما دلَّ عليه ما ذكر بحسب ظاهره: فممَّا يوهم الجهة قوله تعالى: ﴿ يَخَافُونَ رَبَّهُم مِّن فَوْقِهِمْ ﴾ [النحل: ٥٠]، فالسلف يقولون فوقيَّة لا نعلمها (۱) ، والخلف يقولون : المراد بالفوقية التعالي في العظمة؛ فالمعنى يخافون؛ أي: الملائكة ربَّهم من أجل تعاليه في العظمة؛ أي ارتفاعه فيها. ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى [طه، فالسلف يقولون: استواء لا نعلمه والخلف يقولون : المراد به الاستيلاء والمُلك كما قال الشاعر: [من الرجز] قَدِ اسْتَوَى بِشَرٌ عَلَى العِرَاقِ مِنْ غَيْرِ سَيْفِ وَدَمِ مُهْرَاقِ
The conclusion: When something in the Qur'an or Sunnah suggests affirming direction, corporeality, form, or limbs, the people of truth and others except for the mujassima and mushabbiha agree on its ta'wil, because it is obligatory to declare Him transcendent above what the apparent meaning indicates. Among what suggests direction is His saying: 'They fear their Lord above them' (al-Nahl 50). The Salaf say: a 'above-ness' we do not know. The Khalaf say: what is meant by 'above-ness' is exaltedness in majesty; so the meaning is: they fear, i.e., the angels, their Lord because of His exaltedness in majesty, i.e., His loftiness therein. And from that is His saying: 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' (Taha). The Salaf say: an establishment we do not know. The Khalaf say: what is meant by it is domination and sovereignty, as the poet said (in rajaz meter): 'Bishr has established himself over Iraq without sword or bloodshed.'
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents the general methodological rule for interpreting Qur'anic and prophetic texts that apparently attribute direction, corporeality, form, or limbs to God. The commentator states that all Muslims except the anthropomorphists (mujassima and mushabbiha) agree on the necessity of ta'wil (interpretation) because God must be declared transcendent above what the literal sense implies. He then gives two concrete examples. The first is the verse 'They fear their Lord above them' (al-Nahl 50). Here the Salaf (early Muslims) say: 'a 'above-ness' we do not know,' meaning they consign the modality to God without interpreting the word. The Khalaf (later scholars) go further and interpret 'above-ness' as exaltedness in majesty. The second example is the verse 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' (Taha). Again, the Salaf say: 'an establishment we do not know,' while the Khalaf interpret it as domination (istila') and sovereignty (mulk), citing a line of poetry where 'istawa' is used in the sense of 'dominated' without implying physical sitting. This illustrates the two broad approaches: tafwid (consignment) and ta'wil (interpretation), both aimed at preserving divine transcendence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Salaf and Khalaf disagree on the fundamental meaning of the verses.
Correction: They agree on divine transcendence; they only differ on whether to use verbal interpretation or consign the meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the Salaf say about the verse 'al-Rahman 'ala al-'arsh istawa'?
A) They interpret istawa as istila' (domination).
B) They say 'istawa la na'lamuhu' (an establishment we do not know).
C) They affirm that God literally sits on the Throne.
D) They refuse to discuss it.

Key Takeaway
The orthodox approach to ambiguous verses is to affirm transcendence while either consigning the meaning to Allah (Salaf) or interpreting it in a way befitting His majesty (Khalaf).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne,' does your mind instinctively picture God in a place or on a throne? This lesson warns against that. Are you willing to train your heart to say 'I do not know the how' when your intellect demands an image? What concrete step will you take this week to purify your belief about God's transcendence?
?
The Salaf and Khalaf both agree that God is exalted above any imperfection, yet they differ on whether to give a verbal interpretation. Which approach do you find more spiritually secure—leaving the meaning to God, or offering an interpretation with linguistic evidence? Examine your own desire for certainty: can you trust God enough to say 'I do not know,' or do you need an explanation to feel safe in your faith?
?

But more than an argument, do you actually live with the conviction that God is utterly unlike anything—including being above? What would change in your daily worship if you truly felt His absolute transcendence?

Ghazali's Argument from Self-Ignorance for Divine Transcendence
Ghazali answers a question about istiwa using self-ignorance and a poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses human self-ignorance to argue that asking 'how' or 'where' about Allah is improper.

Core Idea
If you cannot know yourself, do not ask how Allah is.

Al-Zamakhshari once asked al-Ghazali about the verse of istiwa. Ghazali's reply is a powerful reminder of humility before Allah. He points out that we cannot even fully understand our own bodies and spirits, so how can we presume to ask 'where' or 'how' about Allah? This lesson presents his argument and his famous poem on divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow from the opening statement to the poetic examples.
The poem uses concrete imagery from everyday life.
2
Identify Ghazali's main argument
Notice the opening sentence: if you cannot know yourself with a 'how' or structure, how can you describe Allah with 'where' or 'how'? This is an analogy from human ignorance.
Self-knowledge is the starting point.
3
Examine the poem's examples
The poem lists things we cannot fully understand: the spirit, breaths, digestion, sleep, and intellect. Each example shows our limits.
These are all within us, yet mysterious.
4
Understand the conclusion of tanzih
The poem ends by affirming that Allah has no 'where' or 'how', He is above all categories, and He is present in all directions without spatial change.
This is the core of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسأل الزمخشري الغزالي عن هذه الآية فأجابه بقوله : إذا استحال أن تعرف نفسك بكيفية أو أبنيَّة، فكيف يليقُ بعبوديتك أن تصفه تعالى به (أين) أو كيف)، وهو مقدَّس عن ذلك؟ ثم جعل يقول : [من الرمل] قُلْ لِمَنْ يَفْهَمُ عَنِّي مَا أَقُولُ: قَصِّرِ القولَ فَذَا شَرْحُ يَطُولُ ثَمَّ سِرُّ غَامِضٌ مِنْ دُونِهِ قَصُرَتْ وَاللهِ أَعْناقُ الفُحُولُ أَنْتَ لَا تَعْرِفُ إِيَّاكَ وَلَا تَدْر مَنْ أَنْتَ وَلَا كَيْفَ الوُصُول لَا وَلَا تَدْرِي صِفَاتٍ رُكْبَتْ فِيكَ حَارَتْ فِي خَفَايَاهَا العُقُولُ أَيْنَ مِنْكَ الرُّوحُ فِي جَوْهَرِها؟ هَلْ تَرَاها فَتَرى كَيْفَ تَجُولُ؟ وَكَذَا الأَنْفَاسُ هَلْ تَحْصُرُهَا؟ لا ولا تَدْرِي مَتَى عَنْكَ تَزُولُ أَيْنَ مِنْكَ العَقِلُ والفَهْمُ إذاً؟ غَلَبَ النَّوْمُ فَقُلْ لِيْ يَا جَهُول أَنْتَ أَكْلَ الخُبُزِ لَا تَعْرِفُهُ كَيْفَ يَجْرِي مِنْكَ أَمْ كَيْفَ تَبُولُ فَإِذَا كَانَتْ طَوَابَاكَ الَّتِي بَيْنَ جَنْبَيْكَ كَذَا فِيْها ضَلُول كَيْفَ تَدْرِي مَنْ عَلَى العَرْشِ اسْتَوَى؟ لَا تَقُلْ: كَيْفَ اسْتَوَى كَيْفَ النُّزُولُ كَيْفَ يُحْكَى الرَّبُّ أَمْ كَيْفَ يُرَى؟ فَلَعَمْرِي لَيْسَ ذَا إِلَّا فُضُول فَهُوَ لَا أَيْنَ وَلَا كَيْفَ لَهُ وَهْوَ رَبُّ الكَيْفِ وَالكَيْفُ يَحُولُ وَهُوَ فَوْقَ الفَوْقِ لَا فَوْقَ لَهُ وَهُوَ فِي كُلِّ النَّوَاحِي لَا يَزُولُ جَلَّ ذَاتاً وَصِفَاتٍ وَسَمَا وَتَعَالَى قَدْرُهُ عَمَّا تَقُولُ
And al-Zamakhshari asked al-Ghazali about this verse, and he answered him by saying: 'If it is impossible for you to know yourself with a 'how' or a structure, then how is it fitting for your servanthood that you describe Him, exalted, with 'where' or 'how'? He is sanctified above that.' Then he began to say (in ramal meter): 'Say to one who understands what I say: short speech, for this explanation would be long. Then there is a hidden mystery beyond which the necks of the great have fallen short. You do not know yourself, nor do you know who you are, nor how to reach, nor do you know the qualities assembled in you; minds are perplexed by their hidden aspects. Where from you is the spirit in its substance? Do you see it so as to see how it moves? Likewise the breaths: can you count them? No, nor do you know when they will depart from you. Where from you are intellect and understanding then? Sleep overcomes, so tell me, O ignorant one: You do not know the eating of bread, how it proceeds from you or how you urinate. If your innermost parts that are between your sides are so confused in them, how do you know the One who established Himself over the Throne? Do not say: 'How did He establish?', 'How does He descend?', 'How is the Lord described?', 'How is He seen?' For by my life, this is nothing but meddlesomeness. He has no 'where' and no 'how', and He is the Lord of 'how', and 'how' changes. He is above the 'above' with no 'above' for Him, and He is in all directions without ceasing. Glorified is He in essence and attributes, exalted, and high above what they say.'
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage recounts an exchange between al-Zamakhshari and al-Ghazali concerning the verse of istiwa. Ghazali's answer is a powerful argument from analogy: if a person cannot fully comprehend their own self—its nature, its spirit, its breath, its inner workings—how can they presume to ask 'where' or 'how' about God? He then recites a lengthy poem that elaborates on human ignorance of the self. The poem uses concrete examples: the spirit, breath, digestion, and sleep to show that even the most basic bodily functions are mysterious. If such basic things are beyond full understanding, then it is pure meddlesomeness (fudul) to ask about God's istiwa or other attributes. The poem concludes with tanzih: God transcends 'where' and 'how', He is the Lord of 'how' while all 'how' changes, He is above all relative 'above', and He is present in all directions without spatial change. The passage is a strong defense of tafwid and tanzih, discouraging speculative questions about divine attributes.

Key Terms
الاستواء
أين
كيف
التنزيه
الفضول
العبودية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali's poem implies God is literally present in all directions (panentheism).
Correction: The poem says 'He is in all directions without ceasing' as a negation of absence, not as spatial indwelling. It affirms His encompassing power and knowledge, not physical presence.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument Ghazali uses in his response to al-Zamakhshari?
A) That the verse of istiwa is unclear and cannot be interpreted.
B) That one cannot know one's own self, so one cannot ask 'how' about God.
C) That istiwa means sitting on the Throne literally.
D) That only scholars should discuss these matters.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's argument uses our inability to fully know our own selves to show that it is improper to ask 'how' or 'where' about Allah, affirming His absolute transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that you cannot fully know your own self—how it functions, where the spirit is. Yet when you think about Allah, do you still find yourself silently demanding an answer to 'how'? What does that demand reveal about your sense of entitlement before your Creator? How could you turn that demand into a prostration of surrender?
?
Ghazali calls asking 'how' about Allah 'fudul' (meddlesomeness). When you study theology, do you approach it as a humble servant seeking to worship better, or as a curious mind trying to satisfy intellectual pride? How would your next dua change if you truly accepted that you have no right to ask 'how'?
?
You have examples from your own body: the breath you cannot stop, the sleep that overcomes your intellect. Which of these do you experience daily but never meditate on? Could this neglect of your own ignorance be the very thing that makes you demand knowledge of Allah's 'how'? What would it mean for you to live today with the awareness that you do not even know yourself?

The Futility of Asking 'How' about Allah
Poem arguing against anthropomorphic questioning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the poet argues that asking 'how' about Allah's attributes is frivolous when we cannot even understand our own existence.

Core Idea
Your ignorance of yourself proves you cannot question how Allah is.

This poem uses a powerful argument: if you don't fully understand your own spirit, breath, or digestion, how can you demand to know how Allah is established on the Throne? The poet calls such questions 'frivolity' and then affirms Allah's utter transcendence beyond place and modality.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Let the poetic rhythm and argument sink in before analyzing.
Notice the recurring rhetorical questions.
2
Identify the argument from self-ignorance
The poet lists things you don't know about yourself: the spirit, breaths, digestion. Ask yourself: why does he start with those?
This is the foundation of his refutation.
3
Find the warning against frivolous questions
After the self-ignorance list, the poet explicitly forbids asking 'how' about Allah's establishment, descent, seeing, etc. Look for the word 'فضول'.
The poet considers such questions a waste.
4
Understand the positive statements about Allah
The poem then affirms that Allah has no 'where' or 'how', and He is the Lord of 'how'. Notice how these statements protect divine transcendence.
This is a crucial Ash'ari position.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The poem ends with exalting Allah's essence and attributes above all speech. Consider how this connects to the beginning: silence in the face of mystery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَنْتَ لَا تَعْرِفُ إِيَّاكَ وَلَا تَدْر مَنْ أَنْتَ وَلَا كَيْفَ الوُصُول لَا وَلَا تَدْرِي صِفَاتٍ رُكْبَتْ فِيكَ حَارَتْ فِي خَفَايَاهَا العُقُولُ أَيْنَ مِنْكَ الرُّوحُ فِي جَوْهَرِها؟ هَلْ تَرَاها فَتَرى كَيْفَ تَجُولُ؟ وَكَذَا الأَنْفَاسُ هَلْ تَحْصُرُهَا؟ لا ولا تَدْرِي مَتَى عَنْكَ تَزُولُ أَيْنَ مِنْكَ العَقِلُ والفَهْمُ إذاً؟ غَلَبَ النَّوْمُ فَقُلْ لِيْ يَا جَهُول أَنْتَ أَكْلَ الخُبُزِ لَا تَعْرِفُهُ كَيْفَ يَجْرِي مِنْكَ أَمْ كَيْفَ تَبُولُ فَإِذَا كَانَتْ طَوَابَاكَ الَّتِي بَيْنَ جَنْبَيْكَ كَذَا فِيْها ضَلُول كَيْفَ تَدْرِي مَنْ عَلَى العَرْشِ اسْتَوَى؟ لَا تَقُلْ: كَيْفَ اسْتَوَى كَيْفَ النُّزُولُ كَيْفَ يُحْكَى الرَّبُّ أَمْ كَيْفَ يُرَى؟ فَلَعَمْرِي لَيْسَ ذَا إِلَّا فُضُول فَهُوَ لَا أَيْنَ وَلَا كَيْفَ لَهُ وَهْوَ رَبُّ الكَيْفِ وَالكَيْفُ يَحُولُ وَهُوَ فَوْقَ الفَوْقِ لَا فَوْقَ لَهُ وَهُوَ فِي كُلِّ النَّوَاحِي لَا يَزُولُ جَلَّ ذَاتاً وَصِفَاتٍ وَسَمَا وَتَعَالَى قَدْرُهُ عَمَّا تَقُولُ
You do not know yourself, nor do you know who you are, nor how to reach [knowledge]. Nor do you know the attributes composed in you; minds are bewildered in their hidden aspects. Where is the spirit in its essence? Do you see it so that you see how it roams? Likewise the breaths: can you count them? No, nor do you know when they will depart from you. Where then is the intellect and understanding? Sleep overpowers [you]; so tell me, O ignorant one: You do not know the eating of bread—how it circulates in you or how you urinate. When your inner organs that are between your sides are thus—with such bewilderment regarding them—how can you know who is established on the Throne? Do not say: 'How is He established? How does He descend? How is the Lord described? Or how is He seen?' By my life, this is nothing but frivolity. For He has no 'where' and no 'how', and He is the Lord of 'how', and 'how' changes. He is above the above, no above for Him, and He is in all directions without ceasing. His essence and attributes are exalted, and He is high, and His measure is far above what you say.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a poem that uses the argument from ignorance of self to silence anthropomorphic questions. It begins by pointing out that a person does not fully know his own spirit, breaths, or bodily processes. If such basic internal phenomena are mysterious, then asking how Allah is established on the Throne or how He descends is futile—the poet calls it 'فضول' (frivolity). The poem then makes positive statements about Allah: He transcends place (لا أين) and modality (لا كيف), and He is the Lord of 'how' (رب الكيف). He is above all without spatial limitation, and He encompasses all directions without change. It concludes by declaring His essence and attributes exalted above all human speech. The tone is exhortative, warning against speculative questions.

Key Terms
استوى
كيف
أين
فضول
رب الكيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think it is permissible to ask 'how' about Allah's attributes such as His establishment on the Throne or His descent.
Correction: The poem clearly labels such questions as 'فضول' (frivolity) because we cannot even understand our own inner realities. Allah transcends 'how' and 'where'.

Checkpoint

A) Wisdom.
B) Frivolity (فضول).
C) Obligatory.
D) Recommended.

Key Takeaway
The poem uses our ignorance of our own selves to demonstrate that asking 'how' about Allah is both presumptuous and futile; Allah is beyond all modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear verses like 'الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى', does a question about 'how' arise in your mind? What does this poem reveal about the arrogance of that question? Commit to replacing that curiosity with the words 'آمنا به' the next time you read such a verse.
?
The poet says you don't even understand how bread digests in your own body. Identify one area of your life where you are demanding to understand 'how' Allah will work before you obey. Will you choose obedience today despite not knowing the mechanism?
?

Affirming Divine Acts Without How: Salaf and Khalaf on Coming and Descent
Verse 'Your Lord came' and hadith of descent with two interpretations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Salaf and Khalaf approach verses and hadith that might imply corporeality, and the principles of tafwid and ta'wil.

Core Idea
Affirming God's actions without knowing how is the path of the Salaf.

This lesson examines two scriptural texts that, taken literally, might suggest Allah has a body. The early Muslims (Salaf) affirmed the words but refrained from explaining how. Later theologians (Khalaf) interpreted them metaphorically. By studying both approaches, you will learn how Muslim scholars safeguard divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the two texts: the verse and the hadith. Pay attention to the language used.
The passage ends abruptly with (هل ... إلخ) indicating a cut-off, but the key positions are clear.
2
Identify the two scriptural texts
Locate the verse from Surah Al-Fajr (89:22) and the well-known hadith of descent. Understand why these might suggest corporeality to some.
The verse says 'And your Lord came' and the hadith says 'Our Lord descends.'
3
Compare the Salaf and Khalaf stances
The Salaf say 'we do not know their modality' (لا نعلمهما), affirming the act but consigning the how to Allah. The Khalaf interpret the coming as the coming of Allah's command and the descent as the descent of an angel.
The Khalaf approach is metaphorical (ta'wil) while the Salaf approach is consignment (tafwid).
4
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider why both groups aim to protect Allah from corporeality. The Salaf avoid interpreting, while the Khalaf reinterpret. Both are valid methodologies in Sunni Islam.
This shows the flexibility of Islamic theology in dealing with ambiguous texts.

Source Text

وممَّا يُوهم الجسميَّة قوله تعالى: ﴿وَجَاءَ رَبُّكَ﴾ [الفجر: (۲۲)]، وحديث الصحيحين: ينزل ربُّنا كلَّ ليلةٍ إلى سماء الدنيا، حيث يبقى ثلث الليل الأخير، ويقولُ: مَنْ يدعوني فأستجيب له مَنْ يسألني فأعطيه مَنْ يستغفرني فأغفر له، فالسلف يقولون مجيء ونزول لا نعلمهما والخلف يقولون المراد: وجاء عذاب ربِّك أو أمرُ ربِّك الشامل للعذاب، والمراد ينزلُ ملكُ ربِّنا فيقول عن الله (هل ... إلخ).
And from what suggests corporeality is Allah's saying: 'And your Lord came' [Al-Fajr: 22], and the hadith of the two Sahihs: 'Our Lord descends every night to the heaven of this world, when the last third of the night remains, and says: Who calls upon Me so that I answer him? Who asks Me so that I give him? Who seeks My forgiveness so that I forgive him?' So the Salaf say: 'coming' and 'descending' - we do not know their modality, and the Khalaf say: what is meant is the command of your Lord came, or the command of your Lord inclusive of punishment came, and what is meant is that the angel of our Lord descends and says on behalf of Allah (such and such... etc.).
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage addresses two scriptural texts that might imply corporeality: the verse 'And your Lord came' and the hadith of Allah's descent. The author presents two approaches: the Salaf (early Muslims) affirm the literal wording but disclaim knowledge of how (لا نعلمهما), while the Khalaf (later theologians) interpret them metaphorically—'coming' as the coming of Allah's command or punishment, and 'descending' as the descent of an angel who speaks on Allah's behalf. This illustrates the principle of tafwid (consigning modality to Allah) versus ta'wil (interpretive metaphor).

Interpreting the Hadith of Adam's Creation in Allah's Image
Discusses the hadith of Adam created in Allah's image with Salaf and Khalaf interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two interpretive approaches to the hadith 'Allah created Adam in His image' and why neither implies corporeality.

Core Idea
The 'image' in the hadith is either an unknowable form or a reference to attributes, not a physical likeness.

This passage tackles another potential misconception about Allah's nature: the famous hadith that Adam was created in Allah's image. Different scholars have interpreted this in ways that protect divine transcendence. The author presents both the Salaf and Khalaf views and discusses the important question of the pronoun referent.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith in context
Start by reading the hadith as narrated by Ahmad, Bukhari, and Muslim about a man striking his slave. The Prophet's response includes the phrase 'Allah created Adam in His image.'
This hadith is sometimes misunderstood to imply anthropomorphism.
2
Understand the Salaf position
The Salaf (early scholars) say: 'a form we do not know it.' They affirm the wording without interpreting the modality, leaving the meaning to Allah.
This is the safer approach according to many traditionalists.
3
Understand the Khalaf interpretation
The Khalaf (later scholars) interpret 'image' as the attribute (الصفة) of hearing, sight, knowledge, and life. Adam is created with these attributes in a general sense, though they are temporal.
This interpretation avoids corporealism by explaining 'image' as a set of qualities.
4
Examine the pronoun referent debate
The pronoun 'His' in 'His image' can refer to Allah or to the brother (the person being struck). The Khalaf prefer referring to Allah; others point to a variant that says 'image of the Most Merciful' or to the version with 'his brother's image.'
The referent affects the meaning: if to Allah, it is about divine attributes; if to the brother, it is about human dignity.
5
Reflect on the ethical implication
If the image is that of the brother, the hadith teaches respect for the human face. The author concludes that one should avoid striking the face out of respect.
This shows how theological interpretation can have practical ethical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يوهم الجسمية الصورة ما رواه أحمد والشيخان أن رجلاً ضرب عبده فنهاه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وقال: إنَّ الله تعالى خلق آدم على صورتِهِ. فالسلفُ يقولون: صورة لا نعلمها والخلف يقولون: المراد بالصورة الصفة من سمع وبصر وعلم وحياة، فهو على صفته في الجملة، وإن كانت صفته تعالى قديمةً وصفة الإنسان حادثة، وهذا بناء على أن الضمير في صورته عائد على الله تعالى، كما يقتضيه ما ورد في بعض الطرق: فإنَّ الله خلق آدم على صورة الرحمن»، وبعضهم جعل الضمير عائداً على الأخ المصرح به في الطريق التي رواها مسلم بلفظ: «فإذا قاتل أحدكم أخاه فليجتنب الوجة، فإن الله خلق آدم على صورته»، وإذا كانت كذلك فينبغي احترامه باتقاء الوجه.
And from what suggests corporeality is the 'form' narrated by Ahmad and the two Shaykhs [Bukhari and Muslim] that a man struck his slave, so the Prophet ﷺ forbade him and said: 'Verily Allah created Adam in His image.' So the Salaf say: a form we do not know it, and the Khalaf say: what is meant by the form is the attribute of hearing, sight, knowledge, and life, so he is upon His attribute in general, even if His attribute is eternal and the human attribute is temporal. And this is based on the pronoun in 'His image' referring to Allah, as is required by what is narrated in some versions: 'Verily Allah created Adam in the image of the Most Merciful.' And some made the pronoun refer to the brother mentioned in the version narrated by Muslim: 'If one of you fights his brother, let him avoid the face, for Allah created Adam in his image.' And if that is so, then one should respect him by avoiding the face.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents another text that might imply corporeality: the hadith 'Allah created Adam in His image.' The author again contrasts the Salaf approach (affirming 'form' without knowing how) with the Khalaf approach (interpreting 'image' as a composite of attributes like hearing, sight, knowledge, life, so that Adam is created with those attributes in a general sense, albeit created and temporal versus Allah's eternal attributes). The key debate is the referent of the pronoun 'His' in 'His image'—whether it refers to Allah or to the person being struck (the brother). The Khalaf interpretation that it refers to Allah avoids corporealism by interpreting 'image' as attributes. The author also notes that some scholars interpret it as the brother's image, which yields an ethical lesson: respect for the human face.

Misconception: The hadith 'Allah created Adam in His image' means Allah has a physical form like a human.
Correction: Scholars interpret the 'image' either as an unknowable form (Salaf) or as attributes like hearing and sight (Khalaf), avoiding corporealism.

The hadith about Adam's creation does not imply corporeality; both Salaf and Khalaf interpretations safeguard Allah's transcendence.

The Salaf and Khalaf on Divine Coming and Descending
The author presents the verse and hadith, then the Salaf and Khalaf interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Salaf and Khalaf approach scriptural texts that seem to imply corporeality, specifically regarding the coming and descending of Allah.

Core Idea
The Salaf affirm without how; the Khalaf interpret metaphorically.

This lesson examines two key scriptural passages that might suggest God has a body: the verse about His 'coming' and the hadith about His 'descending'. We'll see how the early pious ancestors (Salaf) and later scholars (Khalaf) each handle these texts, revealing a fundamental difference in interpretive methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully
Pay attention to the two scriptural citations and the two responses from the Salaf and Khalaf.
The Arabic uses technical terms like مجيء and نزول.
2
Identify the Salaf and Khalaf positions
Notice that the Salaf say 'we do not know how' (تفويض) while the Khalaf offer specific metaphorical interpretations (تأويل).
The Khalaf interpret 'coming' as punishment or command, and 'descent' as an angel's descent.
3
Reflect on the difference in methodology
Consider why the Salaf chose to remain silent on the modality while the Khalaf felt the need to reinterpret. What does this tell us about their theological priorities?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وممَّا يُوهم الجسميَّة قوله تعالى: ﴿وَجَاءَ رَبُّكَ ﴾ [الفجر: (۲۲)، وحديث الصحيحين: ينزل ربُّنا كلَّ ليلةٍ إلى سماء الدنيا، حيث يبقى ثلث الليل الأخير، ويقولُ: مَنْ يدعوني فأستجيب له مَنْ يسألني فأعطيه مَنْ يستغفرني فأغفر له ) ، فالسلف يقولون مجيء ونزول لا نعلمهما والخلف يقولون المراد: وجاء عذاب ربِّك أو أمرُ : ربِّك الشامل للعذاب، والمراد ينزلُ ملكُ ربِّنا فيقول : عن الله (هل ... إلخ). وفي المنن»: أن الغالب أن الموكب الإلهي ينصبُ من الثلث الأخير، وتارةً ينصب من أول النصف الثاني إلا ليلة الجمعة فإنه ينصبُ من غروب الشمس إلى خروج الإمام من صلاة الصبح كما ورد في حديث مسلم.
And among what suggests corporeality is His saying, Exalted is He, 'And your Lord comes' [al-Fajr 22], and the hadith of the two Sahihs: 'Our Lord descends every night to the heaven of the world, when the last third of the night remains, and says: Who calls upon Me that I may answer him? Who asks of Me that I may give him? Who seeks forgiveness from Me that I may forgive him?' So the Salaf say: coming and descending — we do not know how they are. And the Khalaf say: what is meant is 'and the punishment of your Lord came' or 'the command of your Lord encompassing punishment', and what is meant by descent is the descent of an angel of our Lord who says on behalf of God (who... etc.). And in 'al-Munan' [?]: that most often the divine retinue descends in the last third, and sometimes descends from the beginning of the second half, except on Friday night when it descends from sunset until the imam exits from the morning prayer, as is related in the hadith of Muslim.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage addresses scriptural texts that seem to imply corporeality for God. The author cites two examples: the verse 'And your Lord comes' and the well-known hadith of divine descent. He then presents two methodologies: the Salaf (early pious ancestors) who affirm the reality of these attributes without asking how (tafwid), and the Khalaf (later scholars) who interpret them metaphorically (ta'wil). For the coming, the Khalaf interpret it as the coming of God's punishment or command. For the descent, they interpret it as the descent of an angel who speaks on God's behalf. The passage also mentions an additional opinion about the timing of the divine descent, citing a hadith from Muslim. The author does not prefer one view over the other here but merely reports them.

Interpreting the Hadith: Created in God's Image
The author explains Salaf and Khalaf views on the hadith that Adam was created in God's image.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main approaches of the Salaf and Khalaf in interpreting the hadith of Adam being created in God's image, and the implications for divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The hadith about God's image does not mean a physical likeness; scholars offer nuanced interpretations.

This passage addresses a hadith that might seem to imply God has a human-like form. Classical Sunni scholars have carefully interpreted it to safeguard divine transcendence. The Salaf accept the wording without delving into how, while the Khalaf provide specific interpretations, including that 'image' refers to attributes or even to the human brother. Let's explore their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its context
Read the Arabic passage once to get familiar with the wording. Notice the potential ambiguity in the phrase 'His image'.
The hadith is introduced as something that might suggest corporeality (tashbih).
2
Understand the Salaf position
The Salaf say: 'an image, we do not know it.' This is a classic Salaf approach of affirmation without modality (tafwid).
3
Learn the Khalaf interpretations
The Khalaf offer two interpretations: (1) 'image' means God's attributes (hearing, sight, etc.), (2) the pronoun 'his' refers to the brother (based on another version of the hadith).
The first interpretation preserves divine transcendence by denying a physical image; the second has an ethical conclusion.
4
Reflect on the ethical implication
If the pronoun refers to the brother, then one must respect the face and avoid striking it during fights. This shows how theological interpretation can guide behavior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يوهم وقال : إنَّ الله تعالى خلق آدم على صورتِهِ » (٢) . فالسلفُ يقولون : صورة لا نعلمها والخلف يقولون : المراد بالصورة الصفة من سمع وبصر وعلم وحياة، فهو على صفته في الجملة، وإن كانت صفته تعالى قديمةً وصفة الإنسان حادثة، وهذا بناء على أن الضمير في صورته عائد على الله تعالى، كما يقتضيه ما ورد في بعض الطرق : فإنَّ الله خلق آدم على صورة الرحمن»، وبعضهم جعل الضمير عائداً على الأخ المصرح به في الطريق التي رواها مسلم بلفظ : «فإذا قاتل أحدكم أخاه فليجتنب الوجة، فإن الله خلق آدم على صورته»، وإذا كانت كذلك فينبغي احترامه باتقاء الوجه .
And among what suggests [corporeality] is His saying: 'Indeed God created Adam in His image' (2). So the Salaf say: an image — we do not know it. And the Khalaf say: what is meant by image is the attribute of hearing, sight, knowledge, and life; so he (Adam) is upon His attribute in general, even though His attribute is eternal and the attribute of man is created. And this is based on the pronoun in 'His image' referring to God Almighty, as is required by what is narrated in some versions: 'Indeed God created Adam in the image of the Most Merciful.' And some made the pronoun refer to the brother mentioned in the version narrated by Muslim with the wording: 'When one of you fights his brother, let him avoid the face, for God created Adam in his image.' And if that is the case, then he should be respected by avoiding the face.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage deals with the famous hadith that God created Adam in His image (suratihi). The Salaf affirm the existence of an image without specifying how. The Khalaf offer two interpretations: first, that 'image' means the attributes of hearing, sight, knowledge, and life — so Adam was created with these attributes in a general sense, though God's attributes are eternal and man's are temporal. Second, some scholars interpret the pronoun in 'his image' as referring not to God but to the person being struck (i.e., the brother), based on a version of the hadith that says 'let him avoid the face, for God created Adam in his image' — meaning the image of that brother. This interpretation leads to an ethical conclusion: one should respect the face of others because it is created in the image of the person themselves.
