Misconception: The hadith necessarily implies God has a physical image.
Correction: Both the Salaf and Khalaf interpretations avoid this. The Salaf affirm the wording without modality, and the Khalaf interpret 'image' as attributes or refer the pronoun to the brother.

B) The Prophet

Key Takeaway
The hadith of Adam being created in God's image is interpreted by scholars to affirm divine transcendence, either by accepting the wording without explanation or by understanding 'image' as attributes or as referring to the human person.

Salaf and Khalaf on Allah's Hand and Fingers
The author explains the salaf and khalaf views on Allah's hand and fingers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Salaf and Khalaf approaches to interpreting anthropomorphic descriptions of Allah, such as His hand and fingers.

Core Idea
The Salaf affirm without knowing how; the Khalaf interpret to preserve transcendence.

This passage introduces a key methodological divide in Islamic theology: how to understand verses and hadith that attribute physical features to Allah. The Salaf accept them as true without seeking explanation, while the Khalaf offer metaphorical interpretations to safeguard divine transcendence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping later theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once in Arabic, then in English. Notice the two groups mentioned: السلف and الخلف.
The passage quotes Quran 48:10 and a hadith about the heart between two fingers.
2
Identify the Salaf position
The Salaf say: Allah has a face, hand, and fingers, but we do not know them (i.e., we affirm without modality).
This is the tafwīḍ approach: consigning knowledge to Allah.
3
Identify the Khalaf position
The Khalaf interpret 'face' as essence, 'hand' as power, and 'fingers' as attributes (power and will).
This is the ta'wīl approach: metaphorical interpretation.
4
Understand the key terms
Focus on key terms: اليد (hand), الأصابع (fingers), القدرة (power), الإرادة (will).
These terms are central to the controversy.
5
Reflect on the significance
Why does this distinction matter? It shows two valid ways to handle ambiguous texts (mutashābihāt).
Both groups affirm the attributes, but differ in methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَيْدِيهِمْ ﴾ [الفتح: ١٠]، وحديث مسلم : إنَّ قلوب بني آدمَ كُلَّها كقلب واحدٍ بين إصبعين مِنْ أصابع الرحمن ، فالسلف يقولون : الله وجه ويد وأصابع لا نعلمها، والخلف يقولون: المراد من الوجه الذاتُ، وباليد القدرة، والمراد من قوله : بين أصبعين من أصابع الرحمن بين صفتين من صفاته، وهاتان الصفتان: القدرة والإرادة
Their hands [Quran 48:10], and the hadith of Muslim: 'The hearts of the children of Adam are all like one heart between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful.' The Salaf say: Allah has a face, a hand, and fingers, and we do not know them. The Khalaf say: The intended meaning of 'face' is the essence, and by 'hand' is power, and the intended meaning of His statement 'between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful' is between two of His attributes, and these two attributes are power and will.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage explains the positions of the Salaf and Khalaf regarding the interpretation of anthropomorphic expressions in the Quran and Sunnah. The author begins by quoting the verse 'Their hands' (48:10) and the hadith about hearts between two fingers. The Salaf affirm these attributes without knowledge of their modality (bilā kayf). The Khalaf interpret 'face' as the essence (dhāt) and 'hand' as power (qudra), and 'two fingers' as two attributes: power and will (irāda). This shows the two main approaches to the mutashābihāt.

Key Terms
السلف
الخلف
التأويل
اليد
الأصابع
القدرة
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Khalaf deny Allah's attributes altogether.
Correction: The Khalaf do not deny attributes; they interpret them metaphorically to avoid anthropomorphism. For example, they affirm power instead of a physical hand.

Checkpoint

B) Power (قدرة)
C) Knowledge (علم)
D) Essence (ذات)

Key Takeaway
The Salaf affirm anthropomorphic expressions without knowing how, while the Khalaf interpret them metaphorically to preserve divine transcendence, both affirming Allah's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quranic verse about Allah's hand or the hadith about fingers, do you feel a tension between accepting the literal wording and protecting Allah from resembling creation? Which approach—Salaf's affirmation without knowing how or Khalaf's metaphorical interpretation—resonates more with your heart? Can you live with the ambiguity of not knowing how, or do you need a rational explanation? What does that preference reveal about your trust in revelation?
?
The Salaf were content to say 'we do not know' and leave the true meaning to Allah. Is there an area of your faith where you struggle with not having all the answers? How would your relationship with Allah change if you embraced 'bilā kayf' in that area?
?
The Khalaf interpreted the attributes to avoid anthropomorphism, but some accuse them of distorting the text. Have you ever faced a situation where you felt a literal reading of a religious text conflicted with a higher principle? How did you reconcile that conflict? Did you reinterpret, or did you affirm the literal and suspend judgment? Which path aligns more with your current spiritual state?

The First Three Impossibilities for Allah
The commentator lists the first three impossibilities for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three things that are impossible for Allah (non-existence, origination, and annihilation) and their relationship to His necessary attributes.

Core Idea
Allah cannot not exist, cannot begin, and cannot cease: these are the first three impossibilities.

The commentator begins the detailed enumeration of what is rationally impossible for Allah. Following al-Sanusi, he identifies three fundamental impossibilities that directly oppose three of Allah's necessary attributes: existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness. This passage establishes the logical foundation for understanding divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the three impossibilities and their opposites.
2
Identify each impossibility and its opposite attribute
For each impossibility (non-existence, origination, annihilation), find the opposing attribute (existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness). Understand why each is impossible because it contradicts a necessary attribute.
3
Reflect on the logical necessity
Think about why these impossibilities are not arbitrary but logically derived from what Allah must be. Ask yourself: if Allah is eternal, can He have a beginning?
This connects to the core idea that Allah's attributes logically exclude these limitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أجمل المصنف الأضداد، ونحن نذكرها تفصيلاً كما ذكرها السنوسي: فيستحيل عليه تعالى العدم ; وهو ضد الوجود. والحدوث ; وهو ضد القدم. وطرو العدم ; وهو الفناء; وهو ضد البقاء.
And the author summarized the opposites, and we mention them in detail as al-Sanusi mentioned them: So it is impossible for Him (may He be exalted) non-existence; and it is the opposite of existence. And origination; and it is the opposite of pre-eternity. And the befalling of non-existence; which is annihilation; and it is the opposite of everlastingness.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage begins the detailed enumeration of impossibilities. The commentator states that the author of the poem (al-Laqqani) only summarized the opposites, but now he (the commentator) will elaborate as al-Sanusi did. He then lists the first three impossibilities: 1) al-'adam (non-existence), which is the opposite of wujud (existence); 2) al-huduth (origination, coming into being after non-existence), which is the opposite of qidam (pre-eternity); 3) turu' al-'adam (the befalling of non-existence, i.e., annihilation), which is al-fana' (annihilation), the opposite of baqa' (everlastingness). Each is the direct opposite of a necessary attribute: existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness. Note that the second and third are both types of temporal limitation: the second is arising from nothing, the third is ceasing to exist. These are the first three of a longer list of impossibilities derived from the necessary attributes.

Key Terms
العدم
الحدوث
طرو العدم
الفناء
ضد الوجود
ضد القدم
ضد البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah could potentially cease to exist or have a beginning in time.
Correction: Non-existence, origination, and annihilation are impossible for Allah because they directly contradict His necessary attributes of existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the first three impossibilities for Allah mentioned in this passage?
A) Non-existence (al-'adam)
B) Origination (al-huduth)
C) Annihilation (al-fana')
D) Likeness to created things (al-mumathalah)

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot not exist, cannot come into being, and cannot cease to exist; these three impossibilities are derived directly from His attributes of existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that Allah cannot cease to exist. Does your heart truly rest in that certainty, or do you sometimes act as if His support might run out? What would change in your life today if you fully internalized that Allah's existence is permanent and unchanging?
?
The impossibility of Allah having a beginning means He was always there, even before you existed. How does that reality affect the way you approach Him in your prayers? Do you pray as if you are speaking to an eternal, self-sufficient Being, or do you sometimes forget His majesty? What can you do to remind yourself of His pre-eternity before the next prayer?
?
If Allah were subject to origination or annihilation, He would not be worthy of complete reliance. Since you now know these are impossible, will you commit to trusting Him with a matter you have been trying to control yourself? Write down one specific area where you will surrender control, and act on it today.

The Three Primary Impossibilities: Non-existence, Origination, and Annihilation
Al-Jawhari lists the first three impossibilities for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three impossibilities regarding God: non-existence, origination, and annihilation, and how they are opposites of His necessary attributes.

Core Idea
God cannot not exist, begin, or cease to exist—these are impossible.

Before detailing the ten forms of likeness to creation, the author lists three fundamental impossibilities that are clear from reason: God cannot be non-existent, originate from nothing, or be subject to annihilation. These are direct opposites of His attributes of existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness.

Guided Path
1
Read the introductory phrase
Note that the author is providing the detailed list as given by al-Sanusi. This sets the context for the enumeration.
2
Identify the three impossibilities
Find each impossibility (non-existence, origination, annihilation) and its corresponding opposite attribute (existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness).
These are the first three of four total impossibilities.
3
Reflect on their logical necessity
Think about why these impossibilities are self-evident: if God could cease to exist, He would not be necessary; if He had a beginning, He would depend on something else.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أجمل المصنف الأضداد، ونحن نذكرها تفصيلاً كما ذكرها السنوسي: فيستحيل عليه تعالى العدم ؛ وهو ضد الوجود. والحدوث ؛ وهو ضد القدم. وطرو العدم ؛ وهو الفناء؛ وهو ضد البقاء. والمماثلة للحوادث؛ وهي ضد المخالفة للحوادث، والمماثلة مصورة بأن يكون جرماً سواء كان مُركَباً، ويُسمى حينئذ جسماً ، أو غير مركّب، ويُسمى حينئذٍ جوهراً فرداً، لكن المجسمة لا يكفرون إلا إن قالوا : (هو جسم كالأجسام)، أو بأن يكونَ عرضاً يقوم بالجرم؛ أو يكون في جهة للجرم، فليس فوق العرش ولا تحته، ولا عن يمينه ولا عن شماله، ونحو ذلك، أو له هو جهةٌ ; فليس له فوق ولا تحت ولا يمين ولا شمال ونحو ذلك، أو يحلَّ في المكان، فالحلول هو المراد (بالتقييد) في عبارة من عبر به والمراد بالمكان الفراغ الموهوم على رأي المتكلمين، أو المحقق على رأي الحكماء، ومعنى كونه موهوماً عند المتكلمين : أنه يتوهم أنه أمر وجودي، وليس كذلك، بل هو أمر عدمي ، وقيل : معنى كونه موهوماً أنه يتوهم أنه فراغ، وليس كذلك، بل هو مملوء بالهواء، فليس فراغاً محققاً، أو يتقيَّد بالزمان، بحيث تكون حركة الفلك منطبقةً عليه، أو يكرَّ عليه الجديدان الليل والنهار، أو تتصف ذاته العليَّةُ بالحوادث كالقدرة الحادثة والإرادة الحادثة والحركة أو السكون والبياض أو السواد، أو نحو ذلك، أو يتصف بالصِّغَرِ بمعنى قلة الأجزاء، أو بالكبر بمعنى كثرة الأجزاء، فليس صغيراً بمعنى قليل الأجزاء، ولا كبيراً بمعنى كثير الأجزاء، وهذا لا ينافي أنه تعالى كبير في المرتبة والشرف، قال الله تعالى: الْكَبِيرُ الْمُتَعَالِ [الرعد: ٩] . أو يتصف بالأغراض في الأفعال أو الأحكام، فليس فعله كإيجاد زيد لغرض من الأغراض ; أي مصلحة تبعثه على ذلك الفعل، فلا ينافي أنه لحكمة، وإلا لكان عبئاً، وهو مستحيل في حقه تعالى، وليس حكمه كإيجابه الصلاة علينا لغرض من الأغراض أي مصلحة تبعثه على ذلك الحكم، فلا ينافي أنه لحكمة كما علمت فصور المماثلة عشرة.
And the author summarized the opposites, and we mention them in detail as al-Sanusi mentioned them: So non-existence is impossible for Him, exalted; it is the opposite of existence. And origination (al-huduth); it is the opposite of pre-eternity (al-qidam). And the occurrence of non-existence (turu' al-'adam); it is annihilation (al-fana'); it is the opposite of everlastingness (al-baqa'). And likeness to originated things (al-mumathalah lil-hawadith); it is the opposite of dissimilarity to originated things (al-mukhalafah lil-hawadith). Likeness is exemplified by one being a body (jirm), whether it is compound and then called a body (jism), or non-compound and then called an individual substance (jawhar fard); but the anthropomorphists (al-mujassimah) are not deemed disbelievers unless they say 'He is a body like bodies.' Or by being an accident ('arad) subsisting in a body; or by being in a direction (jihah) relative to a body, so that He is not above the Throne nor below it, nor to its right nor to its left, and so on; or by having a direction Himself, so that He has no above, no below, no right, no left, and so on; or by indwelling (hulul) in place; indwelling is what is meant by 'restriction (al-taqyid)' in the expression of whoever expressed it, and what is meant by place is the imagined void (al-faragh al-mawhum) according to the theologians, or the actual void according to the philosophers; and the meaning of its being imagined (mawhum) for the theologians is that it is imagined to be an existent thing, but it is not so; rather it is non-existent; and it is said: the meaning of its being imagined is that it is imagined to be a void, but it is not so; rather it is filled with air, so it is not an actual void; or by being restricted by time, such that the motion of the celestial sphere corresponds to it, or the two new things (al-jadidan) — night and day — come upon Him; or by His most high essence being qualified by originated accidents (al-hawadith) like originated power, originated will, motion or rest, whiteness or blackness, or the like; or by being qualified by smallness (al-sighar) in the sense of fewness of parts, or by greatness (al-kibar) in the sense of numerousness of parts; so He is not small in the sense of few parts, nor great in the sense of many parts; this does not contradict that He, exalted, is great in rank and honor; Allah the Exalted said: 'The Great, the Most High' (al-Kabir al-Muta'al) [Surah al-Ra'd 9]. Or by being qualified by purposes (al-aghrad) in actions or judgments; so His action is not like creating Zayd for some purpose, i.e., an interest that impels Him to that action; this does not contradict that it is for a wisdom, otherwise it would be a burden, and that is impossible for Him, exalted; and His judgment is not like His obligating prayer upon us for some purpose, i.e., an interest that impels Him to that judgment; so this does not contradict that it is for a wisdom, as you have learned. Thus the forms of likeness are ten.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage enumerates four primary impossibilities for God, with the fourth (likeness) divided into ten specific forms. The author begins by stating that he is providing the detailed list as given by al-Sanusi. The first three are straightforward: non-existence (against existence), origination (against pre-eternity), and annihilation (against everlastingness). The fourth and most detailed is likeness to created things (al-mumathalah lil-hawadith). This is the opposite of God's attribute of dissimilarity (mukhalafah lil-hawadith). He then lists the ten forms: 1) He is a body — either compound (jism) or simple (jawhar fard), but note that anthropomorphists are not takfir unless they explicitly say He is like bodies. 2) He is an accident subsisting in a body. 3) He is in a direction relative to a body (e.g., above the Throne). 4) He has a direction Himself. 5) He indwells in place, with an explanation of what 'place' means for theologians (imaginary void) and philosophers (actual void). 6) He is restricted by time (motion of the sphere or succession of night and day). 7) His essence is qualified by originated accidents (like power, will, motion, etc.). 8) He is qualified by smallness (few parts). 9) He is qualified by greatness (many parts). 10) He is qualified by purposes (aghrad) in His actions or judgments. For each, the author clarifies what is meant and gives nuances, e.g., saying that denying greatness in terms of parts does not negate His greatness in rank, citing the Quranic verse. He also distinguishes between 'purpose' and 'wisdom' — God's actions are not for a purpose that impels Him, but they are for a wisdom. This brings the total forms of likeness to ten, as stated at the end.

Correction: The passage affirms that non-existence, origination, and annihilation are absolutely impossible for God, as they contradict His necessary attributes.

The Ten Forms of Likeness to Creation
Al-Jawhari details ten forms of likeness to creation that are impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ten specific ways in which likening God to creation is impossible, and how each form is negated by God's attribute of dissimilarity.

Core Idea
God is unlike creation in ten specific ways—body, accident, direction, place, time, etc.

After establishing the first three impossibilities, the author elaborates on the fourth: likeness to created things. He lists ten distinct forms, each representing a way some groups have wrongly described God. Understanding these helps protect the Islamic concept of tanzih (divine transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Read the opening sentence
The passage begins by stating that likeness (mumathalah) is the opposite of dissimilarity (mukhalafah). Then it lists the ten forms.
Each form is a specific negation of likeness.
2
Identify each of the ten forms
Go through the passage and list each form: being a body, accident, in a direction, having a direction, indwelling in place, restricted by time, qualified by originated accidents, smallness, greatness, and having purposes.
Note the nuances like the exception for anthropomorphists.
3
Understand the final two forms
Pay attention to the distinction between greatness in rank vs. quantity, and between purpose and wisdom.
These have common misunderstandings.
4
Count the forms
At the end, the author says 'thus the forms of likeness are ten.' Verify you have all ten.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever catch yourself imagining God as 'up there' or 'somewhere'? How does the negation of direction challenge that mental image, and what concrete step can you take today to train your heart to worship a God who is beyond place?
?
You likely affirm that God is not like creation, but have you ever silently attributed to Him a human-like purpose—such as needing your worship or being angered like a person? The passage teaches that God's acts are for wisdom, not purposes (aghrāḍ). Are you willing to surrender your expectations of why God does what He does, even when His wisdom is hidden? What is one recent event in your life where you questioned God's wisdom, and how can you reframe it using this distinction?
?
The lesson says that greatness in rank is affirmed while physical greatness is negated. In your heart, have you ever pictured God's power in physical terms—like a giant force or an immense energy? If so, how can you purify your concept of divine greatness to align with this teaching? Write down one attribute you tend to imagine physically and commit to replacing that image with the language of the Qur'an and Sunna.

Ten Impossible Forms of Similarity (Tashbih) for Allah
Lists and explains ten impossible forms of similarity for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ten categories of similarity (tashbih) that are impossible for Allah, and why each is negated by divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Allah is utterly unlike creation; none of the ten forms of similarity apply to Him.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary clarifies the key principle of tanzih (transcendence) by enumerating specific forms of similarity that cannot be attributed to Allah. The author refutes the notion that Allah could be imagined as a void, bound by time, subject to accidents, composed of parts, or acting for a purpose. These negations safeguard the Islamic understanding of God's absolute uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice how the passage begins with 'كذلك' (likewise) and ends by stating that there are ten forms.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the five main categories
The passage presents five broad categories of impossible similarity: 1) being a void, 2) being bound by time, 3) being subject to accidents, 4) being composed of parts, 5) acting for a purpose. Find each in the text.
Each category includes specific examples.
3
Understand the negation of each
For each category, note how the author negates it and provides a clarification. Pay special attention to the distinction between 'greatness in parts' and 'greatness in rank'.
4
Reflect on the overall purpose
Ask yourself: why is it necessary to list these specific forms? They protect against anthropomorphism and affirm tawhid.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك، بل هو أمر عدمي ، وقيل : معنى كونه موهوماً أنه يتوهم أنه فراغ، وليس كذلك، بل هو مملوء بالهواء، فليس فراغاً محققاً، أو يتقيَّد بالزمان، بحيث تكون حركة الفلك منطبقةً عليه، أو يكرَّ عليه الجديدان الليل والنهار، أو تتصف ذاته العليَّةُ بالحوادث كالقدرة الحادثة والإرادة الحادثة والحركة أو السكون والبياض أو السواد، أو نحو ذلك، أو يتصف بالصِّغَرِ بمعنى قلة الأجزاء، أو بالكبر بمعنى كثرة الأجزاء، فليس صغيراً بمعنى قليل الأجزاء، ولا كبيراً بمعنى كثير الأجزاء، وهذا لا ينافي أنه تعالى كبير في المرتبة والشرف، قال الله تعالى: الْكَبِيرُ الْمُتَعَالِ [الرعد: ٩] . أو يتصف بالأغراض في الأفعال أو الأحكام، فليس فعله كإيجاد زيد لغرض من الأغراض؛ أي مصلحة تبعثه على ذلك الفعل، فلا ينافي أنه لحكمة، وإلا لكان عبئاً، وهو مستحيل في حقه تعالى، وليس حكمه كإيجابه الصلاة علينا لغرض من الأغراض أي مصلحة تبعثه على ذلك الحكم، فلا ينافي أنه لحكمة كما علمت فصور المماثلة عشرة.
Likewise, rather it is a non-entity. And it is said: the meaning of it being imaginary is that one imagines it to be a void, but it is not so; rather it is filled with air, so it is not a real void. Or that He is bound by time, such that the motion of the sphere corresponds to it, or that the two renewing [cycles] of night and day pass over Him. Or that His exalted essence is subject to accidents like originated power and originated will and motion or rest and whiteness or blackness, or the like. Or that He is described with smallness in the sense of few parts, or with bigness in the sense of many parts; so He is not small in the sense of few parts, nor big in the sense of many parts. This does not negate that He is Great in rank and honor. Allah said: The Great, the Exalted [al-Ra'd 9]. Or that He is described with purposes in actions or rulings: so His act is not like creating Zayd for a purpose, i.e., a benefit that impels Him to that act; this does not negate that it is for wisdom; otherwise it would be a burden, and that is impossible with respect to Him. And His ruling is not like imposing prayer upon us for a purpose, i.e., a benefit that impels Him to that ruling; this does not negate that it is for wisdom, as you know. So the forms of similarity are ten.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation of various false notions of similarity (tashbih) that are impossible for Allah. It begins by addressing the idea that the 'imaginary' (mawhum) is a void; the author clarifies that it is not a void but filled with air, so the concept of a void is unreal. Then it lists forms of similarity: being bound by time, being subject to accidents (like power, will, motion, color), being described with smallness or bigness in terms of parts (while affirming He is Great in rank), having purposes (aghrad) in actions or rulings that necessitate benefit for Allah—rather, His actions and rulings are for wisdom, not for a personal benefit. The passage concludes that there are ten forms of similarity, implying that these examples are part of that enumeration.

Key Terms
تشبيه
موهوم
أعراض
أغراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that negating smallness and bigness of parts conflicts with Allah being 'Great'.
Correction: The passage clarifies that greatness in rank and honor is affirmed, while smallness/bigness in terms of parts is negated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is Allah not described with smallness or bigness in the sense of parts?
A) Because He is not a body composed of parts.
B) Because smallness and bigness are temporal attributes.
C) Because He is described as Great in rank and honor.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
Allah is absolutely unique and cannot be compared to creation in any of the ten forms of similarity; He is free from limitation, composition, accident, and purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading that Allah is not bound by time, do you still catch yourself imagining Him existing within the same clock that ticks for you? How will you train your mind to eliminate that subtle association tomorrow?
?
The passage says Allah's actions are not motivated by any personal benefit or purpose. Does that idea unsettle you? Why do you think you instinctively expect God to act for reasons you can understand?
?
You learned that Allah is not composed of parts—nothing about Him can be divided or measured. Yet how often do you reduce His greatness to something your mind can neatly categorize? What concrete step will you take today to worship Him as utterly unique, beyond all categories?

Negation of Ignorance, Death, Muteness, Blindness, and Others
Lists attributes opposite to divine perfections: ignorance, death, muteness, blindness, etc.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the negative attributes that must be negated from Allah and how each is the direct opposite of a necessary perfection.

Core Idea
Allah's perfection requires negating all deficiencies—ignorance, death, muteness, blindness, and more.

This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid enumerates attributes that are impossible for Allah. Each is the direct opposite of a positive attribute that Allah necessarily possesses. Understanding these negations is fundamental to tawhid and divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then its literal translation. Notice how each negative attribute is paired with its opposite perfection.
The passage uses a concise listing style.
2
Identify each negative attribute
List each negative attribute mentioned: ignorance (with related terms), death, muteness, blindness, and the reference to powerlessness. For each, note the opposite perfection.
Ignorance includes opinion, doubt, illusion, and sleep.
3
Understand why sleep is included
Sleep is not ignorance itself but involves temporary unawareness, which is incompatible with Allah's perfect knowledge. This shows the thoroughness of negation.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.
4
Consider the doctrine of states
The phrase 'according to the doctrine of states (ahwal)' refers to a theological position about real but non-existent attributes. This nuance affects how some negations are understood.
The ellipsis indicates the list continues beyond what is written.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجهل وما في معناه كالظن والشك والوهم والنوم، وهذا ضد العلم. والموت وهو ضد الحياة. والبَكَمُ النفسي وهو ضد الكلام. والعمى وهو ضد البصر. وكونه عاجزاً ... إلخ على القول بالأحوال.
And ignorance and what is in its meaning, like opinion, doubt, illusion, and sleep; this is the opposite of knowledge. And death; it is the opposite of life. And psychological muteness; it is the opposite of speech. And blindness; it is the opposite of sight. And that He is powerless... etc. according to the doctrine of states (ahwal).
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a brief enumeration of negative attributes that are impossible for Allah, each being the contrary of a necessary positive attribute. It lists ignorance (jahl) and its related concepts: opinion (ẓann), doubt (shakk), illusion (wahm), and sleep (nawm), all opposed to knowledge (ʿilm). Then death (mawt) opposed to life (ḥayāt); psychological muteness (bakan nafsī) opposed to speech (kalām); blindness (ʿamā) opposed to sight (baṣar). Finally, it mentions being powerless (ʿājiz) according to the doctrine of states (aḥwāl), which is a reference to a particular theological position that affirms states as real but not fully existent attributes; the ellipsis indicates a continuation of the list of negations.

Key Terms

موت
بكم
عمى
أحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sleep is itself an attribute of ignorance.
Correction: Sleep is not ignorance but involves temporary unawareness, which is incompatible with Allah's perfect knowledge; thus it is included among the opposites of knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why is sleep included among the opposites of knowledge?
A) Because sleep is a form of ignorance.
B) Because sleep involves a lack of awareness.
C) Because sleep is a type of doubt.
D) Because the author made a mistake.

Key Takeaway
Allah is free from all deficiencies; each negative attribute negated here corresponds to a necessary perfection that Allah possesses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Are there opinions or illusions you hold about Allah that you have not examined against revelation? Will you commit to studying the Qur'an and Sunnah to replace them with certain knowledge?

The Impossibility of Spatial Direction for Allah
Affirms the impossibility of spatial direction for Allah and explains the meaning of al-kawn fi al-jihat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why assigning a spatial direction to Allah is impossible, based on the arguments from change of states and dependence on time.

Core Idea
Allah cannot be in a direction; directions imply change and time, which Allah transcends.

This lesson examines the theological argument that Allah is free from spatial direction. The passage begins by stating this impossibility, then explains that occupying a direction would entail change and need for time, both of which are incompatible with Allah's eternal nature. Supporting quotes from al-Tahawi and Ibn Khaldun reinforce this transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical progression: thesis, explanation, argument, quotes.
The passage uses a deductive style common in kalam.
2
Understand the argument from change of states
If Allah were in a direction, He would be subject to change (e.g., moving from one state to another), implying deficiency and perfection, which is impossible for Allah.
This is a key point: direction entails potential change.
3
See the implication of time
Direction also requires time as a condition for the concept, leading to dependence on created time, which is false.
Time is a contingent entity; Allah is eternal and not dependent on time.
4
Reflect on the quotes
Al-Tahawi affirms that Allah's attributes are eternal and unchanging. Ibn Khaldun advises restricting perception and following revelation.
These quotes support the argument from authority.
5
Connect to divine transcendence
The lesson reinforces tanzih: Allah is beyond spatial and temporal constraints.
This is a foundational principle in Sunni creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة الجهة في حقه تعالى. قوله : (كَالكَوْنِ فِي الجِهَاتِ) أي: ككونه تعالى في جهة من الجهات الست. لو كان الله تعالى يخصص نفسه بمعنى تغير الأحوال فإن إحدى الحالتين نقص والأخرى كمال، ناهيك عن القول بحلول الحوادث في ذاته تعالى، والوقت شرط لهذا التصور، فيلزم عنه افتقار الذات إلى الزمان الحادث، وهو باطل، ولهذا قال العلامة الطحاوي في عقيدته: (ما زال بصفاته قديماً قبل خلقه، لم يزدد بكونهم شيئاً لم يكن قبلهم من صفته، كما كان بصفاته أزليا، كذلك لا يزال عليها أبدياً). يقول ابن خلدون في تاريخه: (فاتهم إدراكك ومدركاتك في الحصر، واتبع ما أمرك الشارع به من اعتقادك وعملك، فهو أحرص على سعادتك، وأعلم بما ينفعك؛ لأنه من طور فوق إدراكك، ومن نطاق أوسع من عقلك). مطلب في نفي الكذب عن الله تعالى وتقدَّس: قال العضد في «المواقف»: (لو اتصف بالكذب .. لكان كذبه قديماً؛ إذ لا يقوم الحادث بذاته تعالى فيلزم أن يمتنع عليه الصدق، فإن ما ثبت قدمه .. امتنع عدمه واللازم باطل).
The impossibility of direction with respect to Him, Exalted. His saying: (like being in directions) i.e., like His being in one of the six directions. If Allah were to specify Himself in the sense of changing states, then one of the two states would be a deficiency and the other a perfection, not to mention the assertion of the occurrence of accidents in His essence, and time is a condition for this conception; so it would necessitate the essence's need for originated time, which is false. For this reason, the eminent al-Tahawi said in his creed: 'He has not ceased to be with His attributes, eternal before creation; He did not increase by their coming into being with something of His attribute that was not before them; just as He was eternal with His attributes, so He will not cease to be upon them eternally.' Ibn Khaldun says in his History: 'So stop your perception and your perceived things from being confined, and follow what the Lawgiver commanded you regarding your belief and practice, for He is more careful of your happiness and more knowing of what benefits you; because it is from a realm above your perception and from a scope wider than your intellect.' A section on the negation of lying from Allah, Exalted and Sanctified: al-Adud said in al-Mawaqif: 'If He were described with lying... then His lie would be eternal, for the originated does not subsist in His essence; so it would necessitate that truth be impossible for Him, for what is established as eternal is impossible to cease; and the conclusion is false.'
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument affirming the impossibility of spatial direction (jiha) for Allah. It begins by stating the impossibility and then explains a statement: 'like being in directions' means being in one of the six spatial directions. It argues that if Allah were to occupy a direction, He would be subject to change of states (deficiency to perfection), which is impossible. More fundamentally, it would imply that accidents occur in His essence, requiring time as a condition, thereby making the essence dependent on created time—also impossible. The passage then quotes al-Tahawi's creed affirming that Allah's attributes are eternal and unchanging, not increased by creation. Ibn Khaldun's advice warns against restricting perception and recommends following the revealed law. Finally, it includes a separate note on the impossibility of lying for Allah, citing al-‘Aḍud: if lying were eternal (since originated attributes cannot subsist in Allah), then truth would be impossible, which is false. The note reinforces the impossibility of originated attributes in Allah.

Key Terms
جهة
كون في الجهات
أعراض
زمان
قديم
أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'direction' could be metaphorical, but the passage strictly negates any literal spatial direction for Allah, even metaphorical assignment of a direction as a locus.
Correction: The passage argues that any notion of direction, even metaphorical, would entail change and time, which are impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
What does al-Tahawi's statement in the passage emphasize?
A) Allah increases in attributes with creation.
B) Allah's attributes are eternal and do not increase.
C) Allah's essence is subject to time.
D) Allah acquires new attributes when He creates.

Key Takeaway
Allah is beyond spatial direction; any such attribution would entail change and time, contradicting His eternal perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you find yourself instinctively pointing upwards or imagining Allah 'above' the skies? After learning that Allah is beyond any direction, how can you retrain your heart to feel His presence without spatial reference?
?
You believe that Allah is beyond time and change. Yet in your daily life, do you sometimes expect Allah to 'act' in a way that assumes He is subject to time—like becoming angry when you sin and then pleased when you repent? How does this lesson challenge your mental model of Allah's relationship to time?
?
The passage quotes Ibn Khaldun: 'Stop your perception and your perceived things from being confined, and follow what the Lawgiver commanded you.' In what area of your faith are you tempted to confine Allah within your own rational categories? What is one specific step you can take this week to submit your reasoning to revelation more fully?

The Impossibility of Lying for Allah
A concise argument negating lying from Allah using a reductio from al-Adud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why lying is impossible for Allah, based on the argument that a lie would have to be eternal, making truth impossible.

Core Idea
If Allah could lie, His lie would be eternal, which would preclude truth, an impossibility.

This short lesson presents a concise argument from al-Adud in al-Mawaqif that negates lying from Allah. The reasoning is that if Allah were described with lying, His lie would be eternal (since accidents do not subsist in His essence), and what is eternal cannot cease; thus truth would be impossible, which is false. Therefore lying cannot be attributed to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and the translation. Notice the logical structure: conditional premise, reason, conclusion.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the premise
If lying were attributed to Allah, the lie would be eternal because originated attributes cannot subsist in Allah's essence.
This is based on the principle that Allah's attributes are eternal.
3
Follow the logical entailment
An eternal lie would make truth impossible, since an eternal attribute cannot cease. But truth is possible for Allah, so the premise is false.
Thus lying is impossible for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The impossibility of direction with respect to Him, Exalted. His saying: (like being in directions) i.e., like His being in one of the six directions. If Allah were to specify Himself in the sense of changing states, then one of the two states would be a deficiency and the other a perfection, not to mention the assertion of the occurrence of accidents in His essence, and time is a condition for this conception; so it would necessitate the essence's need for originated time, which is false. ?

Is there a sin you have justified by convincing yourself that 'Allah will understand'? How does the absolute impossibility of Allah's lying expose that rationalization as a lie you tell yourself?

Impossibility of Direction for Allah
Explanation of why direction is impossible for Allah and rulings on belief in direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why direction is impossible for Allah and the nuanced rulings regarding those who attribute direction to Him.

Core Idea
Belief in a direction for Allah is not automatically kufr—the ruling depends on context and intention.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary addresses a sensitive theological issue: the impossibility of Allah being in a direction. While direction implies limitation and similarity to created things, the scholars do not immediately declare disbelief on those who hold such a belief. The passage presents different scholarly positions and a detailed view that distinguishes between above and below directions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the main point that direction is an example of similarity to accidents.
Notice how the author uses 'كالكون في الجهات' as a representative example.
2
Understand the rulings
Notice the different scholarly positions on takfir for belief in direction, and how they refine the ruling based on the person's understanding.
Al-Nawawi restricts to common folk; Ibn Abi Jamra adds difficulty of understanding negation.
3
Analyze the detailed view
Consider why belief in the direction of above is treated differently from below, and what that implies about theological reasoning.
The distinction is based on honor vs. baseness of the direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة الجهة في حقه تعالى] قوله : (كَالكَوْنِ فِي الجِهَاتِ) أي: ككونه تعالى في جهة من الجهات الست. وهذا مثال من أمثلة المماثلة للحوادث، ويُقاس عليه باقي أمثلة المماثلة)، بل وباقي صور المستحيل، كما أشار إليه المصنف بالكاف. واعلم أن معتقد الجهة لا يكفر كما قاله العز بن عبد السلام، وقيَّده النووي بكونه من العامة، وابن أبي جمرة بعُسْرِ فَهُم نفيها. وفصَّلَ بعضهم فقال: إن اعتقد جهة العلو لم يكفر؛ لأنَّ جهة العلو فيها شرفٌ ورفعةٌ في الجملة، وإن اعتقد جهة السفل كفر؛ لأنَّ جهة السفل فيها خِسَّةٌ ودناءةٌ.
[Impossibility of direction in His right] His saying: (like being in directions) i.e., like His being in one of the six directions. This is an example of similitude to accidents, and the rest of examples of similitude are analogized to it, nay, even the rest of forms of the impossible, as the author indicated with the kaf. And know that the believer in direction does not become a disbeliever, as al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam said, and al-Nawawi restricted it to the common folk, and Ibn Abi Jamra to difficulty of understanding its negation. And some detailed, saying: if he believes in the direction of above, he does not disbelieve because the direction of above has honor and elevation in general, but if he believes in the direction of below, he disbelieves because the direction of below has baseness and lowliness.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage clarifies that direction is impossible for Allah. The author first states that the phrase 'كالكون في الجهات' is an example of similarity to created things. Then he discusses the ruling on someone who believes Allah is in a direction: according to leading scholars, it does not automatically render one a disbeliever. Al-Nawawi restricts this to laypeople who cannot understand the negation; Ibn Abi Jamra adds the condition of difficulty of comprehension. A detailed view distinguishes between believing in the above direction (honorable, so not kufr) and below direction (degrading, so kufr). This shows the nuanced approach to takfir.

Key Terms
الجهة
المماثلة
الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any belief that Allah is in a direction is an act of disbelief (kufr) without exception.
Correction: The passage shows that the ruling is nuanced: many scholars do not consider it kufr, especially for common folk or when the belief is in the direction of above.

Checkpoint
According to the detailed view, what is the ruling on one who believes Allah is in the direction of above?
A) He becomes a disbeliever
B) He does not become a disbeliever
C) His faith is invalid
D) He must repent

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of direction for Allah is a clear doctrine, but the ruling on those who hold it is nuanced and depends on their understanding and the specific direction.

The Ruling on Believing God Has a Direction
Al-Ajhari presents multiple scholarly opinions on the ruling of believing in a direction for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced rulings of classical scholars regarding belief in direction for God, and why immediate takfir is not applied.

Core Idea
Not all directional beliefs about God are equal in Islamic rulings.

The issue of whether God occupies a direction (jiha) is a sensitive topic in Islamic theology. While the Ash'ari position affirms God's transcendence above direction, this passage from Tuhfat al-Murid shows that classical scholars did not automatically declare those who hold such beliefs as disbelievers. Instead, they offered nuanced rulings based on the type of direction and the person's background. Let's explore these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to familiarize yourself with the different opinions presented.
The passage is short but dense; read carefully.
2
Identify each scholar's ruling
List the positions of al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam, al-Nawawi, Ibn Abi Jamra, and the detailed view. Note any conditions they attach.
3
Understand the detailed view
Reflect on why the detailed view distinguishes between direction of loftiness (honor) and direction of lowness (baseness). Consider theological implications.
This distinction is key to avoiding hasty takfir.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this passage teaches us to be cautious in declaring disbelief, and how it shows the depth of Islamic scholarly methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن معتقد الجهة لا يكفر كما قاله العز بن عبد السلام، وقيَّده النووي بكونه من العامة، وابن أبي جمرة بعُسْرِ فَهْمِ نفيها . وفصَّلَ بعضهم فقال : إن اعتقد جهة العلو لم يكفر؛ لأنَّ جهة العلو فيها شرفٌ ورفعةٌ في الجملة، وإن اعتقد جهة السفل كفر ؛ لأنَّ جهة السفل فيها خِسَّةٌ ودناءةٌ .
And know that the one who believes in a direction is not a disbeliever, as al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam said, and al-Nawawi restricted it to the common people, and Ibn Abi Jamra to the difficulty of understanding its negation. And some detailed, saying: If he believes in a direction of loftiness, he is not a disbeliever, because the direction of loftiness has honor and elevation in general; and if he believes in a direction of lowness, he is a disbeliever, because the direction of lowness has baseness and ignobility.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents a nuanced ruling on whether believing that God occupies a direction constitutes disbelief (kufr). It begins by noting that the prominent scholar al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam held that such a belief does not automatically render one a disbeliever. The passage then records restrictions by other authorities: al-Nawawi restricted this leniency to the common people (al-‘āmmah), while Ibn Abi Jamra added the condition that it applies only if the person finds it difficult to understand the negation of direction from God. Finally, a detailed view is given: if someone believes in a direction of loftiness (e.g., above), they are not a disbeliever because loftiness implies honor; but if they believe in a direction of lowness (e.g., below), they are a disbeliever because lowness implies baseness. The passage thus guards against hasty takfīr while affirming that not all directional beliefs are equal.

Key Terms
الجهة
العز بن عبد السلام
النووي
ابن أبي جمرة
معتقد الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any belief that God has a direction is automatically considered disbelief (kufr).
Correction: As shown by al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam and others, such belief does not automatically lead to takfir; conditions such as the type of direction and the person's understanding are taken into account.

Checkpoint
According to the detailed view mentioned, what is the ruling if someone believes God is in a direction of loftiness?
A) He is a disbeliever.
B) He is not a disbeliever.
C) He is a disbeliever only if he is a commoner.
D) He is not a disbeliever only if he finds it difficult to negate direction.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on belief in direction for God is nuanced; takfir is not applied automatically, and distinctions are made based on the type of direction and the person's capacity.

Three Views on Human Voluntary Actions
The author enumerates three views on voluntary actions and refutes one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three theological positions on the creation of human voluntary actions and the Ash'ari refutation of the view that involves shared power.

Core Idea
Human actions are created solely by God; other views are logically untenable.

This lesson introduces a crucial point of disagreement between the Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila: who creates human voluntary actions? The author presents the three main positions and then rejects the idea that both God and the servant can be creators. Understanding this helps clarify the Ash'ari commitment to divine exclusivity in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then read the literal English translation. Notice the structure: an agreed premise, then three differing positions, then a refutation.
The passage is short but contains a complete argument.
2
Identify the three positions
Look for the three views: the Ash'ari view ('we say'), the Mu'tazili view, and the view transmitted from al-Ustadh. See how each is introduced and what the author says about the third.
3
Understand the refutation
The author ends by stating that the third view is impossible because 'the eternal power has no partner nor helper.' Reflect on why this principle rules out shared causation.
This is the key theological principle at stake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن الناس بعد اتفاقهم على أن الله خالق للعباد ولأفعالهم الاضطرارية اختلفوا في أفعالهم الاختيارية : فنحن نقول : إن الله تعالى خالق لها أيضاً. والمعتزلة يقولون : إن العبد خالق لها بقدرة خلقها الله فيه . ونُقل عن الأستاذ أنها بالقدرتين؛ أي: قدرته تعالى وقدرة العبد، وفيه أن القدرة القديمة لا شريك لها ولا معين.
And the conclusion is that people, after their agreement that God is the Creator of the servants and of their involuntary actions, differed regarding their voluntary actions. So we say: Indeed God is the Creator of them as well. And the Mu'tazila say: Indeed the servant is the creator of them by a power God created in him. And it was transmitted from al-Ustadh that they are by two powers: i.e. His power and the power of the servant, and in that (view) the eternal power has no partner nor helper.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage summarizes the different positions on the creation of human voluntary actions. It begins by noting that all agree God creates involuntary actions (like trembling). Where they differ is on voluntary ones. The Ash'ari position (we say) is that God creates them exclusively. The Mu'tazila say the servant creates them using a power God created in him. A third view transmitted from 'al-Ustadh' (likely Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari or another authority) holds that both powers are involved. This last view is refuted by the principle that the eternal power (God's power) cannot have a partner or helper.

Key Terms
أفعال اختيارية
أفعال اضطرارية
القدرة القديمة
الأستاذ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The view that both God and the servant create voluntary actions is a sensible compromise.
Correction: The Ash'ari position rejects this because God's power is unique and cannot share causation; attributing any creative power to a creature undermines divine exclusivity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Ash'ari position on voluntary actions?
A) They are created by the servant by a power God creates in him.
B) They are created by God alone.
C) They are created by both God and the servant together.
D) They are not created by anyone.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position affirms that God alone creates all human actions, both involuntary and voluntary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you genuinely feel that God created that action in you, or do you secretly attribute it to your own ability? How does this passage expose that hidden shirk?

If you truly believed that every voluntary action you take is created by God alone, how would it change the way you approach repentance? Would you still feel pride in your obedience?
?
The Mu'tazila claim that you create your own actions by a power God gave you. In your daily life, do you act as if you are the independent creator of your actions? What is one specific habit that reveals this assumption, and how will you remind yourself of the truth in this lesson?

God's Justice in Inflicting Pain on Children
The author justifies God's infliction of pain on children as a means of reward for their parents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari tradition justifies God's infliction of pain on children and similar beings as a means of reward and blessing.

Core Idea
God's causing pain to children is a hidden mercy, bringing reward to parents.

The author addresses a common objection to divine justice: why does God allow innocent children to suffer? He refutes the Mu'tazili position by showing that such afflictions are actually blessings for which the parents are rewarded, and even animals and the weak benefit from these trials.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage and note the Mu'tazili objection that God's inflicting pain on children seems unjust.
The poem line 'Alam yaraw ilāmahu al-atfālā' sets up the challenge.
2
Understand the linguistic analysis
Examine the author's discussion of 'seeing' (visual vs. knowledge-based) and the grammatical response to the objection that pain is not visible.
The answer is that the effect of pain is seen, not the pain itself.
3
Grasp the wisdom
Understand that the wisdom of children's pain is to obtain reward for their parents, as these are trials leading to divine compensation.
See the citation from Imam al-Haramayn: hardships are blessings.
4
Extend to others
Note the extension to animals and the weak, showing the general principle that afflictions are beneficial.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this doctrine changes the perspective on suffering and gratitude.
Hardships become reasons for thankfulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من [التحسين العقلي] (٢) ٥٢ - أَلَمْ يَرَوْا إِيلَامَهُ الأَطْفَالَا وَشِبْهَهَا؟ فَحَاذِرِ المِحَالَا قوله : (أَلَمْ يَرَوْا ... إلخ) هذا تنبيه على فساد مذهبهم، والرؤية بصرية، ويحتمل أن تكون علميَّةٌ، والأول أبلغ لمزيد التشنيع عليهم ، وهم حقيقون بذلك، خصوصاً في هذا المقام فإن فيه غاية إساءة الأدب. وقوله : (إيلامه) مفعولُ (يروا)، وعلى جعلها علميَّةً يكون المفعول الثاني محذوفاً تقديره حاصلاً مثلاً، وعلى جعلها بصريةً لا تحتاج إلى مفعول ثانٍ. واعترض بأن الإيلام عبارةٌ عن تعلُّق القدرة بالألم، وهو لا يُرى، وأجيب بأنَّه على حذف مضاف والتقدير أثر إيلامه، وذلك الأثر هو الألم. وقوله : (الأطفالا) مفعول (الإيلام) ؛ لأنَّه مصدر مضاف لفاعله وهو الضمير العائد على الله، فالأصل إيلام الله الأطفال، وحكمة إيلام الأطفال حصول الثواب عليه لأبويهم؛ لأنَّ ذلك من المصائب التي يُثاب الشخص عليها، ولهذا قال إمام الحرمين شدائد الدنيا مما يلزم العبد الشكر عليها ؛ لأنَّها نِعَم حقيقة . قوله : (وَشِبْهَهَا) أي: كالدواب ،والعجزة، فإنَّهم لا نفع لهم في إنزال الأسقام
From [Rational Beautification] (2) 52 - Did they not see His infliction of pain upon children and the like? So beware of sophistry! His statement: 'Did they not see... etc.' This is a warning against the corruption of their school. 'Seeing' here is visual, and it may be knowledge-based; the first is more emphatic for increased denunciation of them, and they are worthy of that, especially in this context, as it involves extreme disrespect. His statement: 'His infliction of pain' is the object of 'they see'. If it is considered knowledge-based, the second object is omitted, its implied meaning being 'occurring' for example. If it is considered visual, it does not need a second object. An objection was raised that 'infliction of pain' is an expression of the attachment of power to pain, and pain is not seen. The answer is that there is a deletion of a genitive construct, the implied meaning being 'the effect of His infliction of pain', and that effect is the pain. His statement: 'the children' is the object of 'infliction of pain', because it is a masdar (verbal noun) attached to its subject, which is the pronoun referring to God, so the original is 'God's infliction of pain upon children'. The wisdom of inflicting pain upon children is obtaining reward for their parents, because that is among the afflictions for which a person is rewarded. For this reason, Imam al-Haramayn said: 'The hardships of this world are such that the servant must give thanks for them, because they are truly blessings.' His statement: 'and the like' means such as animals and the weak, for there is no benefit for them in sending down illnesses.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazili objection. It begins by commenting on the poem line 'Alam yaraw ilāmahu al-atfālā...' The author explains that 'seeing' here is primarily visual, but could be knowledge-based; the visual is stronger for condemning the opponents. Then he addresses an objection: pain itself is not visible, but the effect is. Then he gives the wisdom: the pain of children results in reward for their parents, citing Imam al-Haramayn that such hardships are actually blessings to be thanked for. Finally, he extends the argument to similar beings like animals and the helpless.

Key Terms
الإيلام
الأطفال
المصائب
إمام الحرمين
شكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is unjust for causing pain to innocent children.
Correction: The pain is a means of reward for the parents and is actually a blessing, not a punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the wisdom behind God's infliction of pain on children?
A) To punish them for sins
B) To test their parents' faith
C) To reward their parents
D) To show His power

Key Takeaway
God's infliction of pain on the innocent is not unjust but a means of reward and a blessing to be thankful for.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author rebukes those who accuse God of injustice with extreme disrespect. Examine your own heart: have you ever entertained doubts about God's justice when facing a personal tragedy? What would it take for you to surrender to His wisdom and trust that every pain carries a hidden mercy?

Nothing is Obligatory Upon Allah
The commentator defines the principle and defends it against scriptural objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why nothing is obligatory upon Allah and how apparent scriptural evidence is interpreted consistently with divine freedom.

Core Idea
Allah acts by perfect choice; obligation would contradict this.

This lesson addresses a critical point in Islamic theology: nothing is wajib (obligatory) upon Allah. The commentator explains the rational basis for this—Allah acts by free choice—and shows how verses that seem to impose obligations, like the promise of provision, are understood as promises of favor, not legal duties. This safeguards divine transcendence while honoring scriptural language.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key claim
Read the passage's opening line: nothing is wajib upon Allah. Notice the explicit denial of both acts and abstentions being obligatory. This sets the boundary.
2
Grasp the proof
Learn why: Allah is a free agent (فاعل بالاختيار). Obligation would negate freedom. Understand the definition of free choice.
3
Address the objection
Examine the verse about provision. See how the commentator interprets it as a promise of favor, not a legal obligation. Note that hadith are treated similarly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مَا عَلَيْهِ وَاجِبُ) أي : ليس عليه تعالى واجبٌ مِنْ فعل أو ترك؛ لأنَّه تعالى فاعل بالاختيار، ولو وجب عليه فعل أو ترك لما كان مختاراً؛ لأنَّ المختار هو الذي إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك، وأما الآيات الدالة على الوجوب عليه تعالى نحو : ﴿وَمَا دَابَّةٍ فِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَّا عَلَى اللَّهِ رِزْقُهَا﴾ [هود: (٦)] فمحمولةٌ على أن المراد بها الوعد تفضلاً، وكذلك الأحاديث الدالة على ذلك، وتقدَّم الكلام في نظيره من الإيطاء (٢)، فلا تغفل.
His statement: 'Nothing is obligatory upon Him' means: Nothing is obligatory upon the Exalted, whether act or abstention; because the Exalted acts by choice. If an act or abstention were obligatory upon Him, He would not be free, because the free agent is the one who, if He wills, does, and if He wills, refrains. As for the verses that indicate obligation upon the Exalted, such as: {And there is no creature on earth but that upon Allah is its provision} [Hud: 6], these are interpreted to mean that the intent is a promise as a favor. Likewise, the hadiths that indicate that. The discussion of its similar from the repetition has preceded, so do not be heedless.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the line 'ما عليه واجب'. The commentator begins by clarifying that nothing—whether an action or omission—is obligatory upon Allah. The reason: Allah acts by free choice. If an action or omission were obligatory, He would not be free; a free agent can do or refrain as He wills. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: verses and hadith that seem to impose obligations upon Allah, such as the verse about provision. He explains these as promises of favor (وعد تفضلاً), not legal obligations. He concludes by reminding the reader that similar points have already been discussed, so one should not be negligent.

Key Terms
واجب
فاعل بالاختيار
وعد تفضلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah is obliged to reward good and punish evil, or that verses impose duties on Allah.
Correction: Nothing is wajib upon Allah. Allah acts by choice; what appears obligatory is actually a promise of favor.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why can nothing be wajib upon Allah?
A) Because Allah is too great to be bound by obligations
B) Because Allah acts by choice, and obligation would negate free choice
C) Because the Quran explicitly states there is nothing wajib upon Him
D) Because all obligations are only for humans

Key Takeaway
Nothing is wajib upon Allah; this is based on His absolute free choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is not obligated to give you anything—not your next breath, not your provision, not your faith—yet He does, what does that do to the way you receive each blessing? Do you take it as a right or as a free gift? What changes in your heart when you realize He could withhold it at any moment?
?
The lesson says verses like 'upon Allah is its provision' are a promise of favor, not a duty. Examine your inner attitude: Do you ever feel entitled to Allah's mercy or provision? Write down a specific instance where you acted as if you deserved something from Allah. Now, in light of this lesson, repent from that entitlement and thank Him for His sheer generosity.
?
Since nothing is obligatory upon Allah, the very fact that He guides you, forgives you, and provides for you is entirely His free choice. What does that mean for your reliance on Him? Is your trust in Him stronger because you know He chooses to be good to you out of love, not compulsion? Or does it unsettle you? Pray for a heart that rests in His free grace.

The Refutation of Rational Good and Evil Using Children's Pain
The commentator presents the key refutation of Mu'tazilite rational ethics using observable child pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observed fact of children suffering pain refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do what is rationally good.

Core Idea
Children's pain is a visible refutation of rationalist ethics.

The Mu'tazilites argued that good and evil are known by reason and that God must do what is good. But they face a problem: we observe children suffering pain, which seems unjust. The commentator uses this observation to show the failure of their approach. In this lesson, we examine the core argument and its linguistic defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic line and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic line: أَلَمْ يَرَوْا إِيلَامَهُ الأَطْفَالَا وَشِبْهَهَا؟ فَحَاذِرِ المِحَالَا and the literal translation. Notice the rhetorical question.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite doctrine
The Mu'tazilites believed that reason alone can determine good and evil, and that God must act according to what is good. This leads to a problem: why do innocent children suffer pain?
3
Analyze the commentator's response
The commentator explains that the seeing is visual, not just intellectual, to emphasize the reality of the observation. He also addresses an objection that pain is not visible by clarifying that the effect is seen.
This defends the empirical grounding of the argument.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument is a strong refutation: it uses a concrete, undeniable fact to challenge a rationalist system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أَلَمْ يَرَوْا إِيلَامَهُ الأَطْفَالَا وَشِبْهَهَا؟ فَحَاذِرِ المِحَالَا) هذا تنبيه على فساد مذهبهم، والرؤية بصرية، ويحتمل أن تكون علميَّةٌ، والأول أبلغ لمزيد التشنيع عليهم، وهم حقيقون بذلك، خصوصاً في هذا المقام فإن فيه غاية إساءة الأدب. وقوله : (إيلامه) مفعولُ (يروا)، وعلى جعلها علميَّةً يكون المفعول الثاني محذوفاً تقديره حاصلاً مثلاً، وعلى جعلها بصريةً لا تحتاج إلى مفعول ثانٍ. واعترض بأن الإيلام عبارةٌ عن تعلُّق القدرة بالألم، وهو لا يُرى، وأجيب بأنَّه على حذف مضاف والتقدير أثر إيلامه، وذلك الأثر هو الألم. وقوله : (الأطفالا) مفعول (الإيلام) ؛ لأنَّه مصدر مضاف لفاعله وهو الضمير العائد على الله، فالأصل إيلام الله الأطفال، وحكمة إيلام الأطفال حصول الثواب عليه لأبويهم؛ لأنَّ ذلك من المصائب التي يُثاب الشخص عليها، ولهذا قال إمام الحرمين: شدائد الدنيا مما يلزم العبد الشكر عليها؛ لأنَّها نِعَم حقيقة. وقوله : (وَشِبْهَهَا) أي: كالدواب والعجزة، فإنَّهم لا نفع لهم في إنزال الأسقام. وقوله : (فَحَاذِرِ المِحَالا) بكسر الميم بمعنى العقاب، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ شَدِيدُ الْمِحَالِ﴾ [الرعد: ١٣]. ويصح قراءته بفتح الميم بمعنى الشك. وبالضم بمعنى الممتنع. فالمعنى على الأول : فاحذر عقاب الله النازل بهم على إضلالهم. وعلى الثاني : فاحذر الشك في ذلك. وعلى الثالث فاحذر الممتنع وهو وجوب شيء عليه تعالى.
His statement: 'Do they not see His inflicting pain upon children and the like? So beware of (the) punishment?' This is a warning about the corruption of their school (the Mu'tazila). The seeing is visual, and it is possible that it is knowledge-based. The first is more eloquent for greater rebuke against them, and they are deserving of that, especially in this context, for it contains extreme disrespect. And His statement: 'His inflicting pain' is the object of 'they see'. If it is taken as knowledge-based, then the second object is omitted, its implied apposition being 'occurring' for example. If taken as visual, no second object is needed. An objection was raised: 'Inflicting pain' is a relation of power to pain, which is not seen. It was answered: There is an ellipsis of a genitive construct, and the implied meaning is 'the effect of His inflicting pain', and that effect is the pain. And His statement: 'the children' is the object of 'inflicting pain' because it is a verbal noun attached to its agent, the pronoun referring to Allah, so the original is 'Allah's inflicting pain upon children'. The wisdom of inflicting pain upon children is the attainment of reward for their parents because that is among the calamities for which a person is rewarded. For this reason, Imam al-Haramayn said: 'The hardships of the world are such that the servant is obliged to give thanks for them, because they are true blessings.' His statement: 'and the like' i.e., such as beasts and the disabled, for they derive no benefit from the descent of illnesses. His statement: 'so beware of (al-mihaala)' with kasra on the mim meaning punishment. Allah said: {And He is severe in punishment} [Ra'd: 13]. It is also allowed to be read with fatha on the mim meaning doubt. And with damma meaning the impossible. So the meaning according to the first: So beware of Allah's punishment descending upon them for their misguidance. According to the second: So beware of doubt in that. According to the third: So beware of the impossible, which is that something is obligatory upon Him, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the poetic line 'Do they not see His inflicting pain upon children and the like? So beware of (the) punishment.' The commentator first states it is a warning against the corruption of the Mu'tazilite school. He discusses whether 'seeing' is visual or knowledge-based, preferring visual for stronger rebuke. He then analyzes the syntax: 'إيلامه' is the object; if knowledge-based, there is an implied second object. An objection that inflicting pain is not visible is answered: the intended meaning is the effect (the pain). The child's pain is a real observation. Next, he explains the wisdom: the child's pain results in reward for the parents, as calamities are blessings, citing Imam al-Haramayn. 'وشبهها' includes beasts and the disabled. Finally, he explains 'المحال' with three readings: punishment, doubt, or the impossible. The meaning accordingly: beware of divine punishment on misguiders, or beware of doubt, or beware of the impossible notion that something is obligatory upon Allah.

Key Terms
إيلام
الأطفال
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite belief that God must always act according to what is rationally good, and that children's pain is unjust.
Correction: Children's pain is a reality; if reason alone determined good and evil, such pain would be evil and unjust. But it occurs, indicating that God's actions are not bound by human rational judgments.

Checkpoint
What is the primary purpose of the poetic line 'Do they not see His inflicting pain upon children...' according to the commentator?
A) To describe a historical event
B) To warn against the corruption of the Mu'tazilite school
C) To explain the meaning of 'al-mihal'
D) To discuss the reward for parents

Key Takeaway
The observable reality of children suffering pain provides a concrete refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that reason alone can determine good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness the suffering of an innocent child, does your heart immediately reach for a rational explanation? What does that impulse reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom versus your own reasoning?
?
The Mu'tazilites were rebuked for ignoring an obvious sign. Is there a clear sign from Allah in your life that you have been rationalizing away because it doesn't fit your mental framework?
?
If reason alone cannot determine good and evil, what does that mean for the moments you have judged others or justified your own actions based on your own sense of fairness? Are you willing to submit your moral judgments to revelation?

The Wisdom Behind Children's Pain and the Meanings of 'Al-Mihal'
The commentator explains the wisdom of children's pain and the three exegetical readings of 'al-mihal'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the wisdom behind children's pain (reward for parents) and the multiple valid interpretations of the word 'al-mihal' in the poetic line.

Core Idea
Children's pain is a blessing for their parents, and 'al-mihal' carries three meanings.

After establishing that children's pain is a real observation, the commentator explains the divine wisdom behind it: it is a calamity that brings reward to the parents. He also clarifies the word 'al-mihal' which can be read in three ways, each teaching a different lesson. In this lesson, we explore these deeper points.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'وقوله : (الأطفالا)' to the end. Note the explanation of wisdom and the three meanings of 'al-mihal'.
2
Understand the wisdom of children's pain
The commentator states that the pain results in reward for the parents, citing Imam al-Haramayn's view that hardships are true blessings.
This shows that God's actions have wise purposes, even if not immediately apparent.
3
Examine the scope of 'the like'
The commentator includes beasts and the disabled, showing that the argument extends to all innocent creatures.
4
Learn the three readings of 'al-mihal'
The word can be read with kasra (punishment), fatha (doubt), or damma (impossible). Each reading gives a different warning.
This linguistic richness adds depth to the argument.
