Allah Creates Both Good and Evil: The Sunni Position
Commentator explains the Sunni position and grammatical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine that Allah creates both good and evil, and the grammatical structure of the opening line.

Core Idea
Allah is the sole creator of all things, including good and evil.

This lesson begins by analyzing the commentator's grammatical breakdown of the author's line. The apparent meaning affirms that Allah creates both good and evil, which is the Sunni position in contrast to the Mu'tazila who attribute creative power to the servant. The commentator then offers an alternative interpretation to avoid repetition with an earlier statement.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the grammatical analysis of 'جائز' and 'خلق'.
2
Understand the Sunni Stance
The passage states that Ahl al-Sunna holds that it is permissible for Allah to create good and evil. Understand that this includes all actions, including human actions, as created by Allah.
3
Grasp the Commentator's Shift
The commentator shifts the meaning from 'creation' to 'will' to avoid redundancy with an earlier line. Note the use of ellipsis (مجاز بالحذف) to reinterpret the line.
This shows the carefulness in interpreting the author's words to maintain consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَجَائِزٌ عَلَيْهِ خَلْقُ ... إلخ) (جائز) خبرٌ مقدم، و(خلق) مبتدأ مؤخَّر، والمتبادر من كلام المصنفِ التكلُّمُ في مسألة الخلق، فذكر أن مذهب أهل السنة أن الله يجوز عليه خلق الخير والشر، وخالفت المعتزلة فيهما ، فقالوا : إنَّ العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه الاختيارية خيراً كانت أو شرا، وقد صرفه الشارح عن ظاهره فجعله في الإرادة تبعاً للمصنف في شرحه » ؛ لأنَّ إبقاء العبارة على ظاهرها يجعلها مكررة مع قوله سابقاً: فخالق لعبده وما عمل إلا أن يجعل هذا تفصيلاً لما تقدم، وعلى كلام الشارح يكون في العبارة مجاز بالحذف والتقدير إرادة خلق .. إلى آخره. ووافقت المعتزلة على أنَّ اللهَ يريد الخير، وخالفت في أنَّه يريدُ الشرَّ؛ فقالوا : يمتنع عليه تعالى إرادة الشرور والقبائح، وينوا ذلك على أصلهم الفاسد، ومذهبهم الكاسد من التحسين والتقبيح العقليين فيقولون: الله يريد الحسن لذاته، ولا يريد الشر لذاته . وعندنا : الحسن ما حسنه الشرع والقبيحُ ما قبحه الشرع، واستدلت المعتزلة على مذهبهم بأن إرادة الشر شر، وإرادة القبيح قبيحة، والله تعالى منزه عن الشرور والقبائح، ورُدَّ بأنه لا يقبح من الله شيءٌ، غاية الأمر أنه يخفى علينا وجه حُسنه . واستدلت المعتزلة أيضاً على مذهبهم بأنَّ العقاب على ما أراده ظلم، والله تعالى
His statement: 'And it is permissible for Him to create evil and good, like Islam and the ignorance of disbelief.' (Ja'iz) is a predicate fronted, and (creation) is a subject delayed. The apparent from the author's speech is speaking about the issue of creation. So he mentioned that the school of Ahl al-Sunna is that it is permissible for Allah to create good and evil. The Mu'tazila opposed them in both, saying: The servant creates his own voluntary actions, whether good or evil. The commentator diverted it from its apparent meaning and made it about will, following the author in his commentary. Because keeping the expression on its apparent meaning would make it a repetition of his earlier statement: 'So the Creator of His servant and what he does' unless this is taken as a detail of what preceded. According to the commentator's statement, there is a metaphor by ellipsis, the implied meaning being 'the will to create' etc. The Mu'tazila agreed that Allah wills good, but disagreed that He wills evil. They said: It is impossible for Him to will evils and abominations, basing that on their corrupt principle and unsound school of rational good and evil. They say: Allah wills good for its own sake, and does not will evil for its own sake. With us: Good is what the Law considers good, and evil is what the Law considers evil. The Mu'tazila argued for their school that willing evil is evil, and willing what is abominable is abominable, and Allah is exalted above evils and abominations. It was refuted by saying: Nothing is abominable from Allah; at most, its aspect of goodness is hidden from us. The Mu'tazila also argued for their school that punishment for what He wills is injustice, and Allah...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the line 'وَجَائِزٌ عَلَيْهِ خَلْقُ الشَّرِّ وَالخَيْرِ'. The commentator first provides a grammatical analysis: 'جائز' is an advanced predicate, 'خلق' is the delayed subject. The apparent meaning concerns creation: the Sunni position that Allah creates both good and evil. The Mu'tazila oppose, claiming the servant creates his own actions. The commentator notes a metaphorical interpretation that the line refers to will, to avoid repetition. He then explains the Mu'tazilite agreement that Allah wills good but denial that He wills evil, based on rational good/evil. Their argument: willing evil is evil, and Allah is beyond evil. The Sunni response: nothing is abominable from Allah; any apparent evil has a hidden good aspect. The passage ends with the Mu'tazila's second argument (punishment being injustice), which is incomplete.

Key Terms
خلق
جائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila belief that the servant creates his own actions.
Correction: The Sunni position is that Allah is the sole creator; human actions are created by Allah, though acquired by the servant.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the apparent meaning of the line 'وَجَائِزٌ عَلَيْهِ خَلْقُ الشَّرِّ وَالخَيْرِ'?
A) Allah wills both good and evil
B) Allah creates both good and evil
C) The servant creates his own actions
D) Allah only creates good

Key Takeaway
The Sunni doctrine is that Allah creates both good and evil, though the line may be interpreted metaphorically to refer to will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every evil that befalls you—including your own sins—is created by Allah with wisdom? How does this affect your attitude towards your mistakes?
?
If Allah creates both good and evil, how do you reconcile this with your own sense of justice? Does this make you question His goodness, or deepen your reliance on His infinite wisdom?
?
When you witness evil in the world, do you attribute it to Allah’s creation or to human failure? How does your answer shape your trust in Allah’s plan and your response to suffering?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Allah's Will and Evil
Mu'tazila arguments and Sunni refutation on Allah's will and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that Allah cannot will evil and the Sunni refutation based on divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Nothing is abominable from Allah; all His actions have a hidden good.

This lesson explores the theological dispute over whether Allah wills evil. The Mu'tazila argue that willing evil is evil, so Allah cannot do it. The Sunni response is that all of Allah's actions are good, even if we do not perceive their wisdom. The passage also introduces the concept of rational versus revealed good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila Position
Read the passage starting from 'ووافقت المعتزلة'. Notice that they agree Allah wills good but deny He wills evil.
2
Understand Their Rational Good/Evil Principle
The Mu'tazila base their view on rational good and evil: willing evil is inherently evil, so it cannot be attributed to Allah.
3
Examine Their Argument
They argue: 'إرادة الشر شر' (willing evil is evil). Therefore, Allah being perfect cannot will evil.
4
Learn the Sunni Refutation
The Sunni response: 'لا يقبح من الله شيء' (nothing is abominable from Allah). Any apparent evil has a hidden aspect of good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَجَائِزٌ عَلَيْهِ خَلْقُ ... إلخ) (جائز) خبرٌ مقدم، و(خلق) مبتدأ مؤخَّر، والمتبادر من كلام المصنفِ التكلُّمُ في مسألة الخلق، فذكر أن مذهب أهل السنة أن الله يجوز عليه خلق الخير والشر، وخالفت المعتزلة فيهما ، فقالوا : إنَّ العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه الاختيارية خيراً كانت أو شرا، وقد صرفه الشارح عن ظاهره فجعله في الإرادة تبعاً للمصنف في شرحه » ؛ لأنَّ إبقاء العبارة على ظاهرها يجعلها مكررة مع قوله سابقاً: فخالق لعبده وما عمل إلا أن يجعل هذا تفصيلاً لما تقدم، وعلى كلام الشارح يكون في العبارة مجاز بالحذف والتقدير إرادة خلق .. إلى آخره. ووافقت المعتزلة على أنَّ اللهَ يريد الخير، وخالفت في أنَّه يريدُ الشرَّ؛ فقالوا : يمتنع عليه تعالى إرادة الشرور والقبائح، وينوا ذلك على أصلهم الفاسد، ومذهبهم الكاسد من التحسين والتقبيح العقليين فيقولون: الله يريد الحسن لذاته، ولا يريد الشر لذاته . وعندنا : الحسن ما حسنه الشرع والقبيحُ ما قبحه الشرع، واستدلت المعتزلة على مذهبهم بأن إرادة الشر شر، وإرادة القبيح قبيحة، والله تعالى منزه عن الشرور والقبائح، ورُدَّ بأنه لا يقبح من الله شيءٌ، غاية الأمر أنه يخفى علينا وجه حُسنه . واستدلت المعتزلة أيضاً على مذهبهم بأنَّ العقاب على ما أراده ظلم، والله تعالى
His statement: 'And it is permissible for Him to create evil and good, like Islam and the ignorance of disbelief.' (Ja'iz) is a predicate fronted, and (creation) is a subject delayed. The apparent from the author's speech is speaking about the issue of creation. So he mentioned that the school of Ahl al-Sunna is that it is permissible for Allah to create good and evil. The Mu'tazila opposed them in both, saying: The servant creates his own voluntary actions, whether good or evil. The commentator diverted it from its apparent meaning and made it about will, following the author in his commentary. Because keeping the expression on its apparent meaning would make it a repetition of his earlier statement: 'So the Creator of His servant and what he does' unless this is taken as a detail of what preceded. According to the commentator's statement, there is a metaphor by ellipsis, the implied meaning being 'the will to create' etc. The Mu'tazila agreed that Allah wills good, but disagreed that He wills evil. They said: It is impossible for Him to will evils and abominations, basing that on their corrupt principle and unsound school of rational good and evil. They say: Allah wills good for its own sake, and does not will evil for its own sake. With us: Good is what the Law considers good, and evil is what the Law considers evil. The Mu'tazila argued for their school that willing evil is evil, and willing what is abominable is abominable, and Allah is exalted above evils and abominations. It was refuted by saying: Nothing is abominable from Allah; at most, its aspect of goodness is hidden from us. The Mu'tazila also argued for their school that punishment for what He wills is injustice, and Allah...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the line 'وَجَائِزٌ عَلَيْهِ خَلْقُ الشَّرِّ وَالخَيْرِ'. The commentator first provides a grammatical analysis: 'جائز' is an advanced predicate, 'خلق' is the delayed subject. The apparent meaning concerns creation: the Sunni position that Allah creates both good and evil. The Mu'tazila oppose, claiming the servant creates his own actions. The commentator notes a metaphorical interpretation that the line refers to will, to avoid repetition. He then explains the Mu'tazilite agreement that Allah wills good but denial that He wills evil, based on rational good/evil. Their argument: willing evil is evil, and Allah is beyond evil. The Sunni response: nothing is abominable from Allah; any apparent evil has a hidden good aspect. The passage ends with the Mu'tazila's second argument (punishment being injustice), which is incomplete.

Key Terms

إرادة الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if Allah wills and creates evil, then He is evil or unjust.
Correction: In Sunni theology, all of Allah's actions are good and just; any apparent evil has a wise purpose known to Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite argument against Allah willing evil?
A) Evil is not under His control
B) Willing evil is itself evil, and Allah cannot do evil
C) The Quran says He does not will evil
D) Evil is only from the servant

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that Allah cannot will evil because it is inherently evil, but Sunnis respond that all Allah's actions are good and have a hidden wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila were offended that Allah could will evil—they thought it beneath Him. Are you offended when Allah's plan crosses your sense of fairness? Do you trust His definition of good more than your own feelings? Repent of judging God by your standards.
?
If all Allah's actions have a hidden good, what suffering in your life have you never acknowledged as a potential mercy? Write one example and commit to thanking Allah for it, even if you do not understand.

Divine Transcendence
beginner
4 min
First Mu'tazilite argument: willing evil is evil
Presents and refutes the argument that God cannot will evil because it would be evil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that God cannot will evil because willing evil is evil, and the Sunni refutation that what is abominable is defined by divine law, not human standards.

Core Idea
Human moral categories do not apply to Allah's actions.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God willed evil, He would be doing something evil, which contradicts His perfection. This lesson presents their reasoning and the classical Sunni response: nothing from Allah is abominable; we simply may not grasp the wisdom behind His actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the Mu'tazilite argument is stated and then refuted in a single sentence each.
The refutation is concise but profound.
2
Identify the two parts of the passage
The passage has two distinct parts: the Mu'tazilite argument (premise + conclusion) and the Sunni refutation. Trace each.
The refutation begins with 'Wa rudda...'.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite argument
The argument: willing evil is itself evil; willing the abominable is abominable. Since Allah is free from all evil and abomination, He cannot will evil or the abominable.
This relies on the principle of 'tahsin wa taqbih' (good and evil are rational categories).
4
Understand the Sunni refutation
The refutation states that nothing from Allah is abominable. 'Abominable' (qabih) is defined by divine law, and Allah's actions are the source of law. We may not see the wisdom, but that does not make the act evil.
This defends divine transcendence without attributing evil to Allah.
5
Reflect on the core idea
Consider why this distinction matters: it preserves Allah's absolute sovereignty and rejects the idea that human reason can judge His actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدلت المعتزلة على مذهبهم بأن إرادة الشر شر، وإرادة القبيح قبيحة، والله تعالى منزه عن الشرور والقبائح، ورُدَّ بأنه لا يقبح من الله شيءٌ، غاية الأمر أنه يخفى علينا وجه حُسنه.
And the Mu'tazila argued for their madhhab that willing evil is evil, and willing the abominable is abominable, and Allah Almighty is free from evils and abominations. And it was refuted that nothing is abominable from Allah; the utmost is that the aspect of its goodness may be hidden from us.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and refutation of the first Mu'tazilite argument against God willing evil. The argument is: willing evil is itself evil, so God, being pure, cannot will evil. The refutation is that what is abominable (qabih) is defined by divine law, and God's actions are not subject to human standards; nothing from Allah is abominable—we may simply not understand the wisdom behind it.

Key Terms
إرادة الشر
القبيح
التحسين والتقبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation implies that God can do evil or that God sometimes does evil.
Correction: The refutation does not attribute evil to God; it denies that the human category of 'abominable' applies to God's acts. All of God's acts are good in themselves.

Checkpoint
How do the Mu'tazila argue that God cannot will evil?
A) Evil actions are impossible for God.
B) Willing evil is itself evil, and God is pure.
C) The Quran forbids it.
D) God cannot do anything.

Key Takeaway
Human moral categories do not constrain Allah; what He does is good by definition, even if its wisdom is hidden from us.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been troubled by something in your life that seemed evil or unjust, and you questioned Allah's wisdom? How does this lesson force you to reconsider your reaction? What does it require of you now?
?
When you face a calamity or see suffering, do you instinctively judge it as bad? How does the statement 'nothing from Allah is abominable' challenge your natural response? What practical change in attitude does it demand from you?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that willing evil is evil, so Allah cannot will it. But the refutation says that we may not understand the goodness. Is there an area in your life where you have been quick to label something as purely negative? How can you train yourself to trust in the hidden goodness that this lesson teaches? What specific step will you take this week to surrender your judgment to Allah's wisdom?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Argument on God's Punishment
Presents and refutes the argument that punishing for actions He wills would be unjust

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Mu'tazilite argument against God willing evil and the Sunni refutation grounded in divine sovereignty.

Core Idea
God's actions are just because He is the absolute owner of all.

The Mu'tazila argued that punishing someone for an action God wills is unjust, so God cannot will evil. The Sunni response affirms God's absolute ownership: He is sovereign over His creation and not answerable to anyone. This passage presents both the argument and the concise refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the compact style: two clear parts—argument and refutation.
The refutation is short but decisive.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite premise
Find the claim that punishment for what God wills is injustice (zulm). Understand why they think this supports their doctrine that God cannot will evil.
They equate willing an action with approving it.
3
Understand the refutation's logic
The refutation has two prongs: (1) God acts within His own kingdom, so it's not injustice; (2) God is not questioned about His actions. Grasp how these answer the argument.
Ownership removes the possibility of injustice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدلت المعتزلة أيضاً على مذهبهم بأنَّ العقاب على ما أراده ظلم، والله تعالى منزه عن الظلم، وردَّ بأنَّه تصرف في خالص ملكه، وهو لا يعد ظلماً، على أنه سبحانه وتعالى لا يُسأل عما يفعل.
And the Mu'tazila also argued for their madhhab that punishment for what He wills is injustice, and Allah Almighty is free from injustice. And it was refuted that it is disposal in His pure kingdom, and it is not considered injustice, especially since He, Exalted and High, is not questioned about what He does.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite argument: if God wills an action and then punishes the doer, that is unjust, so God cannot will evil. The refutation is that God is the absolute owner of His realm; whatever He does with His property is not injustice. Moreover, God is not accountable to anyone—'He is not questioned about what He does' (Quran 21:23).

Key Terms
الظلم
العقاب
الملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation denies that God punishes at all.
Correction: The refutation does not deny punishment; it argues that punishment is just because God is the absolute owner and sovereign, so His actions are inherently just.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why is punishing for what He wills not injustice?
A) Because the punishment is always deserved.
B) Because God acts within His own kingdom.
C) Because God does not actually punish.
D) Because punishment is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
God's punishment is just because He is the absolute owner of His creation and is not answerable to anyone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly thought God was unfair to you in some situation? In light of this lesson, how does His absolute ownership change your right to complain? Will you surrender that complaint to Him today?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you assume God must answer to your sense of justice? How does knowing He is not questioned (Quran 21:23) humble your heart? What would it look like to trust His justice even without explanation?
?
If God's actions are just simply because He is the Owner, how does that challenge the way you measure fairness in your own life? Are you willing to submit your standards of fairness to His?

God Creates Good and Evil: Sunni vs. Mu'tazila
States the Sunni view that God creates both good and evil and contrasts with Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on the creation of good and evil, how it contrasts with the Mu'tazila view, and the role of divine will and law in determining moral value.

Core Idea
God creates both good and evil; moral value is defined by divine law, not reason.

This passage from the poem tackles a major theological dispute: who creates human actions? The Sunni position affirms that God is the sole creator of all things, including good and evil deeds, while the Mu'tazila argue that humans create their own voluntary actions. The commentator also notes a reinterpretation of the line to avoid redundancy. This lesson unpacks these key differences and their foundations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the clear assertion that God creates both good and evil, and the subsequent discussion of the Mu'tazila opposition.
2
Identify the two main issues
Focus on the two points of disagreement: (1) whether God or man creates human actions, and (2) whether God wills evil. The Mu'tazila deny both, while Sunnis affirm the first and clarify the second.
The commentator reinterprets the line to avoid repetition, but the core Sunni position remains that God is the creator.
3
Understand the basis of moral values
The passage ends with the Sunni principle: good and evil are whatever the Law declares good or evil, not what reason deems good or evil. This refutes the Mu'tazila rationalist approach.
This connects divine sovereignty in creation and legislation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥٣ - وَجَائِزٌ عَلَيْهِ خَلْقُ الشَّرِّ وَالخَيْرِ كَالاسْلَامِ وَجَهْلِ الكُفْرِ قوله : (وَجَائِزٌ عَلَيْهِ خَلْقُ ... إلخ) (جائز) خبرٌ مقدم، و(خلق) مبتدأ مؤخَّر، والمتبادر من كلام المصنفِ التكلُّمُ في مسألة الخلق، فذكر أن مذهب أهل السنة أن الله يجوز عليه خلق الخير والشر، وخالفت المعتزلة فيهما ، فقالوا : إنَّ العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه الاختيارية خيراً كانت أو شرا، وقد صرفه الشارح عن ظاهره فجعله في الإرادة تبعاً للمصنف في شرحه » ؛ لأنَّ إبقاء العبارة على ظاهرها يجعلها مكررة مع قوله سابقاً: فخالق لعبده وما عمل إلا أن يجعل هذا تفصيلاً لما تقدم، وعلى كلام الشارح يكون في العبارة مجاز بالحذف والتقدير إرادة خلق .. إلى آخره. ووافقت المعتزلة على أنَّ اللهَ يريد الخير، وخالفت في أنَّه يريدُ الشرَّ؛ فقالوا : يمتنع عليه تعالى إرادة الشرور والقبائح، وينوا ذلك على أصلهم الفاسد، ومذهبهم الكاسد من التحسين والتقبيح العقليين فيقولون: الله يريد الحسن لذاته، ولا يريد الشر لذاته . وعندنا : الحسن ما حسنه الشرع والقبيحُ ما قبحه الشرع
53 - And it is permissible for Him to create evil and good, like Islam and ignorance of disbelief. His saying: (And it is permissible for Him to create ... etc.) (Permissible) is a predicate advanced, and (creation) is a subject deferred. The apparent meaning from the author's speech is speaking about the matter of creation, so he mentioned that the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna is that it is permissible for Allah to create good and evil, and the Mu'tazila opposed them in both, saying: the servant creates his own voluntary actions, whether good or evil. And the commentator turned it from its apparent meaning and made it about will, following the author in his commentary, because keeping the expression on its apparent meaning would make it repetitive with his earlier saying: 'So Creator of the servant and what he does' unless this is made a detail of what preceded. And according to the commentator's speech, there is in the expression a metaphor by omission, and the estimation is 'will to create' etc. And the Mu'tazila agreed that Allah wills good, but opposed in that He wills evil; they said: it is impossible for Him, Exalted, to will evils and abominations, and they based that on their corrupt principle and rotten madhhab of rational tashih and taqbih, saying: Allah wills the good in itself, and does not will evil in itself. And with us: good is what the Law declares good, and evil is what the Law declares evil.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition of the Sunni position on the creation of good and evil, contrasted with the Mu'tazila. The commentator explains the line 'And it is permissible for Him to create evil and good' from the poem. The apparent reading is about creation: God creates both good and evil. The Mu'tazila reject this, claiming man creates his own voluntary actions. The commentator notes that the author's own commentary reinterprets the line as referring to God's will, to avoid repetition. The Mu'tazila agree God wills good but deny He wills evil, based on their rationalist principle of tashih and taqbih (judging good and evil by reason). The Sunni position is that good and evil are defined by divine law, not reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni view that God creates evil makes Him morally responsible for evil.
Correction: The Sunni position does not attribute moral responsibility to God; rather, it affirms that all creation is from God, and moral values are determined by His law, not by human reason.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila position on the creation of human actions?
A) God creates all actions, good and evil.
B) Man creates his own voluntary actions.
C) God creates only good actions.
D) Angels create actions.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position maintains that God is the creator of all actions, good and evil, and that their moral quality is defined solely by divine law, not rational judgment.

?
The Mu'tazila claimed that reason alone can determine good and evil. Have you ever excused a sin as 'not that bad' based on your own judgment? What would it look like to truly accept that only Allah's law defines good and evil?

God's Justice Beyond Question: The Story of Iblis and al-Shafi'i
Anecdote of Iblis questioning al-Shafi'i about divine justice, with the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's actions in His creation are not subject to human standards of injustice, as illustrated by al-Shafi'i's reply to Iblis.

Core Idea
God's absolute ownership makes His actions always just.

The Ash'ari school affirms that God is free from injustice, but what does that mean when some are destined for Hell? This passage presents a dramatic encounter between Imam al-Shafi'i and Iblis that cuts to the heart of the issue. Through this exchange, we see how the principle 'He is not asked about what He does' resolves the apparent problem of divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the story unfold before interpreting.
Notice the structure: a doctrinal statement, then a narrative.
2
Identify the two conditions in al-Shafi'i's answer
Al-Shafi'i distinguishes between creation for the creature's will and creation for the Creator's will. Find this distinction in the text.
The key phrase is 'if He created you for what you want... but if He created you for what He wants'.
3
Reflect on the theological principle
Why does God's dominion (mulk) make His actions not comparable to human justice? Consider the statement 'He is not asked about what He does'.
This connects to the broader Ash'ari concept of divine attribute of justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منزه عن الظلم، وردَّ بأنَّه تصرف في خالص ملكه، وهو لا يعد ظلماً، على أنه سبحانه وتعالى لا يُسأل عما يفعل . ويُحكى أنَّ إبليس لعنه الله تمثل بين يدي الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه، وقال: يا إمام؛ ما تقولُ فيمن خلقني لما اختار واستعملني فيما اختار، وبعد ذلك إن شاء أدخلني الجنَّة، وإن شاء أدخلني النَّارِ، أَعَدَلَ في ذلك أم جَارَ؟ قال الإمام: فنظرتُ في مسألته فألهمني الله تعالى أن قلت: يا هذا؛ إن كان خلقك لما تريد أنت فقد ظلمك وإن خلقك لما يريد هو فلا يُسأل عما يفعلُ وهم يسألون، فاضمحلَّ إبليس وتلاشى، ثم قال والله يا شافعي؛ لقد أخرجت بمسألتي هذه سبعين ألف عابد من ديوان العبودية إلى ديوان الزندقة.
Free from injustice, and it was replied that it is disposal in His pure dominion, and it is not considered injustice, and that He, exalted, is not asked about what He does. And it is narrated that Iblis, may Allah curse him, appeared before al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, and said: O Imam, what do you say about the One who created me for what He chose and employed me in what He chose, and after that if He wills He admits me to Paradise, and if He wills He admits me to the Fire; is that just or unjust? The Imam said: I pondered his question and Allah inspired me to say: O this one, if He created you for what you want, then He has wronged you; but if He created you for what He wants, then He is not asked about what He does, while they are asked. Then Iblis dissipated and vanished, then he said: By Allah, O Shafi'i, you have removed seventy thousand worshippers from the register of worship to the register of heresy.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage consists of a theological statement followed by an illustrative anecdote. The author begins by stating that God is free from injustice (ẓulm), and that any action in His dominion is not considered unjust; moreover, He is not questioned about what He does. This establishes the principle that God's actions are not subject to human standards of justice because He is the absolute owner. The anecdote then dramatizes this principle: Iblis appears to Imam al-Shafi'i and poses a dilemma about a being who creates and employs someone according to His choice, then either rewards or punishes that person arbitrarily. Al-Shafi'i replies with a nuanced answer: if God had created the person for what the person wants, then God would be unjust, but God creates for His own purpose, and thus cannot be questioned. The answer causes Iblis to vanish, and he reveals that this answer had led many misguided worshippers astray—i.e., the correct theology saves one from heresy. The story reinforces the Ash'ari position that God's will is absolute and His actions are not subject to external criteria of justice.

Key Terms
ظلم
ملك
لا يُسأل عما يفعل
إبليس
الشافعي
ديوان العبودية
ديوان الزندقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the anecdote implies God's actions are arbitrary and therefore unjust.
Correction: The anecdote asserts that what is just for a creature does not apply to the Creator; God's actions are always just because He is the absolute owner.

Checkpoint
In the story, what was the key distinction al-Shafi'i made in his answer to Iblis?
A) God creates people for their own choices, so He is just.
B) If God created Iblis for what Iblis wants, that would be unjust; but He created him for what He wants, so He is not questioned.
C) God does not create evil, so He is blameless.
D) Iblis's question is invalid because Iblis does not exist.

Key Takeaway
God's justice is not constrained by human definitions because He is the absolute owner of creation; His actions are beyond question.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely faced moments when life felt unjust. When you think of those moments, do you secretly resent God's decree? How does al-Shafi'i's response to Iblis expose the root of that resentment: that you forget God's absolute ownership over your life? What will you do the next time that resentment stirs?
?
The devil himself admitted that al-Shafi'i's answer saved seventy thousand from heresy. Reflect: are there comfortable beliefs you hold that actually resemble Iblis's objection—demanding that God answer to your sense of fairness? Write down one area where you need to stop putting yourself in the position of judge over God.
?
If God is not asked about what He does, then your only place is humble submission. Is there a specific area of your life—your career, relationships, health—where you are still arguing with God's plan? What concrete step will you take tonight to surrender that area to Him?
